Aromatic amines are very toxic, being readily absorbed through the skin with fatal results. They are easily oxidized by air; the pure amine is usually colorless, but obtain color when oxidation products are present.

22.5 Salts of amines

Aliphatic amines are as basic as NH3; aromatic amines are less basic. In the relative order of basicity, they are less basic than OH− or OEt−, but more basic than water, ROH, ROR, RCOOR′, etc. Aqueous mineral acids convert amines to salts, and aq. OH− converts it to its free base. Amines and their salts behave in the same way as RCOOH and its salts: the salts are nonvolatile ionic solids, they usually decompose before reaching melting temperature, they are soluble in water but not in nonpolar solvents. The difference in water solubility of the free base and its salt, and the requirement of adding a mineral acid to get a solid to dissolve in water, are techniques used to identify amines.

22.6 Stereochemistry of nitrogen

Nitrogen hybridizes its orbitals in the valence shell to sp3 just as tetrahedral carbon does. Three of the bond orbitals overlap with the s orbitals of hydrogen or carbon bonding orbitals. The fourth has an unshared pair of electrons. In viewing the stereochemistry of nitrogen, it would appear that chirality is possible and that amines would show optical activity. But spectroscopic studies show that the pyramidal arrangement of groups with the nonbonding pair have an energy barrier that requires no bond-breaking comparable energy, and so pyramidal inversion happens easily, making it possible to isolate any enantiomers.

On the other hand, quaternary ammonium salts (still sp3- hybridized) have no nonbonding pair but a fourth group. They do exist as configurational isomers, show optical activity, and their enantiomers can be resolved.

22.7 Industrial source

The ways that amines are prepared industrially are not practical for the lab. The most important is aniline for which there are several cheap ways: 1. reduction of nitrobenzene with iron and dilute HCl or by catalytic hydrogenation: nitrobenzene + Fe + 30% HCl + heat → anilinium chloride + Na2CO3 → aniline. 2. treatment of chlorobenzene with ammonia at high pressures and temperatures with a catalyst: chlorobenzene + NH3 + Cu2O, 200°, 900 psi → aniline.

Methylamine, dimethylamine, and trimethylamine are made from ammonia and methanol in successive substitution of the H atom on nitrogen by heating (450°) in a catalyst (Al2O3). Alkyl halides are also used to make the higher alkylamines. And the fatty acids are treated with ammonia and then reduced to make primary aminoalkanes: RCOOH + NH3, heat → RCONH2 + heat→ RCN (nitrile) + H2/cat. → RCH2NH2 (amine)

22.8 Preparation

1. Reduction of nitro compounds (used chiefly for aromatic amines). ArNO2 or RNO2 + metal, H+ or H2, catalyst → ArNH2 or RNH2.

2. Reaction of halides with ammonia or amines. NH3 + RX → RNH2 + RX → R2NH + RX → R3N + RX → R4N+ (RX must be alkyl or aryl group with electron-withdrawing groups)

3. Reductive amination.

-C=O + NH3 + H2/Ni or NaBH3CN → -CH-NH2 1° amine

-C=O + RNH2 + H2/Ni or NaBH3CN → -CH-NHR 2° amine

-C=O + R2NH + H2/Ni or NaBH3CN → -CH-NR2 3° amine

4. Reduction of nitriles. RCN + 2 H2 / catalyst → RCH2NH2 1° amine

5. Hofmann degradation of amides. RCONH2 or ArCONH2 + OBr− → RNH2 or ArNH2 + CO32−

22.9 Reduction of -NO2 compounds

Reduction of nitro compounds, usually arylnitro compounds, can be done by either (a) catalytic hydrogenation using H2 or (b) chemical reduction with a metal and acid. For the catalytic hydrogenation, the nitro compound is dissolved in alcohol and shaken with finely divided nickel or platinum under H2 gas; the method is useless if the molecule has a C=C bond, which is also easily hydrogenated. Chemical reduction starts by adding HCl to a mixture of the nitro compound and a metal (granulated tin), which forms a salt with the oxidized tin. The amine can be separated from the unreacted nitro compound by taking advantage of the salt of the amines solubility in water.

This reduction of the nitro compounds is useful for preparing the amino for conversion to a diazo compound, and to build more complicated molecules.

22.10 Ammonolysis of halides

Organic halogens are converted to amines on standing at room temp (or with some heating under pressure) with aqueous or alcoholic solution of ammonia. The NH3 displaces the halide from the alkyl group in a nucleophilic substitution reaction in much the same way hydroxide, alkoxide, cyanide, acetylide, and water do. This amounts to ammonolysis. Because of its highly basic nature, the alkyl halide may also undergo elimination instead of substitution, so higher yields are obtained with primary halides (where substitution predominates) and is not done with tertiary halides.

ArX is not very reactive, but are converted to the amines if (a) the ring carries an -NO2 group or other strongly electron-withdrawing group ortho or para to the halogen, or (b) a high temperature or strongly basic reagent is used.

A problem with ammonolysis is that, once the primary amine is formed from the ammonia, the result salt is made into its free base (because of the basic conditions) which can itself react with another RX to form the secondary amine, which can itself react with RX until the nitrogen is exhaustively alkylated. This is less of a problem if a large excess of ammonia is used to reduce the probability that the primary amine will encounter another RX.

22.11 Reductive amination

Many RCHO and RCOR′ can be reduced in the presence of ammonia to form amines; this is reductive amination. The reduction is by a catalytic action or by the use of sodium cyanohydridoborate, NaBH3CN. In the reaction, an imine intermediate is formed, either RCH=NH or R2 C=NH, namely a C=N bond which is sensitive to reduction.

Example reactions: 1. heptaldehyde → n-heptylamine 2. benzaldehyde → benzylamine 3. 2-pentanone → 2-aminopentane 4. acetophenone → α-phenylethylamine.

Often reductive amination is used to convert produce an amine which might otherwise produce an elimination reaction due to the basicity of NH3: cyclohexanol treated with PBr3 to make cyclohexyl bromide cannot be expect to product cyclohexylamine by nucleophilic substitution when treated with ammonia (it makes cyclohexene). Instead the cyclohexanol is treated with K2Cr2O7 in sulfuric acid to produce the ketone, cyclohexanone, which can be reductively aminated (NH3 + H 2/Ni) to form cyclohexylamine.

In some reactions, the 1° amine produced by the reduction process can continue to react with carbonyl and form the 2° amine, but controlling the proportions of the reactants eliminates that possibility.

22.12 Hofmann degradation of amides

The mechanism is discussed in ch. 28. The effect of the Hofmann degradation is to produce an amine from an amide which is reduced in carbon number by one. RCONH2 + OBr− → RNH2 + CO 32−

22.13 Synthesis of 2° and 3° amines

Most of the synthetic reactions shown outline the synthesis of primary amines. The 2° and 3° amines are produced merely by adapting and controlling the reagents in ammonolysis of RX compounds and in reductive amination.

Chap. 23 Amines. Reactions

23.1 Reactions

1. Basicity. Salt formation. RnNHm ↔ RnNHm+1+ n+m=3, n << 3

2. Alkylation. RNH2 + RX → R2NH + RX → R3N + RX → R4N+X−

3. Conversion into amides.

Primary: RNH2 + R′COCl → R′CONHR N-substituted amide

RNH2 + ArSO2Cl → ArSO2NHR N-substituted sulfonamide

Secondary: R2NH + R′COCl → R′CONR2 N,N-disubstituted amide

R2NH + ArSO2Cl → ArSO2NR2 N,N-disubstituted sulfonamide

Tertiary R3N + R′COCl → no reaction

R3N + ArSO2Cl → no reaction under conditions of Hinsberg test

4. Ring substitution in aromatic amines. -NH2, -NHR, -NR2 are powerful activators and ortho,para directors in electrophilic aromatic substitution. -NHCOR are less powerful activators than -NH2.

5. Hofmann elimination from quaternary ammonium salts. -CH-CNR3+ + OH−, heat → C=C + R3N + H2O

6. Reactions with nitrous acid.

Primary aromatic: ArNH2 + HONO → Ar-N≡N+

Primary aliphatic: RNH2 + HONO → [R-N≡N+] + H2O → N2 + mixture of alcohols and alkenes

2° aromatic or aliphatic: ArNHR or R2NH + HONO → ArRN-N=O or R2N-N=O N-nitrosoamine

3° aromatic: C6H5NR2 + HONO → O=NC6H4NR2 p-nitroso compound

23.2 Basicity of amines. Basicity constant

Amines do not differ from ammonia in their ability to form salts with mineral acids, or become free bases with aqueous OH−; they are less basics than OH− but more basic than H2O. Amines react with water to produce the protonated amine and OH−, an equilibrium reaction expressed by a basicity constant:

Although the principal base in aqueous solution is the amine, the measurement of [OH−] is a convenient way to compare basicities. Aliphatic amines tend to have Kb values between 10−3 and 10−4, being somewhat stronger than NH3 itself (Kb = 1.8 × 10−5). Aromatic amines are very weak on the other hand (10−9 or less), and the basicity of substituted anilines is about 1/4000 that of aniline.

23.3 Structure and basicity

Amines are more basic than ROH, ROR, RCOOR′, etc., for the same reason that ammonia is more basic than water: N is less electronegative than O, and can better accommodate the positive charge of the ion. Aliphatic amines are more basic than ammonia because the alkyl groups attached to N stabilize the positive charge by releasing electrons, similar to the stabilization of a carbonium ion.

Why are aromatic amines weak bases? Neither aniline or the anilinium ion have any difference in their resonance structures, and while resonance participates in lowering the energy of both, there is no difference in the ΔG of ionization of the ion or the base. For this reason, the basicity of aniline might be expected to have the same basicity as ammonia. However, the basicity is much lower because the nonbonding pair is need in the free base to participate resonance with the aromatic ring, an energy stabilization much lower than would be possible in the anilinium ion. Because resonance lowers the energy in aniline more than it would in anilinium ion, aniline's affinity for a proton is reduced.

23.4 Effect of substituents on basicity of aromatic amines

Basicity of substituted aromatic amines largely depends on the divide between substituents activating the ring (-NH2, -OCH3, -CH 3) and those deactivating the ring (-NH3+, -NO 2, -SO32−, -COOH, -X). Clearly those releasing electrons to the ring disperse the positive charge better, making the amine a better base. Electron withdrawal tends to intensify the positive charge, and would decrease basicity. It is noted that the effect is opposite for that of aromatic acids, in which the ring is expected to accommodate a negative charge.

The ortho effect seen to increase acidity in RCOOH weaken the basicity of aromatic amines, even with electron-releasing groups on the ring.

23.5 Quaternary ammonium salts. Exhaustive methylation. Hofmann elimination

Amines have been shown to repeatedly attack alkyl halides in a nucleophilic substitution, forming a higher class of amine, which is a side reaction of the ammonolysis of halides. The final product is a quaternary ammonium salt. Primary, secondary, and tertiary amine salts react with OH− to liberate the free base, but 4° amines do not. Quaternary ammonium halides do react with silver oxide, however, to form the quaternary ammonium hydroxide and the silver halide, which precipitates:

R4N+X− + Ag2O → R4N+ OH− + AgX

This hydroxide is just as basic as NaOH or KOH, since is essentially a salt like them. When this hydroxide is heated (to 125° or more), it decomposes to from water, a 3° amine, and an alkene.

[(CH3)3NCH2CH2CH3]+ OH− (heat) → (CH3)3N + CH2=CHCH3 + H2O

This reaction is called the Hofmann elimination, and analogous to dehydrohalogenation of RX. The reaction is typically E2, with the OH− abstracting a proton at the same time the amine is leaving because it is a very good leaving group.

Bases other than OH− can also be used. E1 elimination is also possible, and competing with the elimination reactions are the SN2 and SN1-type reactions as well.

The orientation is also strongly Hofmann, a rule Hofmann formulated after observing the effects of this reaction:

[CH3CH2CH2CH(NMe3)CH3]+ + NaOEt/EtOH → 1-pentene (96%) + 2-pentene (4%)

The transition state has considerable carbanion character because the positively charged nitrogen favors its development by pulling electrons away. The primary carbon can best accommodate the partial negative charge, and so its proton is removed. The stereochemistry of the Hofmann elimination is anti , but less than was believed. Syn elimination is preferred in some cyclic compounds, and can be important for open-chain cmpds by the proper choice of base and solvent. 4° amines are more prone to syn elimination than RX or ROSO3H. Electronically, anti formation of the double bond is favored in eliminations, but conformational factors can force the final outcome.

Structural identification of the amine is often done in two stages: 1. exhaustive methylation of the amine with CH3I, in which the number of methyl groups taken up by the amine are assessed; 2. Formation of its hydroxide with Ag2O, then heating to amine to produce the alkene and 3° amine, often which can be analyzed to deduce the original structure.

insert reactions top of p 755

23.6 Conversion of amines into substituted amides

NH3 can react in a nucleophilic substitution with the acid chlorides of RCOOH or of ArSO2OH to produce amides and sulfonamides, resp. In the same way primary and secondary amines (but not 3° amines, because the loss of proton is required), will react with acid chlorides to form substituted amides. The amides are named as derivatives of the unsubstituted amides: CH 3CONHC2H5, N-ethylacetamide; CH3CH 2CH2CON(CH3)C2H 5, N-methyl-N-ethylbutyramide. Substituted amides of aromatic carboxylic acids are prepared by the Schotten-Bauman technique in which the ArCOCl is added to amine in the presence of a base, either aq. NaOH or pyridine: aniline + benzoyl chloride (in pyridine) → benzanilide; di-n-butylamine + benzenesulfonyl chloride (in NaOH) → N,N-di-n-butylbenzenesulfonamide.

Acetylation of amines is done using the less reactive acetic anhydride (not acetyl chloride). Like unsubstituted amides, the substituted amides undergo either base- or acid-catalyzed hydrolysis. Sulfonamides are hydrolyzed more slowly, the reason being that sulfonyl nucleophilic substitution is distinct from acyl nucleophilic substitution in that it must form a pentavalent intermediate, with a decet around sulfur. Sulfur may be able to accommodate a decet, but it prefers the octet.

The amides and sulfonamides may be contrasted further. With substituted amides and sulfonamides still possessing a hydrogen attached to nitrogen, this nitrogen is quite acidic, especially in the sulfonamide. The sulfonamide proton is less acidic than RCOOH, but as acidic as phenols (Ar-OH), and will react with aqueous hydroxide to form salts.

ArSO2NHR + OH− → H2O + [Ar−SO2−NR]−

The difference in acidity is understandable since the negative charge is distributed over three O atoms instead of the two in -COO−. Similarly sulfonamides are more acidic because the resulting negative charge is dispersed over two O and one N instead of only one O and one N atom.

23.7 Ring substitution of aromatic amines

The -NH2, -NHR, and -NR2 groups are powerful activators of EAS to the aromatic ring, and ortho,para-directing. Resonance stabilization shows the nonbonding pair becomes involved in the pi bonding, making nitrogen assume the positive charge, but permitting every atom (except hydrogen) to have the necessary octet in the valence shell.

The acetamido group, -NHCOCH3 (acetylated amine), is also activating and ortho,para-directing, but less so than a free amino group, mainly because the carbonyl oxygen pulls on the nonbonding pair of nitrogen somewhat. Moreover, being an amide, the basicity is virtually nonexistent in these aromatic amides, since the carbonyl destabilizes the positive charge acquired by nitrogen when protonation occurs.

Quaternary ammonium groups, -NR3+, on the ring have no nonbonding pair to contribute to resonance stabilization in EAS. Because of the positive charge, they destabilize any attempt at EAS reaction, and are meta-directors.

A problem with aromatic amines in EAS is that they are often too reactive: bromination of p-toluidine results in dibromination of both ortho positions, and would also result in bromination at the para position if it were not occupied.

HNO3 is involved in nitration of EAS. But it is also an oxidizing agent, oxidizing the highly reactive ring into a useless tar. In the acidic medium, the amine is protonated, making the substitution controlled by - NH3+ and not by -NH2; the positive charge is usually meta-directing. To control this reactivity of the amine to other future reactions, acetylation is often used to protect the function.

23.8 Sulfonation of aromatic amines. Dipolar ions

Aniline can be sulfonated by baking the anilinium hydrogen sulfate salt at 180-200°. The sulfur group is attached mostly to the para position. This sulfonation is reversible, and the p-isomer is considered the most stable isomer. Hence this reaction is not rate-controlled (being faster), but equilibrium-controlled. The p-aminobenzenesulfonic acid is called sulfanilic acid. Both aniline (m.p. -6°) and benzenesulfonic acid (m.p. 66°) have low melting points, but sulfanilic acid decomposes (at 280- 300°) before its m.p. is reached. Sulfonic acids are very soluble in water; indeed when introduced to a molecule, a sulfonic acid group makes it soluble. But sulfanilic acid is insoluble in organic solvents, are nearly insoluble in water. Amines dissolve in acids because they become salts, but sulfanilic acid is insoluble in aqueous acids (but soluble in aqueous bases).

The reason for these properties is that sulfanilic acid is a dipolar (zwitter) ion. When placed in water, the sulfonic acid donates a proton to the water, but the amine takes up a proton. The molecule has a net charge of zero, when it would be least soluble in water (but many salts are insoluble in water as well). Because it is a dipolar ion (with 2 charges) it should be insoluble in organic solvents. In basic solutions, the OH− is a stronger base than -NH2 and only a single charge is on the molecule, making it soluble. In acid, the amino group is protonated, but the sulfonic acid group is such a weak base it would take a highly concentrated acid to protonate it.

23.9 Sulfanilamide. The sulfa drugs

The amide of sulfanilic acid represents a class of medically important drugs called sulfa drugs. Sulfianilamides are prepared from sulfonamides first by acetylating the anilino group and then by preparing the sulfonyl acid chloride where it can react with ammonia or a substituted amine. Acetylating the anilino group prior to preparing the acid chloride prevents the aromatic amine from reacting with the acid chloride.

Starting from aniline, the molecule is acetylated with acetic anhydride. This is treated with sulfonyl chloride (ClSO3H) to make p- acetamidobenzenesulfonyl chloride. This product can then be treated with either ammonia or substituted amines to make the acetylated sulfaniliamide. Afterward, acid-catalyzed hydrolysis de-acetylates the amino group to make the sulfanilamide. It can be seen that the sulfonamide is itself not hydrolyzed under these conditions, indicating the amides of RCOOH are more easily hydrolyzed. Sulfaniliamides are found to mimic the p-aminobenzoic acid used by bacteria, but are not functional and thereby prevents bacterial growth.

Substituted sulfaniliamides perform much better than sulfaniliamide itself as antimicrobials. The acidity of the proton on nitrogen is quite important. Of the hundreds of sulfa drugs made and tested, the best ones have the right combination of antibacterial activity and low human toxicity are those in which the substituted have a heterocyclic ring:

insert structures on top p. 763

23.10 Reactions of amines with nitrous acid

Each class of amine reacts with nitrous acid (HONO), produced by mixing mineral acids and sodium nitrite (NaNO2), to form a different kind of product.

Primary aromatic amines react to yield diazonium salts, one of the most important reactions in organic chemistry.

[cold conditions]: ArNH2 + NaNO2 + 2 HX → ArN2+X− + NaX + 2 H2O

1° aliphatic amines react to produce these salts, but are unstable and break down to yield a complicated mixture of products. The reaction has little value in synthesis, but the fact that N2 is evolved quantitatively has analytical importance (for amino acids and proteins).

ArNH2 + NaNO2 + HX → RN2+X− H2O → N2 + mixture of alcohols and alkenes

2° amines, whether aliphatic or aromatic, react with HONO to produce N-nitrosoamines.

N-methylaniline + NaNO2 + HCl → N-nitroso-N-methylaniline + NaCl + H2O

3° aromatic amines will undergo ring substitution to yield products with a nitroso group (-N=O) joined to carbon:

insert reaction on top of p 764

The reaction is an EAS reaction with the electrophile nitrosonium ion (+NO) or perhaps another species (H2O+− NO or NOCl). This ion is very weakly electrophilic compared to the electrophiles involved in nitration, sulfonation, halogenation, or the Friedel-Crafts reaction. This nitrosation of the ring occurs only in rings with powerful activating groups, like the dialkylamino (-NR2) or hydroxy (-OH) group.

insert diagram middle p 764

In all reactions with NaNO2 in acid, the +NO becomes an electrophile attacking the available lone pair on the nitrogen of 1° and 2° amines, and a H+ is lost from the amine nitrogen as a result. 3° aromatic amines are attacked on the ring.

3° aliphatic amines (and to an extent, 3° aromatic amines) can react with HONO to form the N-nitroso derivative of a secondary amine; the group lost from the nitrogen appears as an aldehyde or ketone. The mechanism is thought to start with +NO reaction on nitrogen.

23.11 Diazonium salts. Preparation and reactions

1° aromatic amines dissolved or suspended in cold aqueous mineral acid and sodium nitrite for diazonium salts.

ArNH2 + NaNO2 + 2 HX cold → Ar-N≡N:+X− + NaX + 2 H2O

The salt should be immediately used since it decomposes even at ice-bath temperatures. The reactions which diazonium salts can undergo include replacement and coupling.

1. Replacement by nitrogen

Ar-N2+ + :Z → ArZ + N2

(a) Replacement by −Cl, −Br, and −CN. Sandemeyer reaction.

Ar-N2+ CuCl → ArCl + N2

Ar-N2+ CuBr → ArBr + N2

Ar-N2+ CuCN → ArCN + N2

(b) Replacement by −I

Ar-N2+ + I− → ArI + N2

(c) Replacement by −F

Ar-N2+ + BF4− heat → ArF + N2 + BF3

(d) Replacement by −OH

Ar-N2+ + H2O H+ → ArOH + N2

(e) Replacement by −H

Ar-N2+ + H3PO2 H2O → ArH + H3PO3 + N2

2. Coupling

Ar-N2+ X− + C6H5G → Ar−N=N−C6H4G

G must be a strongly electron-releasing group: OH, NR2, NHR, NH2

Many groups can replace the very highly reactive diazonium substituent in addition to those listed. The aryl nitrile in the Sandemeyer reaction is usually hydrolyzed to form the benzoic acid (Ar-CN → Ar-COOH). Other groups replacing the diazonium are, for example, -Ar, -NO2, -OR, -SH, -SR, -NCS, -NCO, -PO3H2, -AsO3H2, -SbO3H2.

23.12 Diazonium salts. Replacement by halogen. Sandemeyer reaction

Replacement of the diazonium salt by -Cl or -Br is done with the cuprous chloride or bromide at room temperature or slightly warmed. N2 is steadily evolved over several hours to make the aryl halide. This is called the Sandemeyer reaction. The Gatterman reaction is a modification of this synthesis, involving Cu powder and HX.

Formation of the aryl iodide does not require CuI or copper. Usually just an iodide salt (KI) is sufficient.

Formation of the aryl fluoride is done with fluoboric acid, HBF4, which is added to the diazonium salt to form the diazonium fluoborate, ArN2+BF4−, a fairly stable salt which is filtered, washed and dried. The dry salt is then heated and decomposes to form the aryl flouride, boron trifluoride, and nitrogen. An analogous procedure is used with the diazonium hexafluorophosphate, ArN2+PF6−.

ArF and ArI compounds, which cannot be synthesized by direct halogenation, and prepared in this way. ArCl and ArBr can be prepared by direct halogenation of the ring, but if a mixture of o- and p-isomers is difficult to separate because of similar b.p. values.

23.13 Diazonium salts. Replacement by −CN. Synthesis of RCOOH

CuCN addition to the diazonium salt produces the aryl nitrile. Prior neutralization the medium of ArN2+ with Na2 CO3 is necessary to prevent loss of CN− by HCN formation. The nitrile may then be acid-hydrolyzed to form the carboxylic acid.

This synthesis leading to an Ar-COOH may be better than the carbonation of the Grignard or the oxidation of alkyl side chains. The Grignard would have been prepared from a -Br side chain, which itself is made from the diazonium anyway; other groups on the molecule may react during the formation of the Grignard as well. A nitro group leading to the diazonium is introduced more readily than an alkyl side chain; moreover, conversion of the side chain by oxidation if there are other groups sensitive to oxidation.

23.14 Diazonium salts. Replacement by −OH. Synthesis of phenols

Diazonium salts react with water to produce phenols, which occurs slowly in ice-cold solutions, which is why diazonium salts are used immediately after preparation. At higher temperatures, it is the chief reaction of diazonium salts. Phenols can couple with ArN2+ to form diazo compounds; but the more acidic the solution is, the slower the coupling. Coupling to the phenol can be minimized by adding slowly the diazonium to a large volume of boiling sulfuric acid.

23.15 Diazonium salts. Replacement by −H

A number of reducing agents, the most useful by hypophosphorus acid, H3PO2, can be used to reduce diazonium to Ar- H. This acid can be used as both the diazotizing and reducing agent: since the diazotization requires NaNO2 and acid, and the reduction is provided by this particular acid, the two processes can occur one after the other. The effect of this action is to remove either -NH2 or -NO 2 from the ring.

23.16 Syntheses using diazonium salts

It was previously mentioned that o- and p-isomers, especially of halogens, are difficult to separate by distillation. So how might they be prepared separately? If o-bromotoluene and p-bromotoluene are to be prepared from toluene, the syntheses might go like this:

C6H5CH3 HNO3
H2SO4
→ 		o-NO2C6H4CH3
+
p-NO2C6H4CH3 		Fe, H+
→ 		NaNO2
HBr
→ 		CuBr
→ 		o-BrC6 H4 CH3
+
p-BrC6 H4CH3

The ortho and para nitro isomers (b.p., 222° and 238°, resp.) prepared in the first step can be better isolated by distillation than the ortho and para bromo isomers (b.p. 182° and 185°, resp.)

Synthesis of the meta isomer is more complicated:

C6H5 CH3 HNO3
H2SO4
→ 		p-NO2C6H4CH3 Fe, H+
→ 		(AcO)2O
→ 		Br2
→ 		4-acetamido-3-bromotoluene
 
4-acetamido-3-bromotoluene H2O / H+
→ 		NaNO2 / HCl
→ 		H3PO2
→ 		m-bromotoluene

The purpose of the synthesis is to introduce a very strong ortho,para- director other than -CH3, putting the halogen in the right position. The group -NHCOCH3 is quite suitable, put their by nitration, reduction, then acetylation; removed by hydrolysis, diazotization, then reduction.

To prepare m-bromophenol, start with nitrobenzene: 1. Br2, Fe; 2. Sn, HCl; 3. NaNO2, HCl; 4. H2O, H+

To prepare 1,2,3-tribromobenzene from p-nitroaniline: 1. Br2, CH 3COOH (produces dibromo deriv ortho to aniline); 2. NaNO2, H2SO4; 3. CuBr; 4. Sn, HCl; 5. NaNO2, HCl; 6. H3PO2.

23.17 Coupling of diazonium salts. Synthesis of azo compounds

Azo compounds have the general formula Ar-N=N-Ar′, and are the result of coupling to stabilize the diazonium. The diazonium, Ar- N2 +, is an electrophile, just as is any cationic molecule, and capable of EAS on another ring. However, Ar-N2+ is a very weak electrophile, and for that reason the ring to which it is coupled must be very active to EAS. Usually an activating group should be attached: -OH, - NR 2, -NHR, or -NH2. The substitution occurs at the para position usually, and coupling with phenols is carried out under mildly alkaline conditions, and with amines mildly acidic solutions. Kinetic studies reveal that the diazonium is the electrophile. Its electrophilicity is about as strong as the nitrosonium ion, +NO, which is involved in nitrosation. Both of these electrophiles requires significant ring activation to be involved in EAS.

Coupling is not so easy as mixing the diazonium and phenol or arylamine. With water present, the diazonium will react with water to produce a phenol; if conditions for coupling are slow, phenol formation may predominate. The phenol which forms may also react with diazonium to form the wrongly coupled product. The right degree of alkalinity or acidity for coupling is necessary; addition of the correct amt. of NaOH, sodium acetate, or sodium carbonate creates an equilibrium which allows formation a product ready to react.

Ar-N≡N+OH−
couples 		NaOH

→
←
H+ 		Ar-N=N−OH
does not couple 		NaOH

→
←
H+ 		Ar-N=N-O−Na+
does not couple

It can be seen that too much alkalinity converts the diazonium into a compound unable to couple. A low concentration of hydroxide, or high acidity would promote the reaction.

A problem with high acidity is that the amine or phenol to be coupled becomes protonated. With the amine, a positive charge develops, deactivating the ring too much to allow the diazonium to react. With phenols, the deprotonated phenolate ion is a much better target for electrophiles because of its negative charge than the protonated phenol; coupling is slower for the phenol than for the phenoxide ion. Experience shows that amines couple best in mildly acidic solutions, and phenols fastest in mildly alkaline solutions.

Azo compounds are important dyes. Some structures are given.

insert structures on p 775.

23.18 Analysis of amines. Hinsberg test

Any water-insoluble compound dissolving in cold, dilute HCl, or a water- soluble material in aqueous solutions turning litmus blue is almost certainly an amine. Elemental analysis shows a nitrogen presence.

The Hinsberg test allows determination of whether the amine is 1°, 2°, or 3°: the amine is shaken with benzenesulfonyl chloride in the presence of aqueous potassium hydroxide. 1° and 2° amines form substituted sulfonamides; 3° amines do not. The 1° amine will form a monosubstituted sulfonamide which has an acidic proton; its reaction with KOH should convert the sulfonamide to a partly soluble salt if the amine is 8 carbons or fewer (a clear solution). Acidification produces the insoluble amide. The 2° amine forms a disubstituted sulfonamide which has no acidic hydrogen and should remain insoluble in the KOH solution. The 3° amine does react with benzenesulfonyl chloride, apparently forming a quaternary sulfonamide salt, which is unstable in the presence of OH−, which displaces it to form the sulfonic acid and regenerate the insoluble R 3N. Upon acidification of the 3° amine, the insoluble compound dissolves as the aqueous soluble salt is formed.

Nitrous acid is useful for identifying amine class as well. In the presence of β-naphthol, highly colored azo compounds are formed which is indicative of 1° aromatic amine conversion to diazonium.

1° and 2° amines can be converted to amine derivatives, namely amides by reaction with the acid chlorides of acetic acid, benzoic acid, or benzenesulfonic acid: the physical properties of these derivatives assist in this determination. Quaternary ammonium salts will form for tertiary amines treated with benzoyl chloride or methyl iodide, and can be assessed.

23.19 Analysis of substituted amides

Typically, amides of RCOOH are insoluble in dilute acid or base. The presence of nitrogen should be determined. Hydrolysis should yield the acid and amine, and determination of their structures will indicate identification the final structure.

23.20 Spectroscopy of amines and substituted amides

In analyzing ir spectra, substituted or unsubstituted amides reveal a C=O band in the 1640-1690 cm−1 region. A free N−H stretch, if present, will be seen at 3050-3550 cm−1 , and an N−H bend for RCONH2 is found at 1600-1640 cm−1 and for RCONHR′ at 1530-1570 cm−1.

For free amines N-H stretching occurs in the 3200-3500 cm−1: for 1° amines, often two bands; for 2°, one band; for 3° amines, no band (since no N-H). 1° amines show N-H bending having strong bands at 650-900 cm−1 (broad) and one at 1560-1650 cm−1.

C−N stretching for aliphatic amines shows a weak band at 1030-1230 cm −1 (3° amines may show a doublet), whereas aromatic amines show a strong two bands at 1180-1360 cm−1.

The N-H protons of amines absorb usually in the range Δ 1-5, where simply proton counting determines the assignment. With N-H protons of amides (− CO−NH−), absorption is seen as a broad, low hump farther downfield in Δ 5-8.

Chap. 24 Phenols

24.1-.2 Structure. Nomenclature. Physical properties.

The general formula is ArOH, with Ar=phenyl, substituted phenyl, or other aryl group. They are distinguished from alcohols in that the -OH is attached directly to the aromatic ring. The simplest member is phenol. The methylphenols have the special name cresols. Occasionally the phenols are named as hydroxy- compounds. Some important phenols: o- chlorophenol, m-cresol (m-methylphenol), catechol (o- hydroxyphenol), resorcinol (m-hydroxyphenol), hydroquinone (p- hydroxyphenol), salicylic acid (o-hydroxybenzoic acid).

The simplest phenols are liquids or low m.p. solids. Hydrogen bonding explains their high b.p. values. Phenol is somewhat soluble in water (9 g per 100 g water), but most other phenols are insoluble. Phenols, like aromatic amines are easily oxidized, and color present in these compounds may be the impurities of oxidized reagent.

The properties of the nitrophenols in the special table show that the ortho- nitrophenol has a very low b.p. and a lower solubility in water than the other isomers. This is because the o-isomer is capable of intramolecular hydrogen bonding: because of the nearness of the -OH and then -NO2 groups to each other on the aromatic ring, the proton on the -O-H group is in an ideal location to hydrogen-bond with one of the oxygens of the nitro group on the same molecule. The m- and p-isomers show no such advantage and form hydrogen bonds between molecules or with the solvent. This intramolecular hydrogen bonding is manifest as both a lower solubility and a lower b.p., both affected by hydrogen bonding with solvents or other molecules.

insert physical properties of phenols and nitrophenols

24.3 Salts of phenols

Unlike ROH, phenols are fairly acidic, but less acidic than water. Aq. OH− converts them to their salts, and acids back into phenols, with salts being soluble in water and the acid forms not soluble. The Ka of phenols is about 10−10. Since carbonic acid is a stronger acid than phenols, NaHCO3 are unable to react with phenols to produce a sodium phenolate. This ability to dissolve in NaOH and not in NaHCO3 can be used to identify a phenol, distinguishing it from RCOOH. Phenols can also be separated from RCOOH by their insolubility in bicarbonate.

24.4 Industrial source

Although the laboratory methods often apply industrially, some special processes exist, especially for production of phenol itself. Some phenol and some cresols come from coal tar. But >90% is synthesized. Fusion of sodium benzenesulfonate with alkali is one method. The Dow process is a second method in which nucleophilic substitution of chlorobenzene by aqueous NaOH at high temperature is done.

C6H5Cl NaOH, 360°, 4500 psi
→ 		C6H5O → phenol

Another process involves peroxidation of cumene (isopropylbenzene) to cumene hydroperoxide, in which the peroxide is hydrolyzed to phenol and acetone.

C6H5CH(CH3)2 O2
→ 		C6H5C(OOH)(CH3)2 H2O, H+
→ 		phenol + acetone

Certain phenols and their ethers are isolated from the essential oils of various plants:

insert structures on p 791 and 792

1. Hydrolysis of diazonium salts.

2. Oxidation of arylthallium compounds

Pb(OAc)4 H2O, OH− H+

ArTl(OOCCF3)2 −−−−—→ ArOOCCF3 −−−—→ ArO− −→ ArOH

Ph3P heat

3. Alkali fusion of sulfonates (ch. 30)

Recent work with thallation of rings shows that arylthallium compounds can be oxidized by lead tetraacetate, Pb(OAc)4, and in the presence of triphenylphosphine to produce the phenolic ester of trifluoroacetic acid. Simple hydrolysis produces the phenol. No isolation of intermediates is necessary in carrying out the steps. It does have two advantages over diazonium synthesis of phenols: (a) speed and high yield as a result of fewer steps, (b) orientation control in the thallation step. A review of ch. 11 will explain the thallation process. For example, the thallation-oxidation of chlorobenzene produces only p-chlorophenol isomer.

24.7 Acidity of phenols

As previously described, phenols are converted to their salts by NaOH, but are not sufficiently acidic to react with NaHCO3. Phenols are stronger acids than water, but weaker than RCOOH. They are also much more acidic than alcohols, which have Ka values between 10−16 and 10−18.

Acidity of phenol can be attributed to resonance stabilization of its conjugate base, phenoxide ion. With phenol, resonance structures in which the oxygen shares a pair of electrons with the ring result in a separation of charges: the oxonium positive formal charge and the carbanion in the ring. This charge separation is less stable than a phenoxide ion, in which the charge can be better distributed as represented by three structures. With alcohols, R-O− has only one structure to represent it, and therefore is a far worse acid.

Aromatic amines have been shown to be weaker bases than aliphatic amines (23.3) for this same resonance effect as phenols are shown to be stronger acids than alcohols. Electron-withdrawing (nitro groups) and -releasing (methyl groups) on the ring also increase and decrease acidity, resp., reflecting their stabilization of the negative charge left after the H+ ion leaves.

24.8 Formation of ethers. Williamson synthesis

Phenols can be made into ethers when treated with RX in alkaline solutions. Methyl ethers can be formed using (CH3)2SO4. The substitution of RX in alkaline conditions, in which phenoxide is an excellent nucleophile, is classically SN2.

Some special aryl ether preparation reactions can use sodium alkoxides:

2,4-dinitrochlorobenzene + Na+−OCH3 → 2,4-dinitroanisole (2,4- dinitrophenyl methyl ether)

Alkylethers are less activating to the ring than the -OH group, which means that some reactions available to the reactive phenols are not available to arylethers, viz., coupling, Kolbe reaction, Reimer-Tiemann, etc. This is because the ether can not ionize to form the highly reactive phenoxide form of phenols. An advantage of lower reactivity is the aromatic ethers are not sensitive to oxidation:

KMnO4, OH−, heat H+

CH3OC6H4CH3 ———————→ −→ CH3OC6H4COOH

24.9 Ester formation. Fries rearrangement

The conversion of phenols to esters by action of acids, acid chlorides, and acid anhydrides has been discussed ( 18.16, 20.8, 20.15). Esters of phenols heated with AlCl3 cause the acyl group to migrate from phenolic oxygen to an ortho or para position of the ring, yielding a ketone. This is called the Fries rearrangement, and is used to synthesize phenolic ketones rather than direct acylation. For example, phenol + propionyl chloride → phenyl propionate, which is then treated with AlCl3 in CS2 to make o- and p-hydroxyphenyl ethyl ketones. (The o-isomer is separated from the p-isomer taking advantage of its ability to be steam-distilled.)

24.10 Ring substitution

EAS on phenols is very much promoted; as for aromatic amines, a nonbonding pair can form a double bond with the ring to participate in resonance. The oxonium formed assumes the charge, but every atom except hydrogen has the octet. The phenoxide form of the phenol is even more prone to EAS than phenol, being quite ready to stabilize any positive charge coming to the ring. Indeed the intermediate features the bound substituent to the ring as an uncharged, unsaturated ketone.

When phenols are treated with aqueous solutions of bromine, just about every H atom ortho or para to the -OH group is replaced by Br. If a low temperature and a solvent of low polarity (chloroform, CCl4, CS2) are used, the reaction can be limited to o- or p-bromophenol, although the p-isomer is the chief product. Polynitration also occurs in concentrated HNO3 with formation of 2,4,6-trinitrophenol (picric acid), but considerable oxidation also occurs. Dilute HNO3 at lower temperatures (20°) will produce mononitrophenols (o-isomer, 40%; p-isomer, 13%), but the yields are poor.

Alkylphenols can be made by F-C alkylation, but the yields are poor. Nitrosation of the ring has been shown earlier, with phenol + NaNO2, H2SO4 at 7-8°; the +NO ion is a weak nucleophile, but phenol has a very active ring.

24.11 Kolbe reaction. Synthesis of phenolic acids

Phenolic salts treated with CO2 (at 125° and 4-7 atm pressure) can result in a substitution reaction (called the Kolbe reaction) just like an EAS reaction. The products are sodium o- or p-hydroxybenzoate, which can be acidified. o-Hydroxybenzoic acid is salicylic acid, and is the chief product of the reaction. The intermediate may involve CO2 attaching itself to the phenoxide oxygen instead of the ring, but the electron-deficient carbon appears to attack the ring.

24.12 Reimer-Tiemann reaction. Synthesis of phenolic aldehydes. Dichlorocarbene

Phenol mixed with CHCl3 in aq. NaOH introduces an -CHO group to the ring, generally in the ortho position, and is known as the Reimer-Tiemann reaction. A substituted benzal is said to form, but is hydrolyzed in the alkaline medium.

CHCl3, aq. NaOH

phenol −−−−−−−−−−→ [o-Cl2CHC6H4O−] −−→ o-OCHC6H4O− −−−→ salicylaldehyde

70° chief product

The mechanism involves a dichlorocarbene (:CCl2) formed from chloroform by action of a strong base and shown to occur earlier in C=C bond addition (9.16). Even though electrically neutral, dichlorocarbene is considered a powerful electrophile because the carbon has only a sextet of electrons.

(1) OH− + CHCl3 −((− :CCl3− + H2O

(2) :CCl3− −((− :CCl2 + Cl−

(3) C6H5O− + :CCl2 −→ [o-OC6H5CCl2]−

(4) [o-OC6H5CCl2]− −→ [o-Cl2CHC6H4O−] (benzal chloride)

24.13-.14 Analysis and spectroscopy of phenols

The difference between the reaction of phenol with NaOH and NaHCO3 has already been thoroughly discussed. Many (but not all) phenols can form a colored complex (ranging from green to blue and violet to red) with FeCl3. Phenols can often be identified by their esters and ethers, and by bromination products.

Phenols are distinguished in ir spectra by the strong and broad O-H stretch in the 3200-3600 cm−1 region present for all ArOH and ROH. They are distinguished from ROH by a C—O stretch which is strong and broad: for phenols, at 1230 cm−1; for ROH, at 1050-1200 cm−1.

Like all ethers, phenolic ethers show no O-H stretch but do show C-O stretching: aryl & vinyl ethers, a strong and broad 1200-1275 cm−1 and a weaker 1020-1075 cm−1; ROR, 1060- 1150 cm−1.

Nmr spectra will show the O-H proton, the shift affected by the degree of H-bonding, and hence by temperature, concentration, and solvent nature. Signals at d 4-7 are to be expected, d 6-12 with intramolecular hydrogen bonding.

Part II Special Topics

Chap. 25 Aryl halides. Nucleophilic aromatic substitution

25.1-.2 Structure. Physical properties

Aryl halides have the general structure ArX, with the requirement of having a halogen directly attached to the aromatic ring. Benzyl chloride is not an ArX, for the chloride is not attached to the ring but the molecule is just a substituted RX compound. They are not as reactive as RX to nucleophilic substitution unless other groups are present on the ring which increase their reactivity, or by the use of very basic reagents or high temps. The nucleophilic aromatic substitution (NAS) reactions they can undergo involve two mechanisms: the bimolecular displacement mechanism for activated ArX, and the elimination-addition mechanism involving a benzyne intermediate.

Related somewhat to aryl halides in the lack of reactivity are the vinyl halides (-C=C-X), with a number of interesting parallels. While ArX can undergo electrophilic substitution, vinyl halides are subject to electrophilic addition. In EAS, ArX deactivates but directs ortho,para; in electrophilic addition, halogen deactivates but causes Markovnikov orientation. By its inductive effect, halogen deactivates the molecule toward electrophilic attack; by resonance it releases electrons but only to certain sites.

Both are also unreactive to nucleophilic substitution, in contrast to alkyl halides. The low reactivity is due to the partial double bond character which exists in the C-X bond.

The physical properties of ArX and RX are very much the same if comparing the same MW. Chlorobenzene and bromobenzene as an example have nearly the same b.p. as n-hexyl chloride and n-hexyl bromide. ArX are insoluble in water and soluble in the usual organics. Boiling points tend to be affected by weight, whereas melting points are affected by a particular symmetry; the p-isomer tends to be 70-100° lower in m.p. than the o- or m-isomer.

insert aryl halide table on p 819

25.3 Preparation

1. From diazonium salts

HNO3 redn. HONO BF4−

ArH −−−−→ ArNO2 −−−→ ArNH2 −−−→ ArN2+ −−−→ ArF

H2SO4 0° CuCl

−−−→ ArCl
