−−−→ ArI

2. Halogenation

Lewis acid

ArH + X2 −−−−−−→ ArX + HX

X2 = Cl2 , Br2

Lewis acid = FeCl3, AlCl3, Tl(OAc)3 , etc.

3. From arylthallium compounds

[ArH + Tl(OOCCF3)3 →] ArTl(OOCCF3)2 + KI −→ ArI

for iodides only

Direct halogenation of the aromatic ring is more useful than for alkanes: although two isomers (ortho and para) are the products, attack is not as random as in free radical halogenation. Moreover, if thallation is done with the bulky thallium acetate, direct bromination can occur exclusively to the para position. RX is often prepared from ROH, but ArX is not prepared from ArOH, being instead prepared from the diazonium salt (ArN2+) which comes from ArNO2. (ROH bears the same relationship as ArNO2 as the precursor to RX as to ArX.)

Halogenation via diazonium salts may be the most important, however. Rings can not be fluorinated or iodinated by directly but through this pathway. Moreover, direct halogenation produces o- and p-isomers which are difficult to separate from one another. On the other hand, the o- and p-nitro isomers which are precursors to the diazonium can be separated more easily (by distillation) from one another.

25.4 Reactions

RX typically undergoes nucleophilic substitution with functions like OH−, OR−, NH3, CN−, etc., to form alcohols, ethers, amines, nitriles, etc. Even F-C alkylation can be seen as a substitution by a basic aromatic ring displacing halide in RX. Aryl halides show no such reactivity except under the most rigorous conditions.

ArX can undergo an NAS reaction readily if the ring contains other groups which are quite deactivating (e.g., -NO2, -NO, -CN) and which are located in the proper position, viz., ortho or para to the halogen. Reactions of inactivated ArX with strong bases or at high temps occur by the benzyne intermediate and can be made to occur.

1. Formation of Grignard reagent (limitations in 15.15)

dry ether

ArBr + Mg −−−−−−→ ArMgBr

tetrahydrofuran

ArCl + Mg −−−−−−→ ArMgCl

2. Substitution in the ring. Electrophilic aromatic substitution (discussed in 11.21)

X: deactivates and directs ortho,para

3. Nucleophilic aromatic substitution. Bimolecular displacement

Ar:X + :Z −−−−−−→ Ar:Z + :X−

Ar must contain strongly electron-withdrawing groups ortho and/or para to −X.

4. Nucleophilic aromatic substitution. Elimination-addition.

Ar:X + :Z −−−−−−→ Ar:Z + :X−

Z is a strong base; ring must not be activated toward bimolecular displacement

25.5 Low reactivity of aryl and vinyl halides

When alcoholic AgNO3 is mixed and warmed with alkyl halides, silver halides are produced as the allyl, benzyl, and 3° bromides react almost instantly, whereas the 1° and 2° bromides react within about 5 minutes. Halogens joined directly to the ring or C=C carbon do not react, even when heated for days. Neither do OH−, OR−, NH3, CN−, or ArH + AlCl3 react with ArX or C=C-X.

25.6 Structure of aryl and vinyl halides

The reason for the low reactivity is attributable to (a) delocalization of electrons by resonance; (b) differences in the σ bond energies because of differences in hybridization of carbon.

insert structures of chlorobenzene and vinyl chloride on p 824.

Structures I-V show the possible resonance structures of chlorobenzene, and VI and VII of vinyl chloride. The fact that the resonance structures showing a carbon-chloride double bond (C=Cl) are possible shows that the C-Cl bond is something more than a single bond, something less than double. The transition state in nucleophilic substitution is far less stable than the reactant, making the Eact for displacement untenable.

The other interpretation is simply that the carbon in alkyl halides is sp3- hybridized to the halogen, while the carbon in ArX and C=C-X is sp2-hybridized. The latter bond is simply shorter and stronger, and the molecule is more stable. Such an interpretation was put forth in 5.4. The C-Cl bond length in chlorobenzene and vinyl chloride is 1.69 compared with a 1.77-1.80 range of bond lengths in many alkyl chlorides. For bromobenzene and vinyl bromide, C-Br bond length is 1.86 compared with 1.91-1.92 in alkyl bromides. Double bonds are shorter than single bonds as shown by physical data and by quantum mechanics.

Another interesting fact is that dipole moments in ArX and C=C-X are smaller than for RX. RX is quite polar because halogens are quite electronegative compared to carbon, causing the polarity; alkyl Cl and Br show values of between 2.02 and 2.15 d. With ArX and C=C-X, it is expected that the very mobile π electrons would contribute to a larger or as large dipole than in RX. However chloro- and bromobenzene have moments of only 1.7 d, and the vinyl analogs even less at 1.4 d. This fact is consistent with the resonance stabilization of these molecules, where the inductive effect of the halogen is opposed by the fact that resonance places the electrons away from where they might want to go. The net displacement is less than were there no resonance. Alternatively, sp2- hybridized carbon is more electronegative than the sp3-carbon, and would be less willing to release electrons to chlorine.

Added in proof of the willingness of halogens to participate in electron pair sharing in resonance at the cost of assuming a formal charge is the mechanism of halogen addition to C=C bonds, in which a cyclic halonium is the only possible explanation for the anti-addition result; here halogen is willing to form 2 bonds.

25.7 Nucleophilic aromatic substitution (NAS): bimolecular displacement

Chlorobenzene can be converted to phenol in aqueous NaOH, but only a very high temperatures over 300° (the Dow process: 6-8% NaOH, 350°, 4500 psi). If an -NO2 group is attached either ortho or para to the ring, the reaction will occur at a lower temp.: 160° with 15% NaOH. A - NO2 group meta to chlorine has no effect. If -NO2 is now attached both para and ortho (2,4-dinitrochlorobenzene), boiling Na2CO3 at 130° will perform the conversion to 2,4- dinitrophenol. And if the other ortho position is nitrated (2,4,6- trinitrochlorobenzene), warm water is enough to form the phenol (picric acid).

This same effect is seen in other nucleophilic displacement reactions, with -NO2 groups making the Ar-X very reactive. Groups other than nitro which are ortho or para to the halogen have similar effects: - N(CH3)3+, -CN, -SO3H, -COOH, -CHO, - COR. These are the familiar ring deactivators which are meta-directing to electrophilic substitution. If such groups are activating to NAS, then it would be expected that electron-withdrawing groups are deactivating, and this is found to be true. The general rule for NAS is that electron withdrawal causes activation and electron release causes deactivation.

25.8 Bimolecular displacement mechanism of NAS

The two-step mechanism for NAS is thus shown:

The first step involves the nucleophilic attack on the ring to form a carbanion as part of the ring. The second step involves expulsion of a weak base, the halide ion, to form the product. The carbanion can be said to be stabilized by resonance structures in the ring, with the anionic charge of the intermediate concentrated on the carbons which are at the positions ortho and para to the carbon holding the nucleophile, but with the charge distributed over the entire molecule. The geometry of the transition state of NAS can be compared to the SN2 reaction of aliphatic halides, in which a pentavalent carbon intermediate in the SN2 reaction appears as a less stable reaction, requiring greater energy input (larger Eact) than the intermediate in NAS, in which the carbon assumes a less crowded tetrahedral geometry. In fact the intermediate in the SN2 reaction is not a stable structure, but is the transition state (the top of the energy hill), whereas in NAS, the carbanion is formed and represents an energy minimum between the transition states of the nucleophile adding and the halide leaving.

25.9-.10 Reactivity and orientation in NAS

Reactivity of the aryl halide in NAS is again a matter of what groups attached to the ring stabilize or ease formation of the incipient carbanion in the transition state. The groups listed in the sections on EAS which are either activating or deactivating in correlation with the electron-releasing or - withdrawing abilities are conversely related to the reactions in NAS; what is activating in EAS is deactivating in NAS, and vice-versa.

In consideration of orientation, a model of the formation of the carbanion intermediate comparing both p- and m-chloronitrobenzene is used. In drawing the resonance structures of intermediates in which the carbanion is rotated to the carbons ortho and para, the most stable resonance structure in either the m- or p-chloronitrobenzene is one of the resonance structures in the p-isomer. Although the -NO2 is electron-withdrawing and affects all carbons on the ring, it does so most with the carbon to which it is attached, and it helps to disperse the anionic charge on this carbon. If the nitro group were ortho to the carbon undergoing substitution, one of its 3 resonance structures would also show this enhanced stability.

insert para and meta resonance structures on bottom p 831, top p 832

25.11 Electron withdrawal by resonance

The inductive effect accounts for a change in aromatic ring reactivity in both EAS and NAS, but inasmuch as ring reactivity is also affected by resonance in EAS, so it may be with NAS as well. Considering the p- and o-isomers of chloronitrobenzene again, the oxygens on the -NO2 are very electronegative, like the nitrogen on the group too. The illustration shows resonance structures of both isomers in which the negative charge leaves the ring and is assumed by the oxygen atom or atoms in the nitro group. The electronegativity of oxygen makes them especially able to assume the charge. Such resonance structures, in which the anionic charge moves out of the ring and onto the more electronegative atoms of the electron-withdrawing group, can be drawn for other groups as well: -CN, -COOH, -COR, -NO.

25.12 Evidence for the two steps in bimolecular displacement

The evidence for the multiple steps in EAS came about from kinetic studies, among others, in which no isotope effect could be observed in formation of the product. Isotope effect studies are of little use in NAS, however, since a higher atomic weight halogen is used and isotopes of a halogen would not amount to a significant change in rate even if the rate depended upon the leaving of the halogen.

However, making use of the J. Bunnet's notion of the element effect, if the rate does not depend upon the breaking of the C-X bond, then it should not matter which halogen is attached. In SN2 and SN1 displacements of alkyl halides, there is a tremendous difference in reactivity, with the C-F bond much more difficult to break than the C-I bond:

Reactivity of RX: R−I > R−Br > R−Cl > R−F

In NAS it has been shown that there is no difference in reactivity among the halides and that, more often than not, the aryl fluoride is the most reactive of the halogens. This comparative indifference in reactivity forces the conclusion that the reaction rate is independent of C-X bond breaking, and that attack of the nucleophile to form the carbanion is the slow step.

The faster reaction of aryl fluorides has been interpreted as a strong inductive effect in fluorine, which serves to stabilize the transition state of nucleophilic attack.

25.13 Nucleophilic substitution: aliphatic and aromatic

Of the three types of nucleophilic substitution, the SN1 reaction involves the leaving of the halide prior to the nucleophile attacking, forming a positive charge on the carbon in which the carbon is in an environment to favorably accommodate the charge. The SN1 reaction is favored because of electron release. With the SN2 reaction, very little charge develops on the carbon which is the center of the reaction, and this is probably because the carbon is in an environment in which it is unsuitable to assume a charged intermediate state. Reactions of the SN2-type are relatively insensitive to electron withdrawal or release. With NAS, the carbon undergoing attack does not assume the developing negative charge at all, but passes it to other carbons forming the especially stable array of the aromatic ring. NAS reactions are promoted by groups promoting electron withdrawal.

25.14 Elimination-addition mechanism for NAS. Benzyne

NAS following the bimolecular displacement mechanism requires the presence of electron-withdrawing groups on the ring, in which the nucleophile (base) is easily added. NAS can occur without such groups, but an especially strong nucleophile (base) is required, and even then, the mechanism is believed to proceed via a benzyne intermediate, or by steps of elimination-addition.

Aryl halides treated with the very strong base (nucleophile) amide ion, NH 2−, in ammonia form aniline. The total mechanism involves 4 steps, the first two corresponding to an elimination to form the benzyne, the last two with the nucleophile attacking the reactive benzyne to form the substituted product, aniline.

Insert mechanisms of 4 steps on p 836 and 837 and draw a benzyne structure.

A proton is pulled of the ring to form a carbanion, and the halide leaves with the bonding electrons while the carbanion bonds to form the benzyne. With reaction of the nucleophile to the benzyne, the proton is restored to the carbon now bearing the anionic charge. The benzyne structure is supposed to involve formation of a p bond within the ring plane (neither above or below with the other conjugate pi bonds), and there is little sideways overlap permitted, making the bond reactive.

That this mechanism is possible comes from a number of facts.

(a) In looking at the benzyne intermediate, it can be deduced that not only the carbon originally bearing the halogen can be a target of the nucleophile, but so can the carbons on either side, depending on the benzyne bond orientation. Using chlorobenzene in which the carbon attached to the Cl atom had been labeled with 14C, J. D. Roberts (1953) found that in a reaction with NH2− in liq. NH3 that 47% of the aniline had -NH2-bonded directly to 14C, and the other 53% to the carbon next to the label.

(b) The loss of a proton on the carbon next to, i.e., ortho to the carbon bearing the halide is necessary in supposing a benzyne intermediate. In fact compounds like 2-bromo-3-methylanisole with no available ortho hydrogens do not react with NH2− in liq. NH3.

(c) The mechanism not only supposes that a proton is removed in the first step to form the benzyne intermediate, but that this should logically be the slow (rate-determining) step in the reaction. Evidence of this comes from experiments in which a 50:50 mixture of bromobenzene and o- deuteriobromobenzene was treated with NH2− for a period, and the amount of unreacted material of both compounds determined. The result showing much more unreacted 2H-bromobenzene indicated an isotope effect which involves hydrogens of the ortho position, and in which the reaction rate depends upon C-H bond breaking.

(d) That the first step is proton removal by the base is further confirmed by a reaction in which o-deuteriofluorobenzene is treated with NH2 −, which forms aniline slowly if at all. When reactant is isolated prior to any significant aniline formation, the reactant is found to be ordinary fluorobenzene. An equilibrium reaction in which base removes the proton (deuterium) and then replaces it because of the difficult of breaking a C-F bond is indicated. Clearly with other halides, such as C-Br, the carbon-halogen bond is broken perhaps as the base is removing the proton, but with a stronger carbon-halogen bond, formation of the benzyne is inhibited.

k1

(1) C6H5X + NH2− −((− :C6H4X− + NH3

k-1

k 2

(2) :C6H4X− —→ C6H4− (benzyne) + X−

for X = F, k-1 >> k2

X = Br, k2 >> k-1

(e) If a benzyne intermediate is to be formed, it must be formed in such an orientation in which the more stable carbanion intermediate is formed in respect to the groups attached to the aromatic ring. When m-bromoanisole and o- bromoanisole are treated with NH2− in liq. NH3, they both yield the same product m-anisidine (m- aminoanisole). The -OCH3 is electron-withdrawing as far as its inductive effect, although in EAS, it is involved in activation of the ring by resonance. But because resonance stabilization is not possible with benzyne in which the carbanion electrons are out of the plane of the p cloud, only the inductive effect is observed. The inductive effect is greater on the carbons nearer the -OCH3 group. When o-bromoanisole is reacting with NH2−, there is only one proton ortho to the Br atom, so the benzyne formed is constrained. And although there are protons on either side of Br in m-bromoanisole, the proton between -OCH3 and -Br is preferentially abstracted (as opposed to the proton para to -OCH3, ortho to -Br) because its carbon will yield the more stable carbanion: the negative charge is dispersed by the closely located -OCH3 group. Both benzynes are equivalent, and even the attack of NH2− to the benzyne intermediate is directed to the carbon meta to -OCH3, as the resulting carbanion intermediate is on the carbon closest to -OCH3 and the more stable. Placing the -NH2 group ortho to -OCH3 with the carbanion meta to -OCH3 would be a less stable carbanion intermediate.

Organolithium compounds can also produce benzyne intermediates. o-fluoroanisole (o-FC6H4OCH3) can be treated with phenyllithium (C6H5−Li+), in which the strong phenyl ion base abstracts proton to form the weaker base, [o- FC6H3OCH3]−Li+ plus the weaker acid, benzene. The carbon-fluorine bond breaks to form the benzyne and Li+F−. The benzyne then reacts with additional phenyllithium to produce the carbanion-containing base Li+[m- C6H5- C6H3OCH3]− which can undergo no further reaction for want of a strong acid. H2O (a strong acid) is added to complete the reaction to form the m-phenylanisole and Li+OH−.

Organolithium compounds (RLi) resemble Grignard reagents (RMgX) in their reactions. The carbon metal bond is not only highly polar covalent, but nearly ionic, more so in RLi than RMgX since Li is more electropositive than Mg. For this reason RLi is more reactive than RMgX.

25.15 Analysis of aryl halides

ArX demonstrate many predictable reactions or nonreactions: insolubility in cold conc. H2SO4; inertness to Br2 in CCl4 and to permanganate; formation of orange to red colors when CHCl3 and AlCl3 are added; dissolution in cold fuming H2SO4, but at a rate slower than for benzene. Elemental analysis should be halogen-positive when evaluating aromatic compounds. RX reacts with AgNO3, whereas ArX and vinyl-X are negative.

Chap. 26 Carbanions. Malonic ester and acetoacetic ester syntheses

26.1 Carbanions in organic synthesis

Carbanions are nucleophilic carbons. Organometallic compounds are examples of such (basic) carbons, in which the strongly or weakly electropositive metal makes possible what amounts to acid-base chemistry. The Grignard and organocadmium reagents, the lithium dialkylcopper of the Corey- House reaction, and the organozinc intermediate of the Reformatsky reaction show carbons with negative charges. The carbanions generated because of the increased acidity α-hydrogens brought about by carbonyls is another example.

26.2 Malonic ester synthesis of RCOOH

The synthesis of larger RCOOH molecules is usually done by the use of ethyl malonate, CH2(COOC2H5), in the malonic ester synthesis. Two factors make this synthesis useful: (a) the alpha hydrogens are quite acidic with the presence of two activating carbonyls instead of one; (b) malonic acid and substituted malonic acids undergo decarboxylation very easily.

The reaction usually involves mixing ethyl malonate with sodium ethoxide (NaOEt) to produce the salt sodiomalonic ester. When brought into contact with an alkyl halide, RX, the basic carbanion of the malonate reacts to produce the nucleophilic substituted product. ArX do not react at all to the carbanion, as it is not sufficiently basic. 3° RX will react, but the elimination product is obtained. The best reaction is with 1° RX—lower yields with 2° RX—to produce the alkylmalonic ester product (and Na+X−).

Since there is still one α-hydrogen remaining, an additional reaction to form the dialkyl malonic ester is possible.

Malonic acid when heated above its m.p. decomposes with loss of CO2 to form acetic acid. In a similar way, the substituted malonic acid can be heated to cause a decomposition resulting in loss of CO2 to form what is a substituted acetic acid. First the ester is hydrolyzed in warm alkaline medium (base catalysis drives the reaction to the right because of a more stable carboxylate ion). The acid salt is acidified then heated (to about 140°), resulting in decarboxylation. Hence, a malonic ester synthesis yields an acetic acid in which one or two of its hydrogens have been replaced by alkyl groups.

When planning the synthesis, the only problem is selection of the proper RX. Consider the structure of the acid. For example, isocaproic acid, (CH3)2CHCH2CH2COOH is an acetic acid in which a hydrogen has been replaced with an isobutyl group. Isobutyl bromide is an ideal RX, being a 1° RX, and the acetic acid group is provide by ethyl malonate:

1. ethyl malonate + NaOEt −−→ Na+CH(COOEt)2− + EtOH

2. Na+CH(COOEt)2− + (CH3)2CHCH2Br −−→ ethyl isobutylmalonate

H2O, OH−, heat H + heat

3. ethyl isobutylmalonate −−→ dicarboxylate salt −−→ dicarboxylic acid −−→ isocaproic acid

If α-methylvaleric acid, CH3CH2CH2CH(CH3)COOH, is to be made, this is clearly a disubstituted acetic acid, with one substitute a methyl group, the other n-propyl. Therefore, two different RX compounds are necessary: methyl bromide and n-propyl bromide. Either the addition of small amounts of one reactant to the other or the proportions of reactants is adjusted to limit the extent of reaction in which one RX is substituted then the other.

Although NaOEt was shown as the base, modifications of malonic ester synthesis are done with potassium tert-butoxide. Moreover leaving groups other than halides are exploited, such as ROSO3Ar or ROSO3R′. Polar aprotic solvents have also been used: DMSO, DMF. In place of simple RX compounds are α-bromo esters (not α-bromo acids).

26.3 Acetoacetic ester synthesis of ketones

Whereas the malonic ester synthesis makes RCOOH, the acetoacetic ester synthesis uses ethyl acetoacetate, CH3COCH2COOEt, to make ketones. The ester is treated with NaOEt to produce the reactive α-carbanion, which then reacts with RX to make the alkylacetoacetate ester. The presence of another a-hydrogen allows one more reaction to make the dialklyacetoacetate ester. Hydrolysis of the ester is done in basic or acidic solution, to make the corresponding acid, which then undergo rapid decarboxylation (heated if necessary) to form a methyl ketone (mono- or disubstituted acetone) of either one form, CH3COCH2R, or another, CH3COCHRR′. The decarboxylation step occurs more readily with acetoacetic acids than with malonic acids.

In planning a synthesis, careful thought is first given as to whether it has the acetone structure. The substitutions on one of the methyl carbons is then assessed. 5-methyl-2-hexanone has a single substitution in which isobutyl bromide would be the ideal halide. 3-methyl-2-hexanone has a structure showing disubstitution, and so n-propyl bromide and methyl bromide would be appropriate in the synthesis.

26.4 Decarboxylation of β-keto acids and malonic acids

These carbanionic mediated syntheses leading to acids or ketones depended upon (i) acidity of a-hydrogens and (ii) decarboxylation. Decarboxylation of b- keto acids involves both examination of the free acid structure and its conjugate base, the carboxylation ion. If CO2 is lost from the carboxylate ion, the resulting molecule is a enolic carbanion which is stabilized by sharing of the negative charge with the ketolic oxygen. Decarboxylation of the acid form would involve proton transfer to the keto oxygen either prior to or in synchrony with loss of CO2. An uncharged enol is clearly present, rearranging to form acetone.

26.5 Direct and indirect alkylation of esters and ketones

Rather than preparing a-substituted acids and ketones via the dicarbonyl esters used, why not do it directly, i.e., by preparing the carbanions of the acids and ketones to react with the alkyl halides? Because certain obstacles obtain: (a) self-condensation, such as an aldol condensation with the ketones; (b) polyalkylation; (c) for unsymmetrical ketones, alkylation at both a-carbons can occur, or at the wrong one. As to the self-condensation reaction, RX competes with the carbonyl carbon of a second ketone molecule for the carbanion. While the right base-solvent combination allows for direct use of ketones, there is limited applicability. With the esters, there is only one particular α-carbon which directs alkylation to that carbon, giving somewhat more control.

26.6 Synthesis of acids and esters via 2-oxazolines

Acids and esters can also be synthesized via 2-oxazolines. The carboxylic acid reacts to form the cyclic 2-oxazoline. This serves to protect the -COOH group. If these compounds are treated with a strong base, such as an organolithium to abstract the hydrogen from the α-carbon, these basic 2- oxazoline can easily react with an alkyl halide to form a substituted 2- oxazoline, and the RCOOH can be regenerated by acidification. The reaction sequence is illustrated:

insert reaction bottom p 855.

The synthesis depends upon: (a) ease of formation and hydrolysis of 2-oxazolines; (b) the retained acidity of the α-hydrogens; (c) the inertness of the ring to a strong base such as the organolithium (Grignards can also be used).

26.7 Organoborane synthesis of acids and ketones

Hydroboration of the C=C followed by oxidation produced alcohols. Organoboranes do not have to be oxidized however. In the presence of base, alkylboranes can react with bromoacetone to produce alkylacetones and with ethyl bromoacetate to yield ethyl alkylacetates.

R3B + BrCH2COCH3 −−→ RCH2COCH3

R3B + BrCH2COOEt −−→ RCH2COOEt

The mechanism of the reaction has been postulated below. A base abstracts a proton from the carbon holding the Br atom, and alpha to the carbonyl. The strong base now finds a (Lewis) acid in the organoborane, the boron atom assuming the negative charge. A 1,2-alkyl shift from the boron to the alpha carbon, with leaving of the Br atom occurs, and protonolysis occurs leaving a substituted acetone product. This last reaction is more difficult than protonolysis of Grignard reagent; it is not equilibrium-controlled, but rate- controlled since it is not the stronger base R:− that gets the proton, but rather the resonance-stabilized RCHCOCH3− carbanion.

insert 4-step mechanism on pp 856-7

The mechanism essentially is three acid-base reactions and a 1,2-alkyl shift. The organoborane synthesis is comparable to acetoacetic ester synthesis since it furnishes an acetone unit (as bromoacetone) or an acetic acid unit (as bromoacetic ester). Bromine plays the same part as −COOEt as it increases the acidity of α-hydrogens and determines the carbon to be alkylated. It is also easily lost during the alkylation process; −COOEt must be removed by a hydrolysis and decarboxylation process.

High yields of product depend upon proper selection of reagents. The best base is the bulky potassium 2,6-di-tert-butylphenoxide. The best alkylating agent is B-alkyl-9-borabicyclo[3.3.1]nonane, or B-alkyl-9-BBN, produced by the reactions shown.

insert reactions bottom p 857

The 9-BBN compound is then used to convert alkenes to ketones and esters:

insert reactions shown at top of p 858

In addition to bromoacetone, other bromomethyl ketones (BrCH2COR) can be used if available. Bromination is best carried out with CuBr2, and on ketones in which R contains no α-hydrogens to compete those on the methyl: acetophenone, methyl tert-butyl ketone.

26.8 Alkylation of carbonyl compounds via enamines

Amines can react with carbonyls by a nucleophilic addition. 1° amines add, but dehydrate to form an imine.

-C=O + H2NR′ —−→ -C(OH)-NHR′ —−→ -C=NR′ (an imine)

If the carbonyl cmpd contains an a-hydrogen, the elimination favors the imine rather than an enamine (-C=C-NHR′) since the imine is more stable. Indeed enamines rapidly tautomerize to form the imine. This is quite analogous to the keto-enol tautomerization wherein the keto form is preferred.

2° amines react to, but in this case the enamine does form so long as there are α-hydrogens; the nitrogen having no available hydrogens, the imine is not possible.

-CH—C=O + HNR′2 —−→ -CH—C(OH)-NHR′ —−→ -C=C−NR′2 (an enamine)

As shown by G. Stork in 1954, enamines have a nucleophilic carbon as a result of the high electron density of the C=C bond. Alkylation of an enamine is possible with an alkyl halide, to form an iminium ion, which can be hydrolyzed to regenerate the carbonyl.

show reaction on p. 859 with the RX compound

The overall process is a 1° amine reacts with a ketone (with at least one a-hydrogen) to produce an alkylated ketone. Although the enamine nitrogen is just as nucleophilic as the carbon, a quaternary nitrogen would result. Heating of the N-alkylated compound changes it to the C-alkylated product.

The nitrogens of enamines behave similarly to aromatic amines, which is not surprising since they are vinyl amines, and aryl and vinyl amines share similar properties. The commonly used 2° amines in the reaction are the heterocyclic pyrrolidine and morpholine:

insert pyrrolidine and morpholine structures on p 860

The best yields are obtained with reactive halides like benzyl and allyl halides, α-halo esters, and α-halo ketones:

insert reactions middle p 860

Chap. 27 α,β-Unsaturated carbonyl compounds. Conjugate addition

27.1-.2 Structure and properties. Preparation

Because these cmpds contain both C=C and C=O, they undergo reactions typical of cmpds containing only those groups: electrophilic addition of acids and halogens, hydrogenation, hydroxylation, and cleavage at C=C; nucleophilic substitution typical of -COOR and addition typical of −COR of −CHO. The double bonds are conjugated, which will bring a special condition to their chemistries. Some α,β-unsaturated compounds possess special names given in the table showing the physical properties.

insert table of a,b-unsaturated carbonyls

Preparation is via the aldol condensation to make unsaturated aldehydes or ketones or the dehydrohalogenation of α-halo acids or the Perkin condensation to make the unsaturated acids.

27.3 Interaction of functional groups

The C=C bond is generally activated by electron-releasing groups, deactivated by electron-withdrawing groups, but nonetheless serves as a source of electrons for electrophiles. Since a carbonium will necessarily form as an intermediate, electron-releasers attached to the C=C bond should stabilize the transition state by dispersing the developing positive charge. C=O, -COOH, −COOR, and -CN groups are very electron-withdrawing, so reactivity to electrophilic addition should be low. It is found that a,b-unsaturated ketones, acids, esters, and nitriles are less reactive than simple alkenes to halogens or hydrogen halides.

Quite the contrary, the deactivation of C=C bond in a,b-unsaturated ketones, acids, esters, and nitriles makes it susceptible toward nucleophilic addition rather than electrophilic addition.

27.4 Electrophilic addition

The carbonyl group both lowers the reactivity and affects the orientation of the addition. With addition of an unsymmetrical agent, the electrophile (H+ if an acid) tends to add to the a-carbon, while the negative group (nucleophilic portion) adds to the β-carbon.

CH2=CH-CHO + HCl (g) —−→ ClCH2CH2CHO

CH2=CH-COOH + H2O ————−→ HOCH2CH2CHO

CH3−CH=CH-COOH + HBr (g) —−→ CH3CHBrCH2COOH

(CH3)2C=CH-CO−CH3 + CH3OH —−→ (CH3)2CH(OCH3)CH2COCH3

The mechanism starts with protonation of the carbonyl oxygen rather than attack of the C=C bond (!). The initially formed oxonium rapidly becomes a dispersed polycarbonium ion as a result of resonance stabilization by the adjacent double bond. Addition of the hydrogen to the b-carbon in the compound would produce a less stable resonance structure since the oxygen does not want to part of the positive charge distribution. The nucleophile :Z is then attracted to one of three carbons bearing the charge. The a-carbon can be ruled out just as it would not allow proper reformation of the p bond. Addition to the carbonyl carbon (a 1,2-addition) would produce an unstable hydroxy compound. Addition to the b-carbon (1,4-addition) produces the more stable product, allowing reformation of the p bond. The result is an enol which quickly tautomerizes to form the keto form, and the product is as shown.

insert mechanism with modifications on p 869, 870

27.5 Nucleophilic addition

Aq. NaCN added to the C=C bond of a,b-unsaturated carbonyls, but by a different mechanism.

NaCN (aq)

C6H5CH=CHCOC6H5 ———−→ C6H5CH(CN)CH2COC6H5

NaCN (aq)

CH3CH=CHCOOEt ———−→ CH3CH(CN)CH2COOEt

Ammonia and certain of its derivatives like amines, hydroxylamine, phenylhydrazine, etc., also add to make β-amino carbonyls.

NaCN (aq)

(CH3)2C=CHCOCH3 + CH3NH2 ———−→ (CH3)2C(NHCH3)CH2COCH3

NaCN (aq)

trans-HOOCC=CHCOOH + NH3 ———−→ HOOCCH(NH2)CH2COOH

 

C6H5C=CHCOOH + NH2OH ———−→ C6H5C(NHOH)−CHCOOH

The second reaction converts fumaric acid to aminosuccinic acid (aspartic acid).

The mechanism involves nucleophilic attack directly on the β-carbon, causing formation of a negative charge resonance-stabilized by the a-carbon and carbonyl carbon and oxygen; the oxygen is particularly suitable for accommodating the charge. The H+ then adds to either the a-carbon or oxygen, forming either the keto or enol, equilibrium ultimately favoring the keto.

The nucleophiles are typically the strong bases which add to the simple carbonyls, aldehydes and ketones. In fact these nucleophiles add to the carbonyl of a,b-unsaturated aldehydes instead of to the carbon- carbon double bond.

27.6 Comparison of electrophilic and nucleophilic addition

Both the nucleophilic and electrophilic addition can be compared in a number of ways: (a) addition occurs in two steps; (b) the first and controlling step is formation of an intermediate ion; (c) orientation of addition and reactivity are determined by stability of the intermediate, or rather, the stability of the transition state leading to formation of the intermediate; (d) the stability depends upon dispersal of the charge.

Of the differences, the nucleophilic addition involves stabilization of a negative charged intermediate, while electrophilic addition a positively charged intermediate. Groups attached to the carbons either activate or deactivate either of the addition reactions, depending upon whether they are electron- withdrawing or -releasing.

27.7 The Michael addition

The Michael addition takes advantages of the acidity of malonic esters or their malonic analogs to form their conjugate bases, which can now act as nucleophiles for a,b-unsaturated compounds.

piperidine

C6H5CH=CHCOC6H5 + CH2(COOEt)2 ———−→ C6H5CH[CH(COOEt)2]CH2COC6H5

OEt−

C6H5CH=CHCOOEt + CH2(COOEt)2 —−→ C6H5CH[CH(COOEt)2]CH2COOEt

OEt−

CH3CH=CHCOOEt + CH3CH(COOEt)2 —−→ CH3CH[C(CH3)(COOEt)2]CH2COOEt

OEt−

CH2=C(CH3)COOEt + NCCH2COOEt —−→ CH2[CH(CN)COOEt]CH(CH3)COOEt

The mechanism is outlined for the case of the malonic ester:

(1) CH2(COOEt)2 + :Base —−→ H:Base+ + CH(COOEt)2−
