| |

CH(COOEt)2 CH(COOEt)2

The carbanionic ethyl malonate ester formed is stabilized by distribution of the negative charge over a five-atom resonance hybrid involving both atoms of both carbonyls and the alpha carbon. Ethyl cyanoacetate and ethyl acetoacetate are similar to ethyl malonate in their stabilization of the carbanion, and by their ability to react with the unsaturated carbonyls.

27.8 The Diels-Alder reaction

Conjugated dienes react with a,b-unsaturated carbonyls in a reaction which produces a six-membered ring called the Diels-Alder reaction.

insert D-A reaction prototype p 876

The mechanism is a concerted single-step reaction most likely, and demonstrates cycloaddition: the diene has 4 p electrons while the dienophile has 2 p electrons to contribute, so it is called [4+2] cycloaddition.

insert all reactions on p 876 and 877

27.9 Quinones

Quinones are cyclic diketones (α,β-unsaturated ketones) of a rather special nature. Oxidation of aniline with dichromate produces p- benzoquinone, a yellow-colored substance. In fact, all quinones are colored because of extensive conjugation. p-Benzoquinone can be reduced (e.g., with SO32−) to form hydroquinone (p-hydroxyphenol), which can be oxidized (e.g., with Fe3+) to re-form the benzoquinone. This ease of cyclic oxidation and reduction makes them ideal oxidizing and reducing systems in biological forms.

Chap. 28 Rearrangements and neighboring group effects. Nonclassical ions

28.1 Rearrangements and neighboring group effects: intramolecular nucleophilic attack

The driving force behind all carbonium ion reactions is the need to provide electrons for the electron-deficient carbon. Rearrangements reflect this need to stabilize not just carbon, but any electron-deficient atom. Carbonium ions result from the departure of a leaving group, and this may cause a group on the molecule to migrate, making rearrangement effectively an intramolecular nucleophilic substitution. As nucleophilic substitution, it may be SN2-like, in which the migrating group may actually help, assist, or push out the leaving group, called anchimeric assistance. Or it may be SN1-like: waiting from the leaving group to disjoin at its own pace, taking advantage of the developed charged intermediate.

Some rearrangements may be considered aborted rearrangements: the near-by group bring the electrons to stabilize the carbocation, but goes back where it came from. This occasions what are neighboring group effects: groups near the reaction center which participate in, direct the course of, or otherwise assist in or promote the reaction. Indeed neighboring group effects involve the same processes as rearrangements, but their mechanism is hidden. In a reaction such as the one illustrated below, the group G may actually participate in the reaction by forming a bridged intermediate to stabilize the carbonium until the proper nucleophile displaces it.

Neighboring group effects reveal themselves by stereochemical changes or by deviations from what is expected in the stereochemistry of the reaction or perhaps by an unusually fast rate of reaction. Examples of neighboring group effects have been studied: the reaction of halohydrin to form an epoxide, which is a stable and isolable briged intermediate.

The neighboring group which will stabilize the carbonium ion must bring with electron pairs to bond with the carbonium. These can be nonbonding pairs as are found on oxygen, nitrogen, sulfur, or bromine. They might be the p electrons of a double bond or in an aromatic ring. In some cases they might be the bonding electrons of s MOs.

Although stronger nucleophiles compete with the neighboring group, its advantage is its proximity as a member of the molecule, making its effective concentration quite high.

28.2 Hofmann rearrangement. Migration to electron-deficient nitrogen

The Hofmann degradation of amides to form amines was briefly discussed in the section on amines. The reaction mechanism is believed to occur as follows:

(1) R−CO− + OBr− —−→ R−CO− + OH−

(2) R−CO−+ OH− —−→ R−CO−− + H2O

(3) R−CO−− —−→ R−CO− + Br−

(4) R−CO− —−→ R−=C=O

(5) R−=C=O + 2 OH− —−→ R−H2 + CO32−

The halogenation of the amide in step 1 is known and is isolable so long as the base is not present. If base is present, the amine forms. In step 2, a proton is removed by hydroxide, a reaction promoted by the increased acidity because of the bonding of bromine. As the halogen leaves in step 3, the nitrogen is now very electron deficient with only 6 valence electrons, but this intermediate does not exist in all likelihood. In step 4 the electron-deficient nitrogen bond with the R group in probably what is an SN2-like reaction, in which the R group pushes out the halide, making step 3 and 4 simultaneous. The carbonyl carbon must similarly let loose its hold. The isocyanate in step 4 is sensitive to the presence of water, and forms the amides and carbonate; the isocyanate can be isolated if the reaction is done without water.

The reaction is essentially similar to the 1,2-shifts seen with carbonium ions, which involve electron-deficient carbons, but the development of electon deficicency occurs while migration takes place rather than before. The evidence of the mechanism is strong: (1) many intermediates have been isolated; (2) the intermediates used to start the reaction yield the degradation products; (3) analogous mechanisms of other reactions support the mechanism.

28.3 Hofmann rearrangement. Intramolecular or intermolecular?

Although the Hofmann rearrangement has been shown as intramolecular, there remains a question of whether it is in fact intermolecular, yielding a mutually assured product. T. J. Prosser and E. L. Eliel carried out the degradition using m-deuteriobenzamide and [15N]benzamide. If the intermolecular reaction occurred, the double-labeled, i.e., [15N]m-deuteriobenzamide, and unlabeled, i.e. just benzamide, would be found. They were not, so the intramolecular mechanism as shown is the correct one.

insert structures on top p 890.

28.4 Hofmann rearrangement. Stereochemistry at the migrating group

The SN2-like substitution supposed as a step in the Hofmann rearrangement has also been studied. Optically active (+)-a-phenylpropionamide was subjected to Hofmann degradation and produce (−)-a-phenylethylamine, which optically pure and shows retention of configuration (see diagram)

insert stereochemical structure middle p 890

This means that the N atom takes the same relative position of the carbonyl C, and that the carbonyl C does not break away until it has been attaching itself to N. If the chiral carbon-carbonyl carbon were to break away, the reaction would in fact become intermolecular, and the freedom would entail considerable loss of configuration. The actual reaction supposes the formation of a pentavalent intermediate about the chiral carbon.

insert reaction sequence bottom p 890

28.5 Hofmann rearrangement. Timing of the steps

Steps (3) and (4) of the rearrangement were considered to be simultaneous. One reason supposing such is that an intermediate with a highly electronegative element like nitrogen possessing only a sextet of electrons is hard to imagine: it is even less stable than a primary carbocation, which are seldom formed.

Another reason is structure on the reaction rate. Various p-substituted benzamides were treated with OBr− and their order of reactivity depended upon the group para to -CONH2.

Order of reactivity: -OCH3 > -CH3 > -H > - Cl > -NO2

The order clearly demonstrates that electron-releasing group enhance the reaction. The migration of the alkyl group has been shown to involve pentavalent carbon, but the migration of an aryl group requires the formation of positive charge in the ring in order to bond with both nitrogen and the carbonyl carbon. This is amounts to an EAS intermediate, and electron-releasing groups clearly activate the ring toward such reactions.

If it were supposed that steps (3) and (4) were distinct, one of the steps would be rate-determining compared to the other. If the rearrangement step, i.e., step 4, is slower, then halide leave with the formation of ArCON, waiting to rearrangement. This would entail an equilibrium in which ArCON could react with Br− to re-form ArCONBr, or more likely, to combine with the water solvent to form ArCONHOH, which is hydroxyamic acid. But this does not form. If therefore the leaving of the halide is the slow step, this is contrary to the kinetic finding that rearrangement is the slow step, and N-Br bond breaking is not. Therefore (3) and (4) are considered concerted reactions. The migrating group undergoes electrophilic substitution, but the nitrogen is involved in nucleophilic substitution.

28.6 Rearrangement of hydroperoxides. Migration to electron-deficient oxygen

24.4 showed how peroxidation of isopropylbenzene (cumene) led to formation of cumene hydroperoxide, which could be acid-treated in water to form phenol and acetone. The mechanism is considered to involve a rearrangement which depends upon formation of an electron-deficient oxygen with a 1,2-shift.

(1) C6H5C(CH3)2OOH + H+ −((− C6H5C(CH3)2OOH2 +

(2) C6H5C(CH3)2OOH2 + −→ C6H5C(CH3)2O+ + H2O

(3)C6H5C(CH3)2O+ −→ C6H5−+O=C(CH3)2

(4) C6H5−+O=C(CH3)2 + H2O −→ C6H5−O−C(CH3)2 OH2+ −((− C6H5−O−C(CH3)2OH + H+

H+

(5) C6H5−O−C(CH3)2OH —→ CH3COCH3 + C6H5OH

Acid in step (1) protonates the hyrdroperoxide to generate a good leaving group in H2O [step (2)], leaving the electron-deficient oxygen. The rearrangement then occurs as in step (3) in Hofmann style, favored by the aromatic ring′s ability to disperse the positive charge typical of EAS. The nucleophilic H2O returns to the carbon controlling most of the positive charge on the alkyl side chain, and a hemi-acetal is formed in step (4), which breaks down in step (5) normally to form the carbonyl and alcohol which forms it: acetone and phenol in this case.

28.7 Rearrangement of hydroperoxides. Migratory aptitude

A difference between the hydroperoxide and Hofmann rearrangement is that the rearrangement could have involved either one of two groups: either the phenyl ring, which actually migrated, or one of the methyl groups which did not. This ultimately would have yielded different products, viz., methanol and acetophenone. Since the phenol and acetone products are formed quantitatively, this indicates the faster migration of phenyl versus methyl.

One clear reason why this is true is the transition state intermediate: migration of -CH3 would have required a pentavalent carbon bearing the positive charge, where the aryl migration produces not a tetrahedral benzenonium ion much better able to disperse the charge and a assume not only a comfortable geometrical transition state, but perhaps be an intermediate.

Insofar as the preference of the aryl group, the substituents attached to it affect which group, if there are many aryl groups, will migrate; and the particular group which migrates is one stabilized by electron-releasing groups, as mentioned in sec. 28.5. p-Nitrotriphenylmethyl hydroperoxide treated with acid forms only phenol, and p-nitrobenzophenone, showing how - NO2 takes away its group′s migrating potential.

28.8 Pinacol rearrangement. Migration to electron-deficient carbon

2,3-dimethyl-2,3-butanediol, also called pinacol, treated with mineral acid forms methyl tert-butyl ketone or 3,3-dimethyl-2-butanone, also called pinacolone, and H2O. The dehydration of this glycol is a model for other glycols as well, since they undergo similar dehydration and what is called the pinacol rearrangement.

The mechanism is similar to most acid-base reactions involving formation of a carbonium, which is a tertiary carbocation, one of the most stable. However, it is not as stable as the protonated carbonyl, so there is a 1,2-shift which allows formation of the protonated ketone. Release of the H+ ion accounts for the observed product.

H<+

(1) (CH3)2C(OH)−C(OH)(CH3)2 −((− (CH3)2C(OH)−C(+OH2)(CH3)2

(2) (CH3)2C(OH)−C(+OH2)(CH3)2 −→ (CH3)2C(OH)−C+(CH3)2 + H2O

(3) (CH3)2C(OH)−C+(CH3)2 −→ CH3C(=+OH)−C(CH3)3

(4) CH3C(=+OH)−C(CH3)3 −→ CH3CO−C(CH3)3 + H+

When the groups attached to the carbon bearing the -OH group differ from one another, the rearrangement may give rise to more than one product. The products obtained depend therefore upon (a) which -OH group leaves in step (2) of the mechanism, (b) which group migrates to the carbonium ion formed from step (2). For 1-phenyl-1,2-propanediol, either one of two carboniums may be formed in the intermediate, either at C1 or C2. If the carbocation is at C1, either the -H group or the -CH3 group could migrate, to form either methyl benzyl ketone or a-phenylpropionaldehyde, resp. If carbocation is at C2, then either the C6H5- group migrates to form a-phenylpropionaldehyde, or -H migrates to form ethyl phenyl ketone. The only product obtained is the methyl benzyl ketone.

This makes sense at least in the consideration of deciding which carbonium ion is to be formed: the C1 carbonium is a benzyl cation whereas the C2 is 2°, so the carbonium should be favored at C1. Predicting the migratory group is not so easy. Aryl groups migrate in preference to alkyl groups (-Ar > -R) as shown in previous sections, but when the migratory group is hydrogen, in the pinacol rearrangement, -H can migrate in preference to -Ar or - R, but -R can migrate in preference to -H.

One difference between the pinacol rearrangement and the rearrangements involving electron-deficient nitrogen and oxygen is that this rearrangement is typically SN1-like in character whereas the others are truly concerted. This reflects the ability of carbon to accept the positive charge in its transition state while the more electronegative elements show no such tolerance.

28.9 Pinacolic deamination. Conformational effects

Llike 1° aromatic amines,1° aliphatic amines react with nitrous acid to produce diazonium salts, but unlike their aromatic counterparts, are much more unstable and break down to form nitrogen and products suggestive of a carbonium mechanism:

RNH2 + NaNO2 + HX −→ RN2+ + X− −→ N2 + R+X−−→ products

If there is a hydroxyl group alpha to the amine group, a 1,2-shift with formation of a carbonyl as in the pinacol rearrangement is seen; this is a pinacolic deamination.

HONO

R2C(OH)C(NH2)R2 —−→ R2C(OH)C+R2 −→ RC(=+OH)CR3 −→ RCOCR3

The mechanism of this reaction permits a better study of the pinacol rearrangement, for the reason that the carbon which will become the carbonium intermediate is certain: it is the one holding the amino group. This is important when studying certain conformational effects of the rearrangement.

Optically active 2-amino-1,1-diphenyl-1-propanol (the chiral carbon is at C2) is treated with HONO to produce 1,2-diphenyl-1-propanone, which has an inverted configuration, and has a lower optical purity (77% inversion, 23% racemization which is equivalent to 88.5% inversion plus 11.5% retention). The stereochemistry of the reaction is typical of an SN 1 reaction with an open carbonium intermediate. C. Collins did the reaction, but with one of the phenyl groups stereospecifically labeled with 14C (the carbon at C1 becomes chiral as well now). Once Collins performed the reaction, the products were degraded to determine the label.

insert projections on p 899

The phenyl group is the migrating group for the reaction, but when the labeled phenyl group is the migratory group, the configuration at C2 is inverted, whereas if the unlabeled phenyl group migrates, the configuation at C2 is retained. The reaction has been interpreted in terms of conformational differences. First, using Newman projections, the most stable conformer of the formed diazonium is drawn, which is a staggered arrangement with the hydrogen atom in between the phenyl groups—clearly the most stable (see diagram). The position of the labeled phenyl group in Collins′ experiment must be anti to the diazonium group.

insert all of Fig. 28.1 here

As the N2 group leaves to form the carbonium, the labeled phenyl group is already in the plane of the empty p orbital of the carbonium, which is ideal for instant migration to give the inverted configuration. In fact, a concerted push from the labeled phenyl group (in SN2-like manner almost certainly accounts for part of the reaction. However, a true open carbonium is in fact formed since the unlabeled phenyl also migrates: this requires a 60° counterclockwise rotation to position the unlabeled phenyl on the opposite side before the migration, which gives a product with retained configruation.

It is clear that the open carbonium intermediate is not very long-lived to allow for thorough C1-C2 bond rotation, otherwise complete racemization is the outcome. The reaction thus demonstrates how conformation plays a role in the product formation: (a) how conformation at the beginning of the reaction determines product formation, and (b) what product can be formed from an ion where conformational rearrangements and bond rotation are constrained. As for (b), the 60° counterclockwise rotation was constrained only by eclipsing of the -OH and -CH3 groups, whereas the clockwise rotation of 120° would have eclipsed the labeled phenyl with -CH3. A 180° bond rotation of the carbonium ion to put the labeled phenyl group in a position to attack from the opposite side—with labeled phenyl migration to form a retained configuration (a product not observed)—would have effectively put two bulky groups (−CH3 and unlabeled phenyl) next to each other, and two relatively less bulky groups (−H and −OH) next to each other: a very unlikely state (such a bond rotation is not required if the initial diazonium is already in a conformation to form that, but that conformation is less stable).

2-amino-1-anisyl-1-phenyl-1-propanol is just like Collins′ reactant except one of the phenyl compounds is an anisyl (p-methoxyphenyl) group instead of labeled and unlabeled phenyl. D. Y. Curtin. The C1 and C2 are both chiral, and Curtin prepared their diastereomers for reaction (see diagram)

insert structures and reaction on p 902.

Diastereomer I and its enantiomer formed products in which anisyl migrated 94% of the reaction, the pheny group 6%. Diasteromer II and its enantiomer had the phenyl group migrating 88% of the reaction, 12% with anisyl migration. The anisyl is relatively more electron-releasing than phenyl, and stabilizes carbonium ions during EAS, but in the reaction with diastereomer II, these electronic effects count for little as phenyl migration occurs in greater proportion.

The most reasonable interpretation of Curtin′s work again is the relative population of conformers of the diazonium parent (see diagram). The groups already in a position and which have any migratory aptitude will be involved in the migration. The anisyl and phenyl groups have comparable bulk and the most stable parent diazonium conformer will reflect this fact.

insert Fig 28.2 here

It should be observed that the percentage of phenyl migration (88%) is quantitatively identical to the results of Collins′ work: this should be so, as it reflects the competition between phenyl migration and rotation about the carbon-carbon in which the steric groups are the same. [The book does not note this, but slight difference of 6% between 94% anisyl migration in one diastereomer and 88% phenyl migration shows how a slight electronic effect may be at play, but overall the steric forces and constraints on conformatioal isomers are significant.]

28.10 Neighboring group effects: stereochemistry

3-bromo-2-butanol treated with conc. HBr produced 2,3-dibromobutane. The reaction is a nucleophilic substitution by bromide ion (SN 1 or SN2). S. Winstein and H. J. Lucas were the ones who described the stereochemistry of this reaction, opening the door to the study of neighboring group effects. Using the racemic erythro-bromohydrin (see diagram) enantiomers, the product was only the meso-dibromobutane. When using the racemic threo-bromohydrin, the products were only racemic dibromobutane enantiomers.

insert Fig 28.3 here

The products show complete retention of configuration, quite unusual in substitution. Using optically active erythro-bromohydrin, they obtained optically inactive meso-dibromobutane; and using optically active threo -bromohydin, they obtained racemic diastereomers of the meso compound (see figure below).

insert Fig 28.4 here

Winstein and Lucas interpreted their results in light of what Roberts and Kimball had proposed earlier about Br2 addition to the C=C bond: formation of a cyclic bromonium ion intermediate. The mechanism must first involve protonation of the -OH group to make it a good leaving group, then a conformation in which the Br on the one carbon is anti to the leaving group −OH2+ is to be assumed. The Br atom will assist the leaving of the −OH2+ with a SN2-like push to begin to stabilize the developing carbonium ion, ultimately to form the cyclic bromonium intermediate. Attack by a Br− nucleophile will occur on the opposite face at either carbon, in which attack (also SN2) at one gives retention at both chiral centers, and attack at the other gives inversion of both chiral centers, accounting for the products.

insert mechanism middle of p 906

An truly open carbonium ion intermediate would have entailed the formation of diastereomers since rotation about the carbon-carbon bond would have occurred. The same process seen in rearrangements occurs here: intramolecular nucleophilic attack. And rearrangement does occur as well: in half the molecules, the bromine forming the cyclic bromonium will migrate from one carbon to the next.

28.11 Neighboring group effects: rate of reaction. Anchimeric assistance

Alkyl halides (RX) can undergo a hydrolysis, and mustard gas (β,β- dichloroethyl sulfide, ClCH2CH2−S−CH2CH2Cl) is not any different except that (a) the kinetics of hydrolysis is first order, with rate independent of the concentration of base, and (b) hydrolysis is faster than for ordinary 1° RCl. The kinetics suggests, of course, that rate is controlled by an SN1 mechanism, in which the molecule unimolecularly produces the carbonium, followed by rapid reaction with the solvent or nucleophile. However, primary alkyl halides hardly form carbonium ions except by brute force, normally reacting by SN2, and the sulfur atom may be sufficiently electron-withdrawing to destabilize any carbonium.

But in this case, the sulfur is sufficiently nucleophilic and in the right position for intramolecular nucleophilic substitution; this accounts for the faster rate of reaction. A cationic intermediate forms, but not an unstable 1° carbonium, rather a cyclic sulfonium in which every atom has the octet. Open-chain sulfonium ions (R3S+) are themselves reactive, but this cyclic sulfonium is even more reactive, with a nucleophilic H2O molecule breaking it up to the alcohol.

Additional evidence comes from solvolytic reactions with substituted cyclohexyl chlorides. In a mixture of water and ethanol, substitution of the 2° alkyl chloride to form the alcohol and the ethyl ether occurs, but the rate can be very much enhanced if there is a particular stabilizing group on the adjacent carbon of the cyclic molecule. The reaction of a 2° alkyl halide in substitution is plausibly SN1, with formation of 2° carboniums and high solvent polarity favorable conditions. If the group on the carbon next to the C-Cl is a phenylthio ether (C6H5S−) and is trans to the -Cl, then the rate is 70,000 times greater than if there were no group (just H−). If instead the group is cis to the -Cl group, the molecule reacts more slowly (0.16 times) than the unsubstituted cyclohexyl chloride. The trans relationship is easily explained, as the group is easily able to be in the anti conformation necessary for anchimeric-assisted intramolecular nucleophilic substitution. Notwithstanding the inability of the cis-position phenylthio ether to assume the anti position, the slowness of the reaction can be attributed to the electron- withdrawing inductive effect taking away the ability to form the carbonium ion more easily.

Solvolysis of the alkyl esters of sulfonic acids with acetic acid is a well- known reaction, producing the alkyl acetates. Acetate, although itself weak base, is nonetheless a much stronger base than the sulfonates, like tosylate (Ts) and brosylate (Br). Loss of the sulfonate ions leaves a carbonium which forms an ion pair with acetate, eventually to combine with the solvent.

R−OTs + HOAc/NaOAc —−→ R−OAc

2-acetoxycyclohexyl tosylate heated in acetic acid causes formation of the diacetate of 1,2-cyclohexanediol. The reactant can exist as diastereomers (in either the cis or trans relationship). Both the cis and trans tosylates yield the trans diacetate. With the cis reactant there is apparently an inverted configuration; with the trans reactant, a retained configuration. Two facts bring the reaction into view: (a) reaction of optically active trans acetate- tosylate isomer yields a racemic mixture of the trans diacetate enantiomers; (b) the trans tosylate reacts 800 times faster than the cis isomer.

insert stereochemical cyclic structures middle p 910

The results show that in the trans isomer, the neighboring acetoxy group, being in the suitable anti conformation, assists the leaving of the tosylate by back-side intramolecular attack to form the acetoxonium ion, a 5-membered cyclic structure dispersing the positive charge mostly around the carboxylate group. A nucleophilic HOAc can then attack at either carbon with equal probability to account for the racemic mixture of trans diacetates.

The cis-acetoxy group contributes no such neighboring group effect, not being able to assume a conformer suitable for back-side attack, hence the rate is slower than for trans.

In comparing the rates of reaction, it has already been explained that the trans isomer is 800 times faster than cis, but it is only one- third as fast as the unsubstituted tosylate.

Cyclohexyl > trans-2-Acetoxycyclohexyl > cis-2-Acetoxycyclohexyl

tosylatetosylate tosylate

1.00 > 0.30 > 0.00045

Despite anchimeric assistance which often makes the trans isomers react much faster than the unsubstituted, here the greater effect is electronic, with the inductive electron-withdrawing of the acetoxy substituent. Hence the potential for anchimeric assistance does not always translate into a faster rate of reaction. Although SN2-like, the attack by the acetoxy group has considerable SN1 character. Cyclic haloniums and protonated epoxides show considerable SN1 character as well in the ring-opening process; according to the microscopic reversibility principle, if ring-opening has SN1 character, so must ring-closing.

28.12 Neighboring group effects. Neighboring aryl

Solvolysis of 3-phenyl-2-butyl tosylate with HOAc forms the acetate ester, 3-phenyl-2-butyl acetate. The reactant and product have two chiral centers and can exist as two racemic modifications. When the racemic erythro tosyl- phenyl diasteromer is treated with HOAc, the racemic erythro acetoxy-phenyl diastereomer is obtained. With threo racemic enantiomers as reactants, the acetolytic products are the threo racemic acetoxy-phenyl enantiomers. Solvolysis is completely stereospecific with retention of configuration.

insert Fig 28.7 here

Use of optically active threo isomer, however, yields the racemic threo enantiomers. D. J. Cram (1949) has interpreted the results by saying that the pi electron cloud of the neighboring phenyl makes it a decent nucleophile, and is involved in back-side attack to push out the tosylate. A bridged-cyclic benzenonium intermediate forms, distributing the positive charge in the ring, an intermediate postulated in mechanisms discussed previously. The reaction finishes with an opposite-side attack by a HOAc nucleophile on either carbon to account for the products.

A problem with the mechanism was not so much the formation of the intermediate bridged ion−although this was questioned, too−but that, if the reaction receives anchimeric assistance from the phenyl group, the rate of acetolysis should be enhanced, as it was found that the unsubstituted sec-butyl tosylate undergoes solvolysis at the same rate as the 3-phenyl-2-butyl tosylates: formolysis a little faster, acetolysis somewhat slower.

A bridged benzenonium must be necessary to explain why other isomers are not formed as well, as would be with an open carbonium. As for reasons why the rates were not different, it was supposed that the rate-determining step is the leaving of tosylate, followed by a very rapid bridging before C-C bond rotation could occur. H. C. Brown suggested that the intermediate ion is not a bridged one, but open carbonium ions in rapid equilibration, with the phenyl group on one, then the other.

Some evidence of mechanism has now been presented. It was believed that 2° carboniums of their own accord with little assistance from the solvent, as for 3° carboniums. Using special secondary substrates with structures which prevent solvent assistance, P. Schleyer (1971) has shown that ordinary secondary substrates require a great deal of solvent assistance (see 14.17). Cram′s proposed mechanism appears correct then: the aryl group can give anchimeric assistance by formation of brigded ions. Competition is not between aryl-assisted solvolysis and unassisted solvolysis, but rather between aryl-assisted and solvent-assisted solvolysis. And anchimeric assistance does not need to proceed at an accelerated rate, where the bridged cation or open cation can form with aryl assistance or an equally strong solvent assistance.

If the neighboring aryl groups contain strongly electron-withdrawing substituents, the reaction products are normal, namely alkenes plus inverted ester, and the rate of solvolysis is the expected one for supposing open cation formation slowed by electron-withdrawing inductive effects. With use of increasingly electron-releasing subsituents (p-Cl, m-CH3, p- CH3, p-CH3O), the rate increases more than expected if only inductive effects were operating. For example, the rate data determinations for reaction of 3-aryl-2-butyl brosylates show that m-tolyl calculates to assist in 73% of the reaction, with 68% of the product having a retained configuration. For p-methyl, the calculation is 87%, found to be 88%. And for p-methoxyphenyl, calculated to be 99%, found to be 100%. The amount of anchimeric assistance given depends upon how nucleophilic the neighboring group is and on how badly the assistance is needed. The more nucleophilic the solvent, the more assistance it gives, with the neighboring group participating hardly at all. The stability of the carbonium ion is a factor, for if it forms readily (as for benzylic or 3° cations), little assistance is need by either solvent or neighboring group.

G. Olah (1970) has shown the existence of a bridged benzenonium ion intermediate using 13C-nmr spectroscopy in analysis the product of a reaction of b-phenylethyl chloride with SbF5 and SO2ClF.

28.13 Neighboring group effects: nonclassical ions

Meerwein (1922) first postulated carbonium ion rearrangements to explain why camphene hydrochloride is converted to isobornyl chloride.

insert reaction structures on top p 915 here; incorporate structures below

The reaction seems easy to explain. Chloride ion (Cl−) is lost to form a 2° cation, in which a 1,2-alkyl shift occurs to form another 2° cation, and then chloride reattaches to that cation. However the chloride attaches from only one side to form the exo chloride (isobornyl chloride) and not the endo chloride (bornyl chloride). Moreover, camphene undergoes solvolysis thousands of times faster than tert-butyl chloride.

Three findings were reported by Winstein (1949): (i) exo-norbornyl brosylate and its enantiomer, along with endo-norbornyl brosylate and its enantiomer, form upon acetolysis only the exo-norbornyl acetate and its enantiomer; (ii) optically active exo-norbornyl brosylate forms racemic exo-norbornyl acetate; (iii) the reaction of exo-norbornyl brosylate to acetolysis is 350 times faster than for the endo brosylate.

insert structures and reactions to p. 916 and second from top.

Winstein interpreted these results as the formation of a nonclassical bridged ion in which brosylate leaves and the carbonium is a hybrid of two structures in which the bond is between carbons C1-C6 (C2 with positive charge), C2-C6 (C1 positive), and C1-C2 (C6 positive). The bridging C6 is actually pentavalent. With the optically active exo brosylate, there is a back-side SN2-like attack by C6 on C2 to push out the brosylate (see diagram below). The endo brosylate geometry permits no such attack; rather, its rate depends upon the brosylate leaving in SN1-like manner to form the open cation followed by rapid conversion to the bridged ion. The nucleophile HOAc can attack either at C1 or C2, but because it must attack from the opposite face, only the exo acetoxy product is possible. The bridged ion intermediate must be supposed, otherwise an open cation would allow formation of the endo diastereomer as well.

insert mechanistic structures and reactions bottom and 2nd from bottom p. 916 and in fig 28.9.

The difference of this reaction with all others previously shown is that saturated carbon is a neighboring group using its σ electrons to give anchimeric assistance to expelling the leaving group, to form a bridged intermediate with a pentavalent carbon. Such ions with delocalized bonding s electons involved are nonclassical ions.

H. C. Brown (1962) has said that the mechanism was too readily accepted, and was not necessarily wrong, but not necessarily right. He offered alternative explanations including the rapidly equilibrating open carbonium ions as was postulated for the cyclic benzenonium ion. He suggested that substitution occurs on the exo face because the endo face lies in a fold and is screened from attack. He also suggested that rate differences between endo and exo brosylate may result from a steric hindrance experienced by the brosylate on the endo side. The 6000-fold higher ethanolysis of camphene HCl compared to tert-butyl chloride has been attributed to the anchimeric assistance-bridged ion intermediate model, but Brown maintains this is the wrong standard for comparison, showing that a number of (3°) cyclopentyl chlorides also react faster than tert-butyl chloride. The faster rate of reaction he has attributed to relief of steric strain, certainly present in the bicyclo camphene with its 5-membered bicyclo rings. On ionization, chloride ion is lost and the methyl group on the sp2-hybridized carbon moves into the plane of the ring: four non-bonded interactions disappear, two for chlorine and two for methyl.

Evidence of a bridging nonclassical ion is now in. G. Olah (1970) reported isolation of a stable norbornyl cation prepared in SbF5- SO2. The data from analysis by pmr, cmr, and Raman spectra suggest the existence of a nonclassical ion with delocalized s electrons. The norbonyl cation is fully bridged. Substituted norbonyl molecules show a different character: 2-methylnorbonyl cation forms some bridging with some s electron delocalization, and 2-phenylnorbonyl cation has no bridging, the open benzylic cation quite stable. (The bridging in the 2-methylnorbornyl cation appears to result not from the C6-C7 bond, but rather the C6-H bond; Olah suggests the back lobe of the C-H bond overlaps the p orbital of C2.)

insert structures on p 918

Chap. 29 Molecular orbitals. Orbital symmetry

29.1 MO theory

MOs are orbitals not centered about the various nuclei, but orbitals centered about all nuclei in the molecule. Quantum mechanical (QM) calculations will not be made since they are enormously complicated, but the results of calculations will be addressed. The concepts of phase and orbitals which are either bonding or antibonding are discussed. Moreover, the importance of orbital symmetry is especially important in seeing why organic reactions occur.

29.2 Wave equations. Phase

Standing waves or stationary waves a vibrating waves with an unchanging waveform and may represent a natural property of the energy in a piece of matter: plucking a guitar string provides a certain wave motion of a particular frequency and no other. The waveform has a certain amplitude (φ), representing the maximum displacement either negative or positive. Where the amplitude of the wave has zero value is the node, and the plane of symmetry at the nodes are called the nodal plane. The upward and downward displacements of the waves correspond to opposite phases of the wave. Superimposition of the waveform with another of the same frequency but aligned such that the sum of the waves cancel each other at all points to zero value, are out of phase waveforms.

The differential equation describing the wave is called a wave equation. Solutions of this equation give the amplitude, φ, as a function, f(x), of the distance along the wave, x. This function is a wave function. Electron waves are described by a wave equation, and the wave functions which are acceptable solutions of this equation give the amplitude, φ, a function not of a single coordinate but of three coordinates to describe motion in three dimensions. These electron wave functions are orbitals.

Electron waves, like string waves, can have nodes. The p orbital has a nodal plane between its two lobes, in which the lobes are of opposite phase, being + or −, and not referring to any charge properties of the orbital. The amplitude or wave function, φ, is the orbital. However, it is the square of the amplitude, φ2, which has physical meaning, being the probability of finding an electron at any particular place. The geometric figures used to describe the physical presence of orbitals typically reflects the place where an electron can be found 95% of the time.

29.3 MOs. LCAO method

In forming bonds, electrons of opposite spin will pair and are attracted by both nuclei in the bond, making electrostatic attraction between nuclei a significant factor in bond-making. The qualitative model used in QM to describe MOs is the method of linear combination of atomic orbitals (LCAO). The MO is the result of a mathematical operation on atomic orbitals.

ψ = φA + φB

where ψ is the MO, φA is atomic orbital A, and φB is atomic orbital B. When the electron is near atom A, ψ resembles φA, when near atom B, ψ resembles φB.

The combination is considered effective, namely the MO is more stable than the AOs, only if AOs φA and φB:

(a) overlap to a considerable extent;

(b) are of comparable energy;

(c) have the same symmetry about the bond axis.

If there is no considerable overlap, the energy of ψ is equal to that of φA or that of φB; if the energy of φA and φB are quite different, the energy of ψ is essentially that of the more stable AO. Insofar as symmetry, the relative phases of any lobes and their disposition in space is of consequence. Hydrogen fluoride is a useful model (see diagram).

insert Fig 29.3 here

The bond can result from overlap of the hydrogen s orbital with the fluorine p orbital. Three p orbitals are available, and only one has the proper orientation for the greatest overlap. The other two (one of them diagrammed on the right) also overlap both lobes, but the mathematical representation of the + and − lobes predict the cancelling out of the bond overlap, and therefore no bonding is likely. This dependence of overlap on phase is critical to predicting chemical bonding.

29.4 Bonding and antibonding orbitals

The LCAO method actually calls for two wave equations describing two MOs: one a bonding MO and the other an antibonding MO:

ψ+ = φA + φB bonding orbital: stabilizes molecule

ψ− = φA − φB antibonding orbital: destabilizes molecule

The addition of two s orbitals to form a s bonding orbital can be done, or likewise the substraction can be done to depict the s* antibonding orbital.

insert figures p 929

The need for one MO for every AO (conservation of orbital number) can be seen easily, since one AO can bring two electrons, two bring four, and only two electrons are allowed in an MO as for an AO. The different combinations of s and p orbitals to form variously either s or p bonding and antibonding combinations is depicted. The phase relationships are also shown.

insert Fig 29.4 on p 930 here

In bonding MOs, the orbital region is between nuclei (where bonding is expected) so that electrons can be attracted by both nuclei; the increase in electrostatic attraction lowers the energy of the system. In antibonding MOs, electron density is away from the area in between nuclei, resulting in nuclei seeing each other, creating a charge repulsive effect, and leading to bond breaking. An additional MO is created in the scheme in which electrons are neither bonding or antibonding, but rather nonbonding. They do not contribute to bond strengthening or weaking in the ground state.

29.5 Electronic configurations of some molecules

MOs can be mathematically mapped out from their AO equations, then the number of available electrons assigned to them. The assignment of electrons to MOs, as for AOs, follows the Pauli exclusion principle and Hund′s rule.

Considering the p bonding orbitals of ethylene, the two p AOs of the carbons can be mathematically combined to form a two MOs, one p bonding MO and one p antibonding MO, which has a higher relative energy for reasons given in the last section. Each carbon contributes one p electron, and in this case, both are paired with opposite spins in the p bonding MO, if the molecule has the lowest energy state, the ground state. The designation is π 2.

The jump of one electron to the p antibonding MO has a certain energy which corresponds to a certain wavelength of the EM spectrum (E=hc /λ, normally in the UV), and a jump corresponds to an excited state. The electron is the antibonding MO, still of opposite spin, and the designation is now ππ*, indicating one electron in the bonding and one in the antibonding MO.

insert fig 29.5

With 1,3-butadiene, four p AO from each carbon mean four MOs for the p electrons. The ground state is ψ12ψ22, with y2 representing a somewhat higher energy state for isolated π orbitals, or rather ψ1 representing a delocalization of the entire conjugated system and a lower energy state. The effect of light absorption to raise the configuration to ψ12ψ2ψ3 in the first excited state is depicted.

insert Fig 29.6 on p. 932

The allyl system, its cation, free radical, and anion are also depicted. Three component p AOs are brought by the three carbons to give rise to ψ1, ψ2, and ψ3 MOs. ψ1 is considered bonding, and ψ3 is antibonding. ψ2 MO involves the end carbons, a node at the middle carbon and has the same energy as an isolated p orbital: it is considered nonbonding. The electron assignments put all electrons in either the bonding MO or nonbonding MO for whatever form the allyl system is in: cation, free radical, or anion. The important thing here is that the bonding ψ1 MO encompasses all three carbons and is therefore more stable than one encompassing only two. It is what gives the allyl system its special stability.

insert fig 29.7 on p 933

As for benzene, it brings 6 component p AOs which means 6 p MOs. The three most stable are all bonding MOs, and they get all of the available electrons. The LCAO method gives one of the bonding MOs, ψ1, the lowest relative energy, with distribution of the electron pair around all 6 carbons, where ψ2 and ψ3 MO have equal energy and distribute over the molecule as well.

insert figures bottom p 933 and figure 29.8 on p 934

29.6 Aromatic character. The Hückel 4n + 2 rule

The low energy of a molecule presented by its ability to delocalize charge or electron density is an important factor explaining stability of the molecule. But delocalization is not enough to explain why certain molecules having a conjugated pi system with a certain number (2, 6, 10, etc.) of pi electrons obtain a greater stability. Hückel proposed the 4n + 2 p electron rule in 1931 after extensive study in using LCAO.

The cyclopentanedienyl system is a useful model. It has five sp2-hybridized carbons with five component p AOs, producing five MOs. The lowest energy MO is one encompassing all five carbons. Two degenerate orbital pairs are higher in energy, but still bonding MOs, and the other two with still higher energy are antibonding MOs. The cation has four electrons: two are paired in the lowest bonding MO; the other two distributed into the degenerate bonding MOs of higher relative energy. With the free radical, an additional electron is paired with either one of the electrons in the degenerate bonding MOs. But with the anion, all three bonding orbitals are filled. This state represents maximum bonding and maximum stability. The configuration of cyclopentadienyl anion, benzene, and cycloheptatrienyl cation are entirely the same, and this accounts for the aromaticity of the molecules.

insert figure 29.9 and combine with elements of figure 29.10 as well.

The same depiction for a 2 π-electron system can be drawn. Cyclopropenyl cation (aromatic with its two π electrons) bring 3 component p AOs, therefore 3 MOs, two of which are degenerate antibonding, and one bonding MO. The free radical and anion forms are forced to put electrons into antibonding MOs, an unstable state.

insert Fig 29.11 on p 937

29.7 Orbital symmetry and the chemical reaction

Bonding results from maximal overlap of orbitals, and that orbitals be of the same phase. Chemical reactions proceed more easily when the energy barrier is not too high. To a large extent, the occurrence of reactions is controlled by orbital symmetry. Of the people contributing to the development of this concept are K. Fukui of Japan and H. C. Longuet-Higgins of England, and in 1965 in a series of papers published by R. B. Woodward and R. Hoffmann.

Orbital symmetry effects are observed in concerted reactions, reactions in which several bonds are made or broken simultaneously. Woodward and Hoffmann formulated what are called symmetry-allowed and symmetry- forbidden rules, which apply only to concerted-type reactions. A symmetry-forbidden reaction is one in which the concerted mechanism is difficult, or if it occurs at all, it will be by a concerted mechanism which is symmetry-allowed or which is a nonconcerted step-wise mechanism.

29.8 Electrocyclic reactions

Conjugated polyenes are known to undergo isomerization reactions under the influence of heat or light which involve cyclization and decyclization. 1,3,5-hexatriene can form 1,3-cyclohexadiene: in the cyclization, one double bond disappears and the double bonds shift their positions to form a still conjugate system. Cyclobutene can be opened to form 1,3-butadiene. These reactions are classified electrocyclic reactions. When substituted molecules are used, the stereochemistry is of particular interest.

3,4-Dimethylcyclobutene can be interconverted to 2,4-hexadiene; the cyclobutene can exist as cis or trans, whereas the hexadiene can exist as cis,trans; cis,cis; or trans,trans. The cis cyclobutene produces only one of the three (the cis,trans hexadiene) and the trans cyclobutene a different isomer (the trans,trans hexadiene). These reactions are therefore completely stereospecific. Moreover, photochemical cyclization of the cis,trans diene produces a different cyclobutene than the one from which the diene is formed by the thermal (heat-promoted) ring opening.

insert fig 29.12 but draw cyclobutenes in 3-D fashion

Interconversions of the dimethylcyclohexadienes and the 2,4,6-octatrienes are also stereospecific, with the thermal and photochemical reactions producing a different stereochemistry (see diagram below). In addition the triene- cyclohexadiene interconversions are opposite to that of the diene- cyclobutene interconversions. For the thermal reactions, the cis methyl groups in the cyclobutene become cis and trans in the diene, whereas the cis methyl groups in the cyclohexadiene are trans and trans in the related triene.

insert entirety of fig 29.13 here

The exact stereochemistry of electrocyclic reactions depends upon (a) the number of double bonds in the polyene and (b) whether the reaction is photochemical or thermal.

Cyclization of the open chain depends upon two π electrons of the polyene forming the new σ bond of the cycloalkene. Which two electrons are used depends upon the highest occupied molecule orbital (HOMO) of the polyene. Electrons in this orbital may be considered to the valence electrons of the molecule, inasmuch valence electrons are available in bonding in an atom; they are less tightly held and more easily manipulated in a reaction.

A disubstituted butadiene, RCH=CH—CH=CHR, has as its HOMO the ψ2 MO (see 29.5) because it is a conjugated diene; its ground state is depicted below. These electrons do not require a significant energy push to bring them to the excited state for bond breaking and making. Bond formation requires p orbital overlap of carbons C1 and C4 of the diene. In order to bring the lobes into proper position for s bonding, bond rotation about the C1-C2 and the C3-C4 bonds must occur. Bond rotation of two bonds in the same direction may be said to be conrotatory, whereas bond rotation of the two bonding in opposite directions is disrotatory.

insert figures at bottom of p 941, and then figure 29.14 on p 942 and 29.15 on p 943

For the conrotatory motion, lobes of the same phase would be brought together in the overlap, and bonds are formed. Disrotatory motion bring lobes of opposite phase together, and antibonding properties obtain. When this motion is considered specifically for the cis,trans- and trans,trans-hexadienes to form the cyclobutenes, the predictions are identical with the actual products.

Why does photochemical cyclization produce the opposite effect? Recalling that butadiene in the excited state causes an electronic transition from ψ2 to ψ3, in which ψ3 is an antibonding MO. Since there is an electron in ψ3, it is now the HOMO, and the orbital symmetry is different. Indeed the orbital symmetry of the terminal carbons is opposite to that of the ground state butadiene, when ψ2 is the HOMO. Hence, a disrotatory motion will bring together lobes of the same phase, with reversed stereochemistry.

insert fig 29.16 on p 943 along with figures on top of p 945.

With thermal cyclization of a disubstituted hexatriene, RCH=CH—CH=CH—CH=CHR, the HOMO for the ground state is ψ3. The orbital symmetry and electronic configuration and relative energy levels is diagrammed just below.

insert fig 29.17 here

The terminal carbons in this case are have their lobes positioned such that disrotatory motion in the heat-induced cyclization causes overlap of the bonding phases, and therefore the trans,cis,trans-octatriene forms the cis- cyclohexadiene, where as the trans,cis,cis-octatriene forms the trans- cyclohexadiene. This is compared to the conrotatory motion of the hexadiene in forming the cyclobutadiene.

Insert fig 29.18 here

In the excited state, as induced by a photochemical process, the y4 orbital is the HOMO, the terminal carbons of the polyene have different symmetry, and a conrotatory motion is necessitated in cyclization.

The Woodward-Hoffmann rules for the electrocyclic reactions are summarized in the table. As the number of pairs of p electrons in the polyene increases, the relative symmetry about the terminal carbons in the HOMO alternates regularly. Also symmetry in the HOMO of the first excited state is always opposite to that in the ground state.

Woodward-Hoffmann Rules for Electrocyclic Reaction

 

 Number of p electrons Reaction Motion

 

 4n thermal conrotatory

 

 

 4n photochemical disrotatory

 

 

      

 

 

4n+2 thermal disrotatory

 

 

4n+2 photochemical conrotatory

 

29.9 Cycloaddition reactions

In the Diels-Alder reaction, a diene reacted with a dienophile to form a cyclohexene, described as a [4+2] cycloaddition since one reactant required 4 p electrons, the other 2 p electrons. The reaction can take place easily, often spontaneously with only a little heat required.

In the reaction the diene was presented to the dienophile with the s- cis conformation, in which the single bond is rotated so that both double bonds are presenting the terminal carbons to the dienophile. The reaction was also shown in such a way that a concerted, syn-addition to the dienophile was occurring, with the terminal carbons of the conjugated diene forming new single bonds in which one of the pi bonds from the diene contributed one bond, and the pi bond in the dienophile contributing the other.

Another fact is that the reaction occurs with endo addition in preference to exo addition. This refers to the position of other unsaturated groups which may be present in the dienophile (for example −CO−O−CO−) preferring to lie nearer the developing double bond in the diene (see figure immediately below).

insert fig. 29.19 on p 949

[2+2] cycloadditions can be obtained, such as two molecules of ethylene forming cyclobutane. However, the reaction is via diradicals and not under thermal conditions producing the concerted mechanism. Thus photochemically- induced [2+2] cycloadditions are common. On the other hand, photochemically induced [4+2] cycloaddtions are difficult to obtain.

In cycloaddition, two new s bonds are to be formed by using p electrons of the reactants. The electrons in the HOMO of one molecule will have to overlap with the HOMO of the other, but since the HOMO of the other already has two electrons, it becomes necessary to find an empty orbital, and the most stable empty orbital, viz., the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO). In the transition state, stabilization is a result of overlap between the HOMO of one reactant and the LUMO of the other.

insert Fig 29.20 on p 950 here

The electronic configuration and orbital symmetry states for alkenes and dienes has been shown in a previous section. Two combinations are possible: either the HOMO of butadiene (ψ2) with the LUMO of ethylene (π*), or the HOMO of ethylene (p) and the LUMO of butadiene (ψ3). In either case, overlap of orbitals of the same phase occurs, and so does bonding (illustrated above). It should not be forgotten that jumps to the LUMO are photochemically necessary.

If thermal [2+2] cycloaddition of ethylene were desired, the picture of orbital overlap of the HOMO, π, of one ethylene would not show the proper phase overlap with the LUMO, π*, another ethylene. The interaction is antibonding and the concerted reaction can not occur. The photochemical [2+2] cycloaddition is symmetry-allowed, since the HOMO of the excited state (π*) does show overlap with the LUMO of the ground state (π*) of another molecule, and the interaction results in bonding.

insert figs 29.21 and 29.22 here

Special terms apply for the bond-making and -breaking on one face of a molecule in concerted reactions; this same face reaction is called suprafacial. If bonds are being broken and formed with the two carbons, but at opposite faces, this type of reaction is antarafacial.

insert figure at bottom p 951

Most reactions discussed up to now have been concerned with suprafacial-type reactions. In the thermal [2+2] cycloaddition reaction, although symmetry forbids a supra,supra concerted reaction, symmetry should allow a supra,antara reaction, although the geometric result clearly makes such a reaction impossible.

insert fig 29.23 here

However, where rings are big enough, the supra,supra and supra,antara processes can occur, leaving orbital symmetry to control how the reaction occurs. The Woodward-Hoffman rules are extended. These cycloadditions are also reversible (cycloreversions) and must follow the same symmetry rules as for cycloadditions.

Woodward-Hoffmann Rules for [i + j] Cycloaddition

 

i + j

Thermal

Photochemical

 

 

4n supra-antara supra-supra

 

 

  antara-supra antara-antara

 

 

     

 

 

4n+2 supra-supra supra-antara

 

 

 

antara-antara

antara-supra

 

29.10 Sigmatropic reactions

A concerted reaction which involves migration of a group on the framework simultaneously with bond (usu. p bond) shifting or rearrangement is called a sigmatropic reaction.

insert figure of 1,3 and 1,5 and 3,3 sigmatropic reaction on p 954

The reactions can be designated to show the distance the group is traveling on the framework, from the carbon it is at (carbon #1) to a carbon five atoms down (carbon #5), which is [1,5]. The Cope rearrangement of 1,5- hexadienes, the reaction is a [3,3] sigmatropic reaction, where the group G is the π framework itself.

In the transition state of the reaction, there must be an overlap of one HOMO on one component and the HOMO of the other. In the allylic radical, the migrating group is passed from one end of the radical to the other, so the end carbons become signficant. It can be seen that the orbital symmetry about the carbons in the framework alternates from C1 to C3 to C5, etc. In the simplest case involving the migration of hydrogen, the sigmatropic shift can be suprafacial or antarafacial (see below).

insert all figs on p 955 here

The transition state requires a 3-center bond between the hydrogen s orbital and the lobes of the p orbitals of the terminal carbons. Whether a suprafacial or antarafacial shift occurs depends upon orbital symmetry. In particular, antarafacial shifting is difficult for small molecules because of the geometrical constraints on such twist; such twisting goes against the need to make all atoms coplanar during charge delocalization in the reaction. For this reason, [1,3] and [1,5] sigmatropic shifts are generally supra shifts. Hence hydrogen migration is only going to occur with [1,5] shifts and not with [1,3] shifts.

insert figs top and middle of p. 956

Experience proves the theory here: [1,3] hydrogen sigmatropic shifts do not occur, and the thermally-induced [1,5] hydrogen shifting does occur with CH3CH=CH—CH=CD2, in which CH2=CH—CH=CH—CHD2 is isolated. With photochemically-induced reactions, it is the reverse: [1,3] hydrogen shifting occurs, but not [1,5].

When 3-deuteurioindene (I) is heated, it is known to cause a scrambling in which the label can be found at all three non-aromatic position (fig below)

insert fig top of p 957

Formation of structure III can be accounted for by a [1,3] shift of H. A [1,3] shift of D would not do anything but regenerate structure I.

insert fig middle p 957

If the p orbitals of the benzene ring were included in the shifting, a [1,5] shift of D can be obtained, placing at the right position, yielding an unstable nonaromatic structure. A [1,5] shift of hydrogen yields the stable aromatic product and structure II is accounted for.

insert fig bottom p 957 and show additionally the [1,3] H shift

If carbon is the migrating group, stereochemistry comes into play. Shifts occurring in which the same lobe of carbon which was bonded to one part of the frame is used to bond the other means the configuration is retained. If however, the opposite lobe (and therefore opposite face) is used, inversion of configuration occurs as in an SN2 reaction. For [1,3] and [1,5] carbon shifts, geometry prevents antarafacial movements, which means suprafacial [1,3] migrations demand inversion and [1,5] shifts demand retention.

insert figs middle and bottom p 958

J. Benson (1968) has reported that deuterium-labeled bicyclo[3.2.0]heptene is converted stereospecifically to exo-norbornene. The reaction is clearly [1,3] and proceeds with complete inversion.

insert reaction on top p. 959 and fig 29.25

H. Kloosterziel (1970) has reported on the rearrangements of diastereomeric 6,9-dimethylspiro[4.4]nona-1,3-dienes (cis-I and trans I) to the dimethylbicylco[4.3.0]nonadienes II, III, and IV. All reactions are stereospecific, with [1,5] sigmatropic shifting and with complete retention of the configuration of the migrating carbon.

insert fig bottom p 959 and fig. 29.26 on top of p 960

Chap. 30 Polynuclear aromatic compounds

30.1 Fused-ring aromatic compounds

Aromatic rings sharing a pair of carbons are said to be fused. The chemistry of the simplest fused-ring hydrocarbon, naphthalene, and two others, anthracene and phenanthrene are treated. All three come from coal tar, with naphthalene making up 5% of coal tar itself.

insert structures of naphthalene, anthracene, and phenanthrene

insert table 30.1 on p 967

The diamond is the ultimate polycyclic aliphatic system, but another allotropic form of elemental carbon, graphite, is a polycyclic fused ring aromatic system. X-ray analysis shows carbons arranged in layers, with each layer a continuous network of planar hexagonal rings. The bonding in all carbons is strong, the 1.42 A bond length only slightly longer than in benzene (1.39 A). The layers are 3.4 A apart, being held togther by weak forces, and probably accounting for why graphite has a greasy feel and comes apart.

30.2 Nomenclature of naphthalene derivatives

Naming monosubstituted naphthalenes is comparatively easy, since only two isomeric forms are possible. The prefixes of either 1- or 2-, or a- or b-, are used interchangably.

insert structure of naphthalene for naming and some other structures on pp968-9

30.3 Structure of napthalene

Naphthaline is aromatic as is benzene, meaning it is not subject to simple addition reactions but rather EAS to preserve the ring structure. All carbons are sp2-hybridized, coplanar (for that reason) and an aromatic sextet of p electrons exist in each ring, with both rings sharing a common pair. The resonance stabilization shown by combustion of naphthalene has been determined to be 61 kcal/mol.

X-ray analysis has shown that all C-C bonds are not equivalent. The C1-C2 bond is shorter (1.365 A) than the C2-C3 bond (1.404 A). There are three possible resonance structures making up the hybrid of naphthalene, and when these 3 structures are drawn, the difference in bond lengths is explained. In two of the structures, the C1-C2 bond is a double bond (known to be shorter in length than a single bond), in the third structure a single bond; the C2-C3 bond is a double bond in only one resonance structure, a single bond in the other structures. Hence, C1-C2 bonds have more double bond character than C2-C3 bonds. (Although two rings with circles in them are drawn for naphthalene structures suggesting some equivalence of all carbons in the molecule, the intent is to show the aromaticity of the molecule and has no other purpose.)

30.4 Reactions of napthalene

Naphthalene is usually obtained as purified from its natural sources, and is rarely prepared in the laboratory.

Like benzene, naphthalene undergoes EAS reactions rather than additions in order to preserve the highly stabilized ring structure.

1. Oxidation
