30.5 Oxidation of napthalene

Naphthalene undergoes oxidation and reduction actions more easily than benzene. Benzene′s resonance stabilization is 36 kcal/mol. The 61 kcal/mol stabilization of naphthalene shows that only 25 kcal/mol is sacrificed by destroying the ring. Oxidation usually stops though with the extra ring, leaving substituted benzenes.

In the presence of O2 gas and V2O5 catalyst and high temperature (500°), phthalic anhydride can be obtained. This reaction is important since coal tar contains much naphthalene and phthalic anhydride is in high demand.

Preparing the carboxylic acid derivatives of naphthalene such as by the oxidation of 2-methylnapthalene to its acid is not as simple as adding the oxidizing reagents. In CrO3 and HOAc at room temperature, naphthalenes oxidize the second ring to form quinones (for naphthalene, 1,4-naphthoquinone in 40% yield; 2-methyl-1,4-naphthoquinone in the case of the 2-methylnaphthalene, 70% yield). Oxidation of methyl side chains is a problem.

30.6 Reduction of napthalene

Chemical reducing agents can reduce naphthalene unlike benzene, which is unreactive. One double bond in naphthalene is reduced when treated with sodium metal in ethanol at 78°, the product 1,4-dihydronaphthalene. The second double bond in the ring is reduced to 1,2,3,4-tetrahydronaphthalene (also called tetralin) with Na metal in isopentyl alcohol at 132°. Further reduction of tetralin to decalin (C6H 18) involves reduction of the benzene ring which remains after chemical reduction, and this certainly requires catalytic hydrogenation under very vigorous conditions.

30.7 Dehydrogenation of hydroaromatic compounds. Aromatization

Compounds like 1,4-dihydronaphthalene, tetralin, and decalin have the right carbon skeleton for forming aromatic rings, but have too many hydrogens. These compounds are classed as hydroaromatic compounds. Rather than reduction of aromatic ring systems, a preferred process is to dehydrogenate these saturated rings to produce the aromatic systems, the process called aromatization.

Typically the catalysts used in hydrogenation are used in dehydrogenation as well. The hydrogenation-dehydrogenation process is certainly reversible, the catalyst being used only to lower the energy barrier involved in the reaction. Since the reaction is reversible, an equilibrium obtains. The equilibrium is shifted either by decreasing or increasing concentration of one factor in the reaction. Hydrogenation is promoted with high pressures of H2 gas; dehydrogenation by sweeping away formed H2 with an inert gas like CO2. In one elegant modification of the H2 removal process, 1-(a-naphthyl)-cyclohexene is aromatized to 1-phenylnaphthalene by incubating in the presence of chloranil (tetrachlorobenzoquinone), which readily accepts H2 to form tetrachlorohydroquinone.

insert 2nd reaction on p 975

The stabilization resulting from aromatization is often strong enough to force the removal of certain groups other than hydrogen. Abietic acid (a substance in rosin) aromatizes to form 1-methyl-7-isopropylphenanthrene by eliminating a methyl group at the point of ring fusion (the so-called angular methyl group).

insert reaction bottom p. 975

Aromatization is also done in the presence of selenium, sulfurm and organic disulfides (RSSR), with hydrogen removed to form hydrogen selenide, hydrogen sulfide, and thiols. Many polynuclear aromatics are synthesized from their open-chain forms first by ring closure, then by aromatization. Naturally occurring hydroaromatic compounds are analyzed by aromatization and the aromatized product compared to the tables. This reveals structures (e.g. cholersterol aromatized by Se and heat to Diels′ hydrocarbon: 3′- methyl-1,2-cyclopentenophenanthrene).

30.8 Nitration and halogenation of naphthalene

Nitration and halogenation occur exclusively in the 1- or a-position. Chlorination and bromination occur so readily that a Lewis acid catalyst is unnecessary. Other groups are prepared at the 1-position starting from the 1- nitronaphthalene: reduction to the amine, oxidiation to diazonium, then either introduction of the halide, nitrile, or reaction with aromatics to form azo compounds. With the 1-halonaphthalene, synthesis of the Grignard leads to formation of alcohols, ketones, etc.

30.9 Orientation of electrophilic substitution in naphthalene

Why the EAS reactions occur at the a-position can be ascertained if it is remembered that EAS involves formation of a carbonium ion intermediate, and formation of the most stable carbonium intermediate. In nitration, the nitronium NO2+ is the electrophile. If nitration is at the alpha position, structures of the carbonium ion intermediate can be drawn and some conclusion about their stabilities made. All possible resonance structures should be drawn. In the figure, the possible resonance structures for attack at both alpha and beta positions is shown. Two of the alpha-attack resonance structure are relatively stable since the aromaticity of the other ring is preserved in their intermediate; for beta attack, only one resonance structure shows such ability.

insert resonance structure drawings on p 978

Because of this difference in the number of stable resonance structures forming the hybrid, the alpha attack is considered to be preferable. This rule about the number of stabilizing structure contributing to the transition state or a true intermediate holds in considerations of the orientation of a chemical reaction.

30.10 Friedel-Crafts acylation of naphthalene

Depending upon the solvent used, naphthalene can be acetylated with acetyl chloride in presence of AlCl3; if CS2 or something like tetrachloroethane is the solvent, acetylation is predominantly at the alpha position; if nitrobenzene is the solvent, substitution is beta. The effect of nitrobenzene is attributed to its forming a complex with the acid chloride and AlCl3 which attacks the roomier beta position.

This acetylation (and sulfonation in the next section) allows access to beta derivatives of naphthalene. For example treatment of 2-acetonaphthalene (methyl b-naphthyl ketone) with hypohalite (NaOCl, 60-70°) is the best route to b- naphthoic acid (88% yield; chloroform is another product).

Naphthalene can also be treated with succinic anhydride (in AlCl3 and nitrobenzene) and this attaches to both the alpha and beta position to form 4-(1-naphthyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid [b-(1-naphthoyl)propionic acid] and 4-(2-naphthyl)-4-oxobutanoic acid [b-(2-naphthoyl)propionic acid], resp.

F-C alkylations have little success: the high reactivity of naphthalene causes side reactions and polyalkylations. Alkylnaphthalenes are obtained typically by acylation and ring closure.

30.11 Sulfonation of naphthalene

Naphthalene can be sulfonated using concentrated H2SO4. At 80°, mostly the alpha substituted 1- naphthalenesulfonic acid is formed. When the temperature is raised to 160°, it is converted to the 2-isomer. Even after the sulfonation has occurred at the 1-isomer, raising the temperature to 160° converts it to the 2-isomer, showing that an equilibrium exists. The reversibility of sulfonation reactions has been discussed previously (Sec. 11.12).

The reason for this is rate vs. equilibrium. Since sulfonation at the a- position involves formation of a more stable intermediate carbonium ion, its formation is more rapid. However, attack by H+ at the sulfonated position is also rapid, therefore desulfonation is as rapid as sulfonation. Sulfonation at the b-position is slower, too, but so is desulfonation. This rate vs. equilibrium has been shown with 1,2- and 1,4-addition to conjugated dienes (Sec 8.22), Friedel-Crafts alkylation of toluene (12.11), and sulfonation of phenols (see prob. 24.13, p. 803).

30.12 Naphthols

As with phenols, naphthols can be made from corresponding sulfonic acids with alkali. They can also be made from naphthylamines in very high yields by direct hydrolysis under acidic conditions, a reaction which does not work in the benzene series but is superior to hydrolysis of diazonium salts.

insert both reactions on bottom p 981

a-Substituted naphthalenes are often prepared by a sequence of reactions which lead back to the nitro compound. Preparation of b-substituted naphthalenes cannot start with nitro compounds since nitration is not possible as the beta position. For all b-substituted compounds, it is better to sulfonate to make 2-naphthalenesulfonic acid, then prepare the 2-naphthol. From the 2-naphthol, b-napthylamine is made by the Bucherer reaction by heating the naphthol under pressure with ammonia and ammonium sulfite, a reaction which is not useful with the benzene series except in rare cases. From the b-naphthylamine, the diazonium can be produced, which can be used to make halides, nitriles, azo compounds, etc.

insert series on p 982

30.13 Orientation of electrophilic substitution in naphthalene derivatives

Naphthalene undergoes substitution at chiefly the a-position, and sulfonation and F-C acylation at either the a- or b-position. To what position will a second group attach? Such orientation is difficult to assess, since 7 positions are possible, whereas only 3 are possible with benzene. The rules are as follows:

(a) An activating (electron-releasing) group tends to direct further substitution into the same ring. Activating groups at position 1 direct further substitution to position 4, and to a lesser extent to position 2. Activating groups at position 2 direct substitution to position 1.

(b) A deactivating (electron-withdrawing) group tends to direct further substitution into the other ring: at an a-position in nitration or halogenation, or at an a- or b-position (depending upon temperature) in sulfonation.

The reactions below provide examples:

insert all reactions on p 983

These rules do not always hold in sulfonation, because the reaction is reversible and at high temperatures tends to take place at a b-position.

The mechanism involved in directing orientation incorporate the same rules seen with attack of naphthalene. Relative stabilities of carbonium ion intermediates, resonance structures tending to preserve the aromatic sextet in one ring. In general, when the electrophile comes to the ring, it is that ring which will assume the positive charge, so electron-withdrawing groups will destabilize any attack of the ring to which they are attached; electron withdrawing groups will stabilize same-ring attack. In the case of electron-releasing groups at position 1, resonance structures can be drawn when substitution is at the 2- or 4- position which place the + charge on the carbon to which the electron-releasing group is attached, hence making it the more stable. This is true whether the activating group releases by induction (as with methyl) or resonance (as with hydroxyl). Electron-releasing groups at the 2-position direct strictly to the 1-position since its resonance structure is quite stable; substitution at the 3-position requires a resonance structure breaking up the aromatic sextet of the other ring.

insert structures on bottom p 984, and on p 985.

30.14 Synthesis of naphthalene derivatives by ring closure. The Haworth synthesis

Typically benzene derivatives are produced starting from benzene or one of its substitutes. One rarely ever attempts to follow a synthetic route which creates the benzene ring. In some cases a ring system is generated by a two stage process of ring closure (or cyclization) and then aromatization. The Haworth synthesis is an important route in preparing naphthalene derivatives. All of the steps are familiar: F-C acylation to start the second ring, a Clemmensen reduction, then another F-C acylation to close the ring, another Clemmensen reduction, then aromatization.

insert fig 30.2 on p 987

Substituted naphthalenes can be prepared from a few approaches:

(a) A substituted benzene rather than benzene is used, then the naphthalene is synthesized from it. Toluene, anisole, or bromobenzene or any group G = −R, −X, −OCH3 is the substituted benzene. The F-C acylation with succinic anhydride should place it mostly para to the group. The continued Haworth sequence produces a b-substituted naphthalene.

insert sequence middle p 986

(b) The intermediate cyclic ketone (an a-tetralone) is treated with a Grignard reagent or organolithium to produce and alkyl or aryl group into the a-position. Dehydration with hot acid leads to the alkenyl benzene, which can be quickly aromatized with Pd catalyst and heat to form an a-alkylnaphthalene

insert sequence bottom p 986

(c) The keto acid obtained after the first F-C acylation of a substituted benzene can be esterified, then treated with a Grignared, which reacts with the keto group to form the tertiary alcohol. Dehydration, ester hydrolysis, then catalytic hydrogenation produces the substituted benzene, and Haworth synthesis can proceed to make the 1,6-disubstituted naphthalene

insert sequence top p 988

30.15-.16 Nomenclature and structure of anthracene and phenanthrene derivatives

The positions of the important substitutable carbons on anthracene and phenanthrene are shown:

insert structures bottom p 988

Anthracene can assume 4 possible resonance structure. Phenanthrene can be drawn as a resonance hybrid of 5 possible resonance structures. Heats of combustion analyses show a resonance energy stabilization of 84 kcal/mol for anthracene, 92 kcal/mole for phenanthrene, the difference probably due to the extra resonance structure which can be drawn.

30.17 Reactions of anthracene and phenanthrene

Anthracene and phenanthrene are even less resistant to oxidation than naphthalene. Both are oxidized to their 9,10-quinones and reduced to their 9,10-dihydro compounds. The attack at the 9,10 positions is predictable since it leaves two benzene ring structures intact, with a sacrifice of only 12 kcal resonance energy (84−2 36) for anthracene, and 20 kcal (92−2 36) for phenanthrene. (With phenanthrene, the structure remains conjugated, estimated anywhere from 0 to 8 kcal stabilization, so the actual sacrifice may be less than 20 kcal.)

insert all structure/reactions on p 990

Although both compounds undergo EAS, the reactions are of little use since mixture and polysubstitution products abound. Derivatives are usually obtained by electrophilic substitution of the 9,10-anthraquinone or 9.10-dihydrophenanthrene.

Bromination for either phenanthrene or anthracene takes place at the 9-position. Without the Lewis acid catalyst in the phenanthrene reaction, the reaction is addition rather than substitution to form the 9,10-dibromo-9,10-dihydrophenanthrene. The mechanism of attack at the 9-position is easy to understand: a stable carbonium intermediate is formed in which either a nucleophile can add to the carbonium to make the addition product or a proton can be lost to form the substitution product. The sacrifice of energy is adding has already been shown to be minimal. In all the mechanism preserves two of the three aromatic rings.

30.18 Preparation of anthracene derivatives by ring closure. Anthraquinones

The derivatives of anthracene are seldom prepared from anthracene itself. Typically a ring closure to form a reducible anthraquinone is typical. The reduction is done with Zn and alkali, but is seldom done since anthraquinones are very important compounds. Reaction first involves F-C acylation of benzene with phthalic anhydride to form the o-benzoylbenzoic acid, and ring closure to form the 9,10-anthraquinone is done with sulfuric acid and heat.

insert reaction sequence bottom p 992

Where monosubstituted benzenes, such as toluene, are used, F-C acylation is to the para position typically, ultimately to yield a 2-substituted anthraquinone. (Blocked para positions can direct the F-C acylation to the ortho position.

Instead of benzene, a polynuclear compound such as naphthalene can be used to give a product with three or more rings.

insert sequence 2nd from top on p 993

The intermediate o-aroylbenzoic acid produced after F-C acylation can first be reduced at the keto group (with Zn and alkali) prior to ring closure. With ring closure, anthrone (a keton group in the 9 position) is formed, which reacts with Grignard reagents and is dehydrated to form 9-alkylanthracenes.

insert reaction sequence 3rd from top p 993

Anthraquinoid dyes like alizarin, indanthrene Golden Yellow GK, and indanthrone, are very important commercial products.

30.19 Preparation of phenanthrene derivatives by ring closure

Starting with naphthalene instead of benzene, the Haworth synthesis can be used to synthesize substituted phenanthrenes. Naphthalene can be acylated in AlCl3 with succinic anhydride at either the 1- or 2-positions, the keto group reduced via Clemmensen, attachment of the other end to the 1- or 2-position which was not substituted before, then reduction of the keto and then aromatization to yield a phenanthrene. The second acylation step of the 2-substituted naphthalene to the 3-position does not occur, which would form the anthracene.

30.20 Carcinogenic hydrocarbons

Polynuclear hydrocarbons are being studied intensively for the cancer-causing properties. The more powerful carcinogens are the derivatives of 1,2-benzanthracene:

insert structures on p 996 and 997

Chap. 31 Heterocyclic compounds

31.1 Heterocyclic systems

Any heterocyclic ring is one with a ring made up of more than one kind of atom. In the systems studied so far, nearly all members of the ring are carbon and are thus homocyclic or alicyclic. Other ring systems are numerous however.

insert all structures on p 1002

insert table 31.1 on p 1003

Some heterocyclic compounds have already been encountered: cyclic anhydrides, cyclic imides, lactones, lactams, cyclic acetals of dihydroxy alcohols, the solvents dioxane and tetrahydrofuran.

Three-membered rings have been encountered: epoxides, aziridines; the fleeting but important carbonium ion intermediate structures cyclic halonium and cyclic sulfonium.

Typically heterocyclic reagents are used as protecting groups: the 2-oxazoline ring and the temporary formation of tetrahydropyranyl(THP) esters, which are resistant to alkali but cleaved easily with acid.

A large number of heterocyclic compounds are naturally occurring: penicillin G, thiamine, reserpine (a tranquilizer), nicotine, copper phthalocyanine, and chlorophyll a.

31.2 Five-membered rings. Structure of pyrrole, furan, and thiophene

The three simplest five-membered heterocycles containing a single hetero atom are pyrrole, furan, and thiophene. One might judge them to be conjugated dienes with an amine function, an ether, and a thioether, and undergoing reactions typical of these functions. However, except for a tendency to undergo addition, they do not have the expected properties.

Rather these compounds and their derivatives undergo electrophilic substitution: nitration, sulfonation, halogenation, F-C acylation, and even the Reimer-Tiemann reaction and coupling with diazonium salts. Heats of combustion put an estimate of 22-28 kcal/mol resonance stabilization, somewhat less than for benzene (36 kca/mol) but far greater than for conjugated dienes (~3 kcal/mol). Hence these compounds are clearly aromatic.

However, when the structure of pyrrole is analyzed, it can be shown that all of the ring atom lie in a plane: the four sp2-hybridized carbons necessarily force the nitrogen into this position. The carbons bring 1 p electron to the ring in the pi structure (4 total) but nitrogen brings a nonbonding pair in one of its p orbitals. That make the 4n + 2 H ckel six needed for aromaticity.

As a result of the nitrogen′s nonbonding pair contributing to the aromatic character of the ring, this nonbonding pair is no longer available in the typical acid-base chemistry characteristic of amines in general. Pyrrole is a very weak base in fact, with a Kb ~ 2.5 × 10−14. In representing pyrrole structure, it becomes necessary to represent nitrogen with a positive charge as its electron pair is distributed to all other four carbons, which can be represented as carbanions in the structure.

Furan and thiophene can be similarly analyzed in terms of their aromaticity. Each brings a nonbonding pair in one its p orbitals to form an aromatic sextet. As a result, their resonance structures show the ring bearing a distributed negative charge and the hetero atom positively charged.

31.3 Source of pyrrole, furan, and thiophene

Pyrrole and thiophene can be obtained in small amounts from coal tar. During fractional distillation, thiophene (b.p. 84°) is collected in the benzene fraction (b.p. 80°); benzene typically contains a 0.5% thiophene contamination and must be especially treated if thiophene-free benzene is to be used.

Industrial synthesis of thiophene is possible from n-butane and sulfur.

560°

n-butane + S ——→ thiophene + H2S

Pyrrole can be synthesized in many ways, but one is from acetylene and acetaldehyde:

560° NH3, pressure

HC≡CH + 2 HCHO ——→ HOCH2C≡CCH2OH ————→ pyrrole

Furan is more readily obtained from the decarbonylation (elimination of carbon monoxide) of furfural (furfuraldehyde), which is prepared from the treatment of oat hulls, corncobs, or rice hulls with hot HCl. Pentosan (polypentosides) present in the plant materials are hydrolyzed to pentoses, and are caramelized (dehydrated) to the furfural.

insert reaction sequence middle p 1007

Although substituted pyrroles, furans, and thiophenes can be made from substitution, some unique reactions can be made from ring closure of open-chain compounds. Acetonylacetone (2,5-hexadione) can be closed in phosphorus pentoxide, ammonium carbonate, or phosphorus pentasulfide to make the furan, pyrrole, and thiophene, resp.

insert reaction bottom p 1007

31.4 Electrophilic substitution in pyrrole, furan, and thiophene. Reactivity and orientation

The EAS reactions which these heterocycles undergo have already been listed. In fact these rings are much more reactive than benzene, resembling the very active benzene derivatives (amines and phenols) in undergoing such reactions as the Reimer-Tiemann, nitrosation, and coupling with diazonium salts. The substitution occurs primarily at the 2-position.

Furan + pyridine:SO3 ——→ 2-furansulfonic acid

0°

Furan + (CH3CO)2O + Et2O:BF3 ——→ 2-acetylfuran

Thiophene + C6H5COCl + SnCl4 ——→ 2-benzoylthiophene

Pyrrole + C6H5N≡N+Cl− ——→ 2-(phenylazo)pyrrole

Pyrrole + CHCl3 + KOH ——→ 2-pyrrolecarboxyaldehyde (low yield)

In some of the reactions, the high reactivity means that relatively weak Lewis acid catalysts can be used: stannic chloride in the F-C acylation of thiophene. Pyrrole and furan are sensitive to protic acids, the former undergoing polymerization, the latter ring-opening, so the sulfonating agent must be modified.

In understanding EAS in the aromatic heterocycles, again the more stable carbonium ion intermediate is likely to form. If attack were to occur at the 3-position yields two possible resonance structures in the positively charged ring intermediate. The charge is distributed to the nitrogen and the 2-carbon in these intermediates. Attack at the 2-position produces three possible resonance structures in the intermediate, with positively charged nitrogen or carbons at positions 3 and 5 being involved. Because of the more favorable entropy of charge distribution, attack at position 2 is favored. In both the attacks at the 2- or 3-position, there is one resonance structure in which every atom has the octet (the one in which nitrogen assumes the positive charge), a very stable arrangement, which is why pyrrole resembles anilinium ion in its reactivity.

31.5 Saturated five-membered heterocycles

The aromatic heterocycles have their fully saturated analogs. Pyrrole can be catalytically reduced with H2, Ni at 200-250° to form pyrrolidine. Furan can be similarly reduced but at a lower temperature (50°) to form tetrahydrofuran. Thiophene poisons most catalysts, so its saturated analog, tetrahydrothiophene, is prepared from open-chain compounds.

heat

BrCH2CH2CH2CH2Br + Na2S ——→ tetrahydrothiophene

Of course, saturation destroys the aromaticity of the ring. But the saturated rings acquire the properties typical of the functional groups present which were missing with aromatization: pyrrolidine is a typical secondary aliphatic amine which is significantly basic; tetrahydrofuran is a typical alphatic ether, and tetrahydrothiophene a typical aliphatic thioether. THF is used in reductions with LiAlH4 in preparation of ArMgCl, and in hydroborations. Oxidiation of tetrahydrothiophene produces tetramethylene sulfone (of sulfolane), which is a solvent. Pyrrolidine is a secondary amine used in preparing enamines.

31.6 Six-membered rings. Structure of pyridine

Of the many possible six-membered aromatic heterocycles, only pyridine is considered because of its widespread use. Evidence of pyridine′s aromatic nature comes from its flatness, with bond angles of 120°. All bond lengths between atoms in the ring are the same: the four C-C bonds and the two C-N bonds. It resists addition and prefers EAS-type reactions. Heat of combustion analysis shows a 23 kcal/mol resonance stabilization.

Pyridine does differ from pyrrole in its aromaticity. It is a much stronger base. In determining the contributions of different electrons to the pi ring, and the drawing of the sp2-hybridized orbitals, an unshared pair is held in one of the sp2 orbitals. This means that it is not necessary for this nonbonding pair to contribute to ring aromaticity; it can easily participate in acid-base chemistry, so pyridine is therefore a stronger base than pyrrole.

31.7 Source of pyridine compounds

Pyridine is isolated from coal tar. It is found also with a number of methylpyridines, the most important being the monomethyl pyridines, or picolines. Oxidation of picolines (either the 2-, 3-, or 4-picolines) produces pyridine carboxylic acids. The 3-isomer is nicotinic acid or niacin and is a vitamin. The 4-isomer, isonicotinic acid, has been used in the hydrazide form (isonazid) in the treatment of tuberculosis.

Pyridine derivatives are in such demand that a synthesis involving ring closure and using acreolin and ammonia has been developed.

KMnO4

2 CH2=CH—CHO + NH3 ——→ 3-methylpyridine (β-picoline) ——→ nicotinic acid

31.8 Reactions of pyridine

Pyridine is an aromatic and a base. Typically it undergoes both EAS and NAS, and it is also a base.

31.9 Electrophilic substitution in pyridine

In EAS, pyridine resembles a highly deactivated benzene derivative. It undergoes nitration, sulfonation, and halogenation only under very vigorous conditions, but does not undergo F-C reactions at all. Substitution is at the 3- (b-) position.

insert the reactions bottom p 1013

The reactivity and orientation can be accounted for in the intermediate structures of EAS. If attack is at the 4- (or γ-)position of pyridine, three resonance structures can be drawn with positive charge at the two positions ortho and at the para (nitrogen) position. This last resonance structure is especially unstable: while N is willing to share electrons and assume a positive charge, it is surrounded by only a sextet of electrons, a resonance structure untenable for a highly electronegative atom such as nitrogen. Attack at the 2-position (this time nitrogen is ortho) follows the same reasoning in drawing the resonance structures.

Attack at the 3-position at least affords that in all resonance structures, every atom has the octet.

However, all of these resonance structures are less stable because of nitrogen′s inductive effect which intensifies the positive charge in the ring intermediate, making it much less reactive than benzene.

31.10 Nucleophilic substitution in pyridine

Knowing that pyridine resembles a benzene with deactivating properties, it is known that what is deactivating in EAS is usually activating insofar as NAS.

NH3, 180-200°

2-bromopyridine ————→ 2-aminopyridine

NH3, 180-200°

4-chloropyridine ————→ 4-aminopyridine

In fact, the reactivity of pyridine to NAS is so great that the powerfully basic hydride ion, :H−, can be displaced. One is amination by sodium amide (Chichibabin reaction) and another alkylation or arylation of organolithium compounds.

Na<+NH2 −+, heat

pyridine + NH3 —————→ 2-aminopyridine + H2

heat

pyridine + C6H5−Li+ ————→ 2-phenylpyridine + Li:H

The mechanism involves stabilizing the developing negative charge in the ring. With nucleophilic attack at the 4-position, two carbanion and one nitroanion resonance intermediates can be drawn, the latter one putting the negative charge on the nitrogen. Being quite electronegative, the latter resonance structure is especially stable, and every atom has an octet. As before, attack at the 2-position resembles attack at the 4-position. With 3-position attack, every intermediate is a carbanion, and while stable, the most stable resonance structure puts the negative charge on the nitrogen. For this reason, 2- and 4-position attacks should be more favorable to 3-position attacks.

31.11 Basicity of pyridine

Pyridine is intermediate in its basicity (pKa = 5.36), with pyrrole (pKa = 0.4) and most amines (pKa ~ 10). Why is pyridine such a weaker base than aliphatic amines?

Benzene is a stronger acid that an alkane, shown by its ability to displace an alkane from its salts; hence phenyl anion, C6H5−, is a weaker base than the alkyl ion, R−.

R:−Na+ + C6H5:H ———((— R:H + C6H5:−Na+

Similarly, acetylene is a stronger acid than benzene, meaning acetylide ion is a weaker base than the phenyl anion.

C6H5−Na+ + HC≡C:H ———((— C6H5:H + HC≡C:−Na+

The relative acidities and basicities can be shown as:

Relative acidity: HC≡C:H > C6H5:H > R:H

Relative basicity: HC≡C:− < C6H5:− < R:−

The difference in basicity may result from the nature of the carbanions. In the alkyl, phenyl, and acetylide ions, the unshared pair is found in an sp3, sp2, and sp orbital. The successively increasing s character tends to pull the electrons toward the carbon, making them less available in for an acid. The increasing p character, with the p lobe more extended from the nuclear center, puts the electron pair at a greater distance allowing for interaction. Hence electron pairs held in sp3 orbitals would be expected to be more basic than those in sp2 orbitals. That is the difference between the aliphatic amines, with their nonbonding pairs in sp3 orbitals, and pyridine, its nonbonding pair in sp2 orbital.

As weak a base as it is, it can still react with RX (like CH3I) to form a quaternary ammonium salt.

31.12 Reduction of pyridine

Pyridine can be catalytically hydrogenated to form its aliphatic heterocyclic, piperidine. (H2, Pt, HCl, 25°, 3 atm are the conditions). Piperidine (pKa = 11.3) has the basicity typical of a secondary aliphatic amine. Pyridine and piperidine are common in naturally occurring alkaloids, such as nicotine, strychnine, cocaine, and reserpine.

31.13 Fused rings. Quinoline. The Skraup synthesis

Quinoline (pKa = 4.48) is a benzene ring and pyridine ring fused as shown.

insert quinoline structure on p 1018

It can be found in coal tar. While certain derivatives are prepared by substitution of quinoline, the ring closure of a benzene derivative is the typical method.

The Skraup synthesis is the typical method for preparing substituted quinolines. Quinoline itself is obtained by a complex mixture of aniline reacting with glycerol and nitrobenzene in the presence of concentrated sulfuric acid and ferrous sulfate.

insert reaction top 1019

The mechanism appears to have the following steps.

(1) Glycerol by the action of hot sulfuric acid is caramelized (dehydrated) to form the a,b-unsaturated acrolein.

insert reaction

(2) Acrolein undergoes a nucleophilic addition in the presence of aniline to form a b-(phenylamino)propionaldehyde

insert reaction

(3) Ring closing occurs via an EAS when the a proton attaches to the carbonyl oxygen of the aldehyde, givign a carbonium ion character to the carbonyl carbon. This substitutes ortho to the activated anilium-substituted ring. The intermediate is an alcohol which dehydrates in the presence of the acid to form a 1,2-dihydroquinoline.

insert reaction

(4) Three of the 1,2-dihydroquinoline can be oxidized by nitrobenzene in the acid environment to form quinoline (the other products: aniline and 2 H2O).

insert reaction

The ferrous sulfate is apparently there to moderate what would otherwise be a very vigorous reaction.

The components of the basic synthesis can be modified to produce a wide variety of quinoline derivatives. Aniline and crotonaldehyde can make 2-methylquinoline (quinaldine). 3-nitro-4-aminoanisole + glycerol can be used to make 6-methoxy-8- nitroquinoline. 2-aminonaphthalene + glycerol can be used to produce 5,6-benzoquinoline (1-azaphenanthrene).

The nitrobenzene can be replaced by arsenic acid, H3AsO4, which usually produces a less violent reaction. Vanadium pentoxide is sometimes added as a catalyst. The sulfuric acid can be replaced by phosphoric acid or other acids.

31.14 Isoquinoline. The Bischler-Napieralski synthesis

Isoquinoline (pKa = 5.04) is a benzene ring and pyridine ring fused in a different way. Derivatives of isoquinoline can be made via the Bischler-Napieralski synthesis. Acyl derivatives of b-phenylethylamine can be cyclized by treatment with acids (P2O5) to produce a dihydroisoquinoline, which can be aromatized.

insert quinoline structure and reaction sequence at bottom p 1021
