Part III Biomolecules

Chap. 33 Fats

33.1 The organic chemistry of biomolecules

The study of biology at the molecular level is called biochemistry. The study of biochemistry must be built upon a study of the fundamentals of organic chemistry. The principle classes of biomolecules: fats, carbohydrates, proteins, and nucleic acids. Structures of these molecules is complex on several levels: sequence of functional groups, configuration, and conformation. It is the application of organic chemistry on more complex substances.

33.2 Occurrence and composition of fats

Biochemists have defined lipids as a set of biomolecules which are insoluble in water and extractable from cells with organic solvents of low polarity (ether, chloroform). This includes the steroids and terpenes.

Fats are the main constituents of storage fat cells in plants and animals, acting as an energy reserve. Liquid fats are oils, and many vegetable fats are liquid at room temperature.

Chemically fats are the carboxylic acid ester derived from a single polyalcohol, glycerol, and are known as glycerides. More specifically, they are triacylglycerols. The composition of different fats and oils is given in the table below to demonstrate the complexity of fats.

insert table 33.1 on p 1057

A number of unsaturated acids exist and all the double bonds are invariably in a cis configuration: oleic acid, CH3(CH2)CH=CH(CH2)7COOH; linoleic acid, CH3(CH2)4CH=CHCH2CH=CH(CH2)7COOH; linolenic acid, CH3CH2CH=CHCH2CH=CHCH2CH=CH(CH2 )7COOH. The unsaturation of these molecules with this particular stereochemistry is of vital biological importance: it lowers the m.p. of the fat. The long-chain, non-branching nature of fatty acids allows them to fit quite well in a lattice and form a solid phase; the better they fit, the stronger the intermolecular forces, the higher the m.p. With unsaturation in the trans- configuration, there is no significant change in the linear conformation assumed by the fat. With a cis configuration, a significant bend is obtained and the hinders the ability of the fat to settle into a solid phase lattice.

33.3 Hydrolysis of fats. Soap. Micelles

Making soap is one of the oldest chemical process, although not older than producing alcohol. German tribesmen in Caeser′s time boiled goat tallow in potash leached from the ashes of wood fires, and carried out a process done by modern soap manufacturers: hydrolysis of glycerides. The alkaline hydrolysis of fat yields glycerol and salts of fatty acids. Soap made from olive oil is called Castile soap. Adding alcohol makes it transparent; beating air into makes it float. If potassium salts of fatty acids are made, it becomes a soft soap.

These fatty acid salts dissolve in water, but are not truly in solution like most water soluble substances. Rather, soap is dispersed into spherical clusters called micelles, each containing perhaps hundreds of soap molecules. Fatty acid salts are amphipathic, with a polar end and a nonpolar end. These micelles form as a result of like molecules or components of molecules seeking like. Soap derives its cleansing properties from its ability to emulsify the hydrophobic elements in dirt that are not dispersed easily by water alone.

Hard water causes a bathtub ring because the high amount of calcium and magnesium combine with the fatty carboxylates to form an insoluble mass.

33.4 Fats as sources of pure acids and alcohols

Sodium soaps treated with mineral acids (or by hydrolysis of fats under acidic conditions) liberate a mixture of free RCOOH. Fractional distillation of these mixtures on a commercial scale makes these acids available at 90% purity.

Instead of hydrolysis, fats can be transesterified in methanol to produce glycerol and a mixture of methyl fatty esters. These fats can be reduced to their alcohols or fractionally distilled to provide are ready source of long straight-chain molecules for further manipulation.

33.5 Detergents

Straight-chain primary alcohols obtained from fats, particularly the C8 and C10 members are used to produce high boiling esters which serve as plasticizers (e.g., octyl phthalate). The C12 to C18 alcohols are used in large quantities in the manufacture of detergents and other cleansing agents.

All detergents share the same properties with soap: they are amphipathic. Lauryl alcohol (the n-C12 alcohol) is converted to its hydrogen sulfate with H2SO4, and this acid treated with NaOH to produce the sodium salt (SDS).

Fatty alcohols treated with ethylene oxide yield nonionic detergents. Lauryl alcohol can react with 8 ethylene oxide in alkaline medium to produce a polyether alcohol (ethoxylate). The many oxygens in the polyether end of the molecule make it water soluble.

The most widely used detergents are the Na salts of alkylbenzenesulfonic acids. Long-chain alkyl groups are attached to the benzene ring by a F-C catalyst with an RX, alkene, or alcohol. Sulfonation and then neutralization produces the final product.

Polypropylene was used to synthesize these alkylbenzenesulfonates, but it resisted biological degradation. Now soft (biodegradable) detergents are prepared: alkyl sulfates, polyether alcohols and their sulfates, and alkylbenzenesulfonates in which the phenyl group is randomly attached to various secondary positions of a long straight-chain (C12 to C18 range).

Detergents like these act exactly as does soap, but some advantages they have are that their sulfates and sulfonates remain soluble in hard water (the magnesium and calcium salts being soluble) and, being salts of strong acids, their solutions are neutral whereas soaps, being salts of weak acids, yield slightly alkaline solutions.

33.6 Unsaturated fats. Hardening of oils. Drying oils

Hydrogenation of cheaply available unsaturated fats, viz., plant oils, used for cooking (lard and butter are still preferred in the kitchen) is done under mild conditions (Ni catalyst, 175-190°, 20-40 psi) for hardening these oils for commercial purposes. This does not also subject the ester linkage to hydrogenolysis.

Unsaturated fats are more prone to become rancid than hydrogenated fats. The rancidity is a result of the presence of volatile and bad-smelling acids and aldehydes, which occur because of an attack by O2 at the reactive allylic positions in fat molecules. By partial hydrogenation, the elimination of these allylic positions is afforded.

Linseed oil and tung oil are important because of the high content of unsaturated glycerides. They are called drying oils and are used in the manufacture of paints and varnishes. The drying of paint is more than evaporation of a solvent (such as turpentine), but is a chemical reaction in which oxygen reacts with the fat to cross-link and polymerize via free radicals, the fat with the components of the paint and other chemicals to produce a tough organic film.

33.7 Phosphoglycerides. Phosphate esters

Another class of lipids are the phosphoglycerides, in which two fatty acids are esterified at the 1 and 2 position of glycerol, and a phosphoric acid is esterified to the third position. The esters are therefore carboxylic acid esters and phosphate esters. These lipids are responsible for forming the membranes of cells.

Phosphoric acid can actually form up to three esters centered about the phosphate group. The acid and its monoesters exist as ions in solution. Their esters can be hydrolyzed to their component alcohols and the phosphoric acid. In examining the (hydro)lytic reactions of carboxylates and sulfonates, there were found to be different cleavage sites. For esters of carboxylates, the nucleophile attack the acyl carbon, and the C-O bond of the RCOOR′ ester was cleaved. In esters of sulfonates, the O‐R bond in ArSO2OR was cleaved. With phosphate esters, cleavage can occur at either R—O or O—P in the structure ROPO3H2, but it depends upon the nature of the ROH that will be formed. In carboxylate esters, RCOO− is strongly basic and a poor leaving group from R+, so O‐R bond cleavage in RCOOR′ is not anticipated. In sulfonates, the RSO2O− is a good leaving group, and cleaving of this O‐R bond is likely. Phosphoric acids are intermediate in their acidity, hence phosphate is a better leaving group than carboxylate but poorer than sulfonate.

In acid solutions, all phosphate esters are readily cleaved to phosphoric acid. Hydrolysis in alkaline solutions is limited to trialkyl phosphates, (RO)3PO, in which one alkoxy group is removed. The mono- and dialkyl esters are inert in the alkaline solution, for the reason that the one -OH group on phosphate is acidic and becomes ionized, repelling the approach of hydroxide anion in substitution reactions.

33.8 Phospholipids and cell membranes

Like soaps, phospholipids are amphipathic. For this reason, one expects the formation of micelles in aqueous solutions. In biological processes, they tend to form a bilayer, with two rows of molecules stacked up against each other. This layer controls the flow of polar molecules into the fatty layer. (A simplistic summary of the action of phospholipid action in biological life forms is given.)

Chap. 34 Carbohydrates. Monosaccharides

34.1 Introduction

The sugar (+)-glucose is formed by plants from CO2 and H2O in the process of photosynthesis, in which radiant energy is incorporated by chlorophyll into making the high energy bonds in this reductive process. Thousands of (+)-glucose molecules can form polymers of cellulose to make the supporting framework of the plant, or can be formed into other polymers of starch to be stored in seeds and used as food.

Animals consuming starch and some consuming cellulose can break down the glucose polymers to individual (+)-glucose units, then re-polymerize them to form glycogen, which is namely animal starch. The energy in (+)-glucose is obtained when glucose is broken down into CO2 and H2O. Some glucose can be converted to fats, some can react with nitrogen-containing substances to form amino acids. All the sugars mentioned belong to the class of organic compounds called carbohydrates. Carbohydrates are everywhere: food, clothing (cotton, linen, rayon, and cellulose acetate), shelter (the wood of a house is mostly cellulose).

34.2 Definition and classification

Carbohydrates are polyhydroxy aldehydes, polyhydroxy ketones, or the compounds that can be hydrolyzed to them. A carbohydrate not hydrolyzable to a simpler compound is a monosaccharide. Any carbohydrate hydrolyzable to two monosaccharides is a disaccharide. Any carbohydrate hydrolyzable to many monosaccharide subunits is a polysaccharide.

Monosaccharides are subdivided into aldoses if they contain aldehyde groups, ketoses if they contain keto groups. They can be considered trisoses, tetroses, pentoses, hexoses, etc., depending upon carbon number. An aldohexose is a six-carbon monosaccharide with an aldehyde group. Most naturally occurring monosaccharides are pentoses or hexoses.

Carbohydrates which react with Fehling′s (or Benedict′s) or Tollens' reagents are called reducing sugars. All monosaccharides, whether ketose or aldose, are reducing sugars. Most disaccharides are also; sucrose (common table sugar) is an exception.

34.3 (+)-Glucose: an aldohexose

Perhaps the most important bioorganic molecule because of its sheer abundance, (+)-glucose is typical of the chemistry of most structures. Its structure has been deduced using a variety of organic reactions and physical methods used in organic chemistry.

Elemental analysis and molecular weight determinations reveals a molecular formula of C6H12O6. Reaction with phenylhydrazine produces a glucose phenylhydrazone indicating the presence of a carbonyl group, namely an aldehyde. Reaction with Br2 in water (the bromoform reaction) produces gluconic acid. Reaction with HNO3 produces the dicarboxylic acid, glucaric (or saccharic) acid, HOOC(CHOH)4COOH. Reaction with acetic anhydride produces penta-O- acetylglucose. Reaction with H2 with a Ni catalyst produces an alcohol, glucitol (sorbitol). When glucitol is treated with acetic anhydride, hexa-O-acetylglucitol (hexa-O-acetylsorbitol) is the product; or when glucitol is treated with HI and heat, 2-iodohexane is the product. When treated with HCN, then hydrolyzed, then treated with HI and heat, heptanoic acid is isolated.

All of these reactions helped to define (+)-glucose as 6-carbon, straight- chain, pentahydroxy aldehyde, namely an aldohexose.

34.4 (−)-Fructose: a 2-ketohexose

Second only to glucose in importance, this widely naturally occurring sugar was established as a ketohexose by the following chemistry: fructose was treated with HCN to produce the cyanohydrin (two diastereomers) possible, which was hydrolyzed to produce a hydroxy acid, then heated with HI to produce racemic a-methylcaproic acid.

34.5 Stereoisomers of (+)-glucose. Nomenclature of aldose derivatives

Four chiral centers are present in the glucose structure. The structure for glucose is typically represented by the cross formula, in which lines cross (the point of intersection representing the chiral center) and the horizontal lines represent groups projecting toward the viewer, and vertical lines project back from the viewer. (The book does not call them Fischer projections, but Emil Fischer described their use.) The -CH2OH group is always shown at the bottom, and the aldehyde or alternative group is shown at the top. This dissimilarity of the two ends of the aldohexose means that no meso compounds are possible. Therefore 24 or 16 stereoisomers are possible for aldohexoses. Certainly the enantiomer of (+)-glucose, (−)-glucose, is one of them. The others are diastereomers of this structure with their own particular names, with (+)-mannose and (+)-galactose being particularly abundant.

Products of reactions on the aldohexoses take their derivatives names as a root from the sugar (see table).

insert table 34.1 here

34.6 Oxidation. Effect of alkali

Aldoses are oxidized in 4 different ways: (i) with Fehling's or Tollens' reagent; (ii) with bromine water; (iii) by nitric acid; and (iv) with periodic acid, HIO4.

Being aldehydes, the aldoses certainly reduce Tollens' reagent. They also reduce Fehling's solution, an alkaline solution of cupric ion complexed with tartrate ion (or Benedict's solution, where complexing is with citrate ion). In the latter, a deep blue color obtains, and red cuprous oxide precipitates. A problem with these reactions is that they do not distinguish between ketoses and aldoses, for this reduction is a behavior of a-hydroxy ketoses. Moreover, they are not used in the preparation of aldonic (monocarboxylic) acids from aldoses, as the alkalinity of the medium causes extensive isomerization of the sugars and decomposition of the chain. (The alkaline medium induces enediol tautomers which are reactive.)

Bromine water does oxidize aldoses, but not ketoses, since it is acidic in part and prevents isomerization of ketoses to aldoses. It is used to synthesize aldonic acids of aldoses.

Nitric acid is a more vigorous oxidizer of aldoses, oxidizing the terminal carbons (the aldehyde and the -CH2OH groups), forming the aldaric (dicarboxylic) acids.

The reaction of compounds containing either −OH or =O groups on adjacent carbons to periodic acid, HIO4, has already been discussed (a reaction shown by L. Malaprade in 1928), and is very useful in analyzing carbohydrate structure.

34.7 Osazone formation. Epimers

Aldehydes react with phenylhydrazines to produce phenylhydrazones. With excess phenylhydrazine, the reaction proceeds even further to produce osazones, which are sugars with two phenylhydrazine residues per molecule. A third phenylhydrazine produces aniline and ammonia.

—CHOH-CHO + 3 C6H5NHNH2 —→ —C(=NNHC6H5)-C=NNHC6H5 + aniline + NH3

Osazones are not limited to carbohydrates, but are typical of a-hydroxy aldehydes and ketones (e.g. benzoin, C6H5CHOHCOC6H5). Removing the phenylhydrazines by mixing acid with an aldehyde (like benzaldehyde) produce dicarbonyls callled osones.

C6H5CHO, H+

—C(=NNHC6H5)-C=NNHC6H5 —————→ —C(=O)-CHO + 2 C6H5CH=NNHC6H5

P. Griess (1858) had discovered how to make diazonium salts. E. Fischer (1875) found that reduction of benzenediazonium chloride by SO2 produces phenylhydrazine. Then in 1884, Fischer showed what a powerful tool the phenylhydrazine was in studying carbohydrates. One of the useful aspects is that osazone formation destroys the configuration at carbon C2. Hence (+)-glucose and (+)-mannose yield the same osazone. Since mannose and glucose are epimeric at C2, the configuration at the other carbons must be the same.

34.8 Lengthening of the carbon chain of aldoses. The Killiani-Fischer synthesis

H. Kiliani (1886) reported that an aldose can be converted into two aldonic acids with one carbon more than the aldose by addition of HCN followed by hydrolysis of the cyanohydrin. Fischer (1890) reported that reduction of an aldonic acid (in the form of its lactone) can be controlled to yield the corresponding aldose. Hence, combining the Kiliani-Fischer synthetic reactions can convert an aldopentose into two aldohexoses.

Addition fo cyanide to the carbonyl generates a new chiral center, giving two possible configurations. The result is two new diastereomeric cyanohydrins. The diastereomers are generated in unequal amounts because the aldehyde itself is chiral and is affected by neighboring groups.

Hydrolysis of the cyanohydrin under acidic conditions is likely to result in the formation of a γ- or Δ-lactone, with a γ-lactone more likely. The lactone is reduced by sodium amalgam with CO2 (the Fischer reduction); today, the reducing agent NaBH4 is used, a reagent unknown to chemists during Fischer′s time. The diastereomers are separable, having different physical properties; this is actually done with the aldonic acids, as they are easier to separate than the epimeric sugars.

34.9 Shortening the carbon chain of aldoses. The Ruff degradation

Aldosugars can be degraded as well to form an aldose of one less carbon. One method is the Ruff degradation. First the aldose is oxidized to the aldonic acid with bromine water. The calcium salt of the acid is made by mixing with CaCO3, then the calcium aldonate is treated with hydrogen peroxide in the presence of ferric salts. The aldose (less one carbon) and CO32− are produced as a result.

34.10 Conversion of an aldose into its epimer

Aldose sugars can be epimerized at one of the chiral carbons by a reaction in which the aldose is oxidized to its aldonic acid with bromine water, and then a 3° amine (in particular, pyridine) is added which specifically epimerizes the C2 carbon in an equilibrium reaction, the only carbon epimerized. The aldonic acid and its epimer are separated and their lactones formed, which are then reduced. The epimeric aldose is thus obtained.

34.11 Configuration of (+)-glucose. The Fischer proof

In 1888, very few monosaccharides were known: (+)-glucose, (−)-fructose, (+)-arabinose. (+)-Mannose was just synthesized. Aldoses were known to be reduced to alditols and oxidized to mono- (aldonic) and dicarboxylic (aldaric) acids. Theories of stereoisomerism and optical activity had been proposed by van′t Hoff and Le Bel (1874). The structure of (+)-glucose was known, but not its stereochemistry. In 1891, Fischer reported after 3 years what the stereochemical structure was of glucose (he received a Nobel Prize for it in 1902). Although the absolute configuration could not be known, he could limit the 16 possibilities to a pair of enantiomers.

Fischer first rejected 8 of the 16 by placing the -OH group which would be C5 carbon on the right of his projection, an arbitrary decision which proved correct. (+)-glucose was related to the aldopentose (−)-arabinose, which had to one of 4 structures. He decided on one of the four structures from the evidence known:

1. (−)-arabinose treated with HNO3 produces an optically active dicarboxylic acid, which means that two of the four structures could be eliminated as their oxidation would produce optically inactive meso dicarboxylic acids. The C2 carbon -OH group of (−)-arabinose must be on the left in the Fischer projection, which would mean the C3 carbon -OH group is on the left, being one carbon removed.

2. (−)-arabinose is converted to (+)-glucose and (+)-mannose in the Kiliani-Fischer synthesis. This is interpreted easily that glucose and mannose are C2 epimers, with the same configuation at C3, C4 and C5 as (−)-arabinose. The possible structures for glucose and mannose are limited to four, either I and II, or III and IV:

insert structures III, IV, and VII, and VIII top p. 1082.

3. Oxidation of (+)-glucose and (+)-mannose by HNO3 yields optically active dicarboxylic acids. This defines the configuration at C4 carbon, in which the -OH group is shown on the right in the projection. A left-sided -OH group is certain to produce an optically inactive meso dicarboxylic acid with either glucose or mannose during the oxidation. The stereochemistry of (−)-arabinose, with its three chiral carbons at C3, C4, and C5, is now entirely assigned at this point. There are two structures left, one being glucose, one being mannose.

insert structure of arabinose on p 1084, indicate C4 is also as glucose or mannose

4. When another hexose, (+)-gulose, is oxidized with HNO3, optically active (+)-glucaric acid is produced, the same dicarboxylic acid produced by nitric acid oxidation of (+)-glucose. (+)-Glucaric acid might have two possible configurations (V and VI), one of which is derived from two aldoses which are diastereomers (structure I and VII), and one from what looks like another two diastereomers (VIII), but which is just the same one rotated 180°. Since structure I is projected in the right manner (CHO up and CH2OH down), structure I is (+)-glucose. (+)-Mannose must therefore be structure II.

34.12 Configuration of aldoses

The aldopentose (−)-ribose forms the same osazone as (−)-arabinose. This means that the ribose structure is a C2 epimer of arabinose, and ribose must have the structure shown.

insert ribose structure on p 1086

The two remaining aldopentoses, (+)-xylose and (−)-lyxose, must differ from arabinose and ribose in their C3 configuration. The epimeric positions of the C2 carbon were determined in that (+)-xylose oxidized by HNO3 forms an optically inactive meso aldaric acid, suggesting the proper symmetry.

insert xylose and lyxose structures on p. 1086.

(−)-Arabinose can be degraded to (−)-erythrose, which must have the structure shown. Proof of this structure obtains when (−)- erythrose is oxidized by HNO3 to optically inactive meso- tartartic acid.

Degradation of (+)-xylose produces the tetrose (−)-threose. Its oxidation produces the expectedly optically active (−)-tartaric acid.

insert structures of erythrose and threose here

34.13 Optical families. d and l

In many reactions, the absolute configuration is not known and not even that important. What is often important is the relative configuration, and this is not especially easy.

In the salad days of carbohydrate chemistry, the simplest optically active carbohydrate, the aldotriose glyceraldehyde, was used as a standard of configurational reference. The (+)-glyceraldehyde was arbitrarily assigned a configuration (I) and designated as d-glyceraldehyde; its enantiomer, (−)-glyceraldehyde,was called l-glyceraldehyde. The chance was 50:50 that the configurations assigned were correct (and it just happened to be correct). So long as no chiral center bonds were broken or the stereochemistry involving the breaking of any chiral bonds accounted for, molecules derived from this standard could be configurationally known.

The d and l designations do not indicate optical rotation directions, but were designed to group optical families in which preserved stereochemical configurations could be used to relate one molecule to another.

34.14 Tartaric acid

The importance of (+)-tartaric acid in chemistry today can not be overestimated. Pasteur formulated the theory of enantiomerism and explained optical activity as a result of his work with tartaric acid, and 100 years later, Bijvoet studied the sodium-rubidium salt of tartaric acid using x-ray diffraction to establish its absolute configuration, and thereby established the absolute configuration of all chiral molecules in the whole of organic chemistry. The configurational link between d-(+)-glyceraldehyde and d-(−)-tartaric acid had been made in 1917, in which glyceraldehyde was extended with HCN to give two diastereomers. The nitriles were hydrolyzed with Ba(OH)2, then oxidized with HNO3. One diastereomer produces the meso -tartaric acid, the other d-(−)-tartaric acid.

When Bijvoet had determined the absolute configuration of the l-(+)-tartaric acid, he found that it, and therefore its enantiomer, and its precursor glyceraldehyde, had the true and same absolute configurations which had been arbitrarily assigned.

insert structures of d- and l-tartaric acids on p 1091

34.15 Families of aldoses. Absolute configuration

With designation of d-(+)-glyceraldehyde as showing in a projection in which the carbonyl is at the top, and the -OH group on the right of the chiral center, all larger carbohydrates related to the d-glyceraldehyde belong to either the d or l families, with the letter designating the position of the lowest chiral center. Had Bijvoet′s data shown the opposite of what E. Fischer has assumed as the absolute configuration of glucose, the D family might have the -OH group on the left at the lowest chiral center.

34.16 Cyclic structure of d-(+)-glucose. Formation of glucosides

Although there is a great deal of evidence showing glucose is a pentahydroxy aldehyde with configurations well-established, there were some problems in accounting for this structure.

(a) d-(+)-Glucose fails to react with reagents which identify substances as aldehydes (carbonyls). Although glucose easily oxidizes, it gives a negative Schiff test and does not form a bisulfite addition product.

(b) d-(+)-Glucose exists in two isomeric forms which appear to undergo mutarotation. Crystals of d-(+)-glucose (m.p. 146°) dissolved in water show a change in their specific rotation from +112° to 52.7° over a gradual period. On the other when crystals of d-(+)-glucose (m.p. 150°), which are obtained by crystallization at over 98°, are dissolved in water, their specific rotation gradually rises from +19° to +52.7°. The form of glucose with the higher rotation is called α- d-(+)- glucose, the form with the lower rotation β-d-(+)-glucose, and the change in rotation which occurs during this equilibrium process is called mutarotation.

(c) d-(+)-Glucose forms two isomeric methyl d-glucosides. In the presence of anhydrous HCl, aldehydes react with alcohols to form acetals. First the hemiacetal is formed, addition to the carbonyl, then substitution of the remaining −OH group to form the acetal in the acid- catalyzed process. With glucose, however, only one −CH3 group is present instead of two. It does not spontaneously revert to the aldehyde and alcohol in water, requiring hydrolysis by aqueous acids. Moreover, there are two different monomethyl derivatives, one with m.p. 165° and [α] = +158°, which is methyl α- d -glucoside, and the other with m.p. 107° and [α] = −33°, which is methyl-β- d-glucoside. These do not undergo mutarotation nor reduce Tollens' or Fehling's reagent.

In 1895 glucose was given a cyclic structure (as a result of work by Tollens, Tanret, and Fischer). Its ring size was corrected in 1926, and the preferred conformations now elucidated in recent years.

insert all of fig 34.8 here on p 1095

The hemiacetal form of glucose results simply because alcohol is part of its ring structure. It has two isomeric forms because it now one more chiral center than in the open-chain form (at C1). a-d-(+)-glucose and b-d-(+)-glucose are diastereomers, with a different C1 configuration, and are given the special name anomers. Being hemiacetals, they are readily hydrolyzed in water to the open-chain form, which exists as an equilibrium mixture with both anomers. Reactions of aqueous solutions of d-(+)-glucose to form osazones or to reduce Tollens' or Fehling's reagent presumably result from what little open-chain form exists, and as all of it is consumed, more is generated to continue to react. This concentration of open-chain form is much too low (< 0.5%) for easily reversible aldehyde reaction like bisulfite addition or the Schiff test.

The methyl d-glucosides exist as cyclic structures. They are full acetals, despite only one methanolic group, because the sugar -OH serves as the other alcohol. These glucosides do not undergo mutarotation, being stable in water. If they are heated in aqueous acid, however, hydrolysis to the cyclic hemiacetal forms occurs. In basic solutions they are stable, as are typical of acetals. They are non-reducing sugars, not being able to revert to the open-chain aldehyde for reaction with Tollens' or Fehling's reagent.

34.17 Configuration about C-1

With glucose being shown to be a cyclic structure, a question about the configuration of C1 in anomeric structures was given an answer in 1909 by C. S. Hudson who proposed that the d-series of the more dextrorotatory member of α,β pair of anomers be called α-d-, the other being named β-d-. For the l-series, the more levorotatory was to be named α-l, while the other was to be β-l. Hence the enantiomer of α-d-(+)-glucose is α-l-(−)-glucose. In projections, the -OH or -OCH3 for the α-d-anomer would be shown to the right of the chiral carbon (or down in cyclic projections).

Hudson′s proposals turned out to be correct, although the assumptions about configuration and optical activity upon which they were based were incorrect. That a-d-glucose and a-d-glucosides have the same configuration comes from evidence that enzymatic hydrolysis of methyl a-d-glucoside yields highly rotating a-d-glucose, and for methyl b-d-glucoside it liberates b-d-glucose.

C1 configuration is the same in all methyl a-glycosides of all d-aldohexoses. This was proved by HIO4 oxidation, which does not destroy the C1 configuration (or at C5), which give the same products. This could not be true if they had different configurations at C1. And the proof of absolute configuration putting -OH on the right of C1 in projections comes from x-ray diffraction.

34.18 Methylation

While CH3OH and dry HCl produces methyl glucosides, converting the sugar hemiacetal to the acetal, when methyl d-glucoside is treated with methyl sulfate, (CH3)2SO4, and NaOH, the four remaining -OH groups become methylated to form tetra-O-methyl-d-glucoside. This reaction is a modification of the Williamson ether synthesis, because of the relatively high acidity of the −OH groups.

Treatment of tetra-O-methyl-d-glucoside with dilute HCl removes only the methoxy group at C1, forming tetra-O-methyl-d-glucose. The methoxy group at C1 is an acetal, whereas the other groups are true ethers and are more stable.

34.19 Determination of ring size

Evidence of a ring structure comes from chemical degradation experiments. For example, methyl b-d-glucoside is treated with (CH3)2SO4 and NaOH, then the product is hydrolyzed by dil. HCl to obtain tetra-O-methyl-d-glucose. Vigorous oxidation with HNO3 follows. The −CHO and free −OH group which ought to be present should be converted to yield a keto acid with continued oxidation of the ketone group to yield a cleaved carbon-carbon bond on either side of the keto group.

Oxidation in fact yields the trimethoxyglutaric acid, HOOC(CHOCH3)3COOH, and dimethoxysuccinic acid, HOOC(CHOCH3)2COOH. The first product results from cleavage of the C5-C6 bond, the second from the C4-C5 bond. The entire structure is predicted if the reactant oxidized is 2,3,4,6-tetra-O-methyl-d-glucose. These reactions are possible only if C1 and C5 are joined via the C5 hydroxyl in a six-membered ring. Hence, establishment of ring structure, largely shown by Sir W. N. Haworth, has been shown by this chemistry. Other evidence comes from x-ray diffraction and enzymatic hydrolysis.

Carbohydrates which have six-membered rings with an oxygen one of the elements are called pyranoses, based on the structure of the cyclic ether, pyran. Five-membered carbohydrate rings are called furanoses, after the aromatic ether, furan.

34.20 Conformation

In the six-membered ring structure of d-(+)-glucose, the C—O−C bond angle is about 111°, near the tetrahedral angle of 109.5°. Being a six-membered ring, the puckered conformation of cyclohexane in its chair structure is predicted, which provides minimal torsional and van der Waals strain. The x-ray data shows this to be correct.

However, two chair conformations are possible. One is more stable than the other, the reason being that the bulky groups, −OH groups on carbons 1-4 and −CH2OH on C5, can all obtain equatorial positions in b-d-(+)-glucose, with −H groups anti to each other. With a-d-(+)-glucose, this maximally stable arrangement is not possible. R. E. Reeves working with Cu complexes of carbohydrates has found that the first requirement is to place the −CH2OH group in the equatorial position, since it is the most bulky. Then proceeding from C4 to C3 to C2 and so on, the other groups are placed equatorially or axially to prevent the strain. This places the C1 −OH group in the axial position in a-d-(+)-glucose. In some sugars, such as a-d-idopyranose, there would be too many −OH groups in the axial position in order to accomodate −CH2OH in the equatorial position; therefore, the more stable conformer is to place the −OH groups equatorially and force the −CH2OH into the axial position.

Of all the d-aldohexoses, it is b-d-(+)-glucose that can assume a conformation in which every bulky group occupies the equatorial position, and so it should not be surprising that this molecule is the most widely occurring in nature.

In methylated and acetylated pyranoses, bulky groups occupy the equatorial positions with one general exception: a methoxy or acetoxy group on C1 tends to be axial. This anomeric effect is believed to result from a dipole repulsion between the dipoles of C1-oxygen and the ring oxygen.

Chap. 35 Carbohydrates. Disaccharides and polysaccharides

35.1 Disaccharides

Four disaccharides are considered here: (+)-maltose (malt sugar), (+)-cellobiose, (+)-lactose (milk sugar), (+)-sucrose (cane or beet sugar).

35.2 (+)-Maltose

(+)-Maltose is obtained by partial acid hydrolysis of starch, but can also be obtained by enzymatic hydrolysis with diastase. It molecular formula is C12H22O11. It reduces Tollens' and Fehling's reagents. It reacts with phenylhydrazine to form an osazone, C12H20O9(=NNHC6H5)2. It is oxidized in bromine water to form a monocarboxylic acid, maltobionic acid. It exists in alpha ([a] = +168°) and beta ([α] = +112°) forms which undergo mutarotation in solution (equilibrium [α] = +136°).

All these facts indicate that (+)-maltose has a C=O group existing in a reactive hemiacetal form. It has only one such free C=O as show by the osazone formation and bromine water oxidation.

Hydrolyzed in aqueous acid or treated with maltase, chemical analysis show the product to be completely d-(+)-glucose. Hence maltose consist of two d-(+)-glucose units formed by a condensation reaction. While hydrolysis by acid is characteristic of all glycosides, hydrolysis by maltase is characteristic only of alpha-glucosides.

The question of linkage of which −OH groups on the sugar was addressed by the sequence of oxidation, methylation, and hydrolysis of maltose. First bromine water converts maltose to maltobionic acid, the acid is treated with methyl sulfate and NaOH to form octa-O-methyl-d-maltobionic acid, which is then hydrolyzed in acidic solution. The products are 2,3,5,6-tetra-O-methyl-d-gluconic acid and 2,3,4,6-tetra-O-methyl-d-glucose. Hence the C4 of the reducing glucose is connected to the C1 of another via an oxygen. Maltose is therefore 4-O-(α-d-glucopyranosyl)-d-glucopyranoside.

35.3 (+)-Cellobiose

Celluose (cotton fibers) treated for several days in sulfuric acid and acetic anhydride yield products which are acetylated and hydrolyzed. The octaacetate of (+)-cellobiose is obtained. Alkaline hydrolysis yields the octaacetate itself. It has the same molecular formular as maltose, is a reducing sugar, forms an osazone, and exists in α and β anomeric forms capable of mutarotation. It produces the same products with the sequence of oxidation, methylation, and hydrolysis, so the linkage must be the same as well.

However, it cannot be hydrolyzed by maltase. It is hydrolyzed by an enzyme in bitter almonds, emulsin, which is known to hydrolyzed β-linkages. Hence cellobiose is like maltose except that the C1 to C4 linkage is β about the C1 carbon.

35.4 (+)-Lactose

This sugar makes up about 5% of human and cow milk. It is a by-product of cheese manufacture, being found in whey, the aqueous solution remaining after milk proteins have coagulated. The sourness of milk results from lactose metabolism (by Lactobacillus bulgaricus) to lactic acid.

(+)-Lactose is has same formula as the first two disaccharides, is a reducing sugar, forms and osazone, exists in α and β forms. Acidic hydrolysis or emulsin treatment converts lactose to d-(+)-galactose and d-(+)-glucose. Lactose must be a β-linked disaccharide.

The question remains as to whether lactose is a galactoside or a glucoside. Hydrolysis of the lactosazone produces d-(+)-galactose and d-glucosazone. Hydrolysis of lactobionic acid forms d-gluconic acid and d-(+)-galactose. Clearly d-(+)-glucose contains the free aldehyde necessary for osazone and acid formation. Hence it is a substituted d-glucose with a d-galactosyl group at one of the hydroxyl positions; it is a galactoside.

The oxidation, methylation, and hydrolysis sequence shows it to be galactose C1-glucose C4 linked.

35.5 (+)-Sucrose

Although its structure is identical with all three disaccharides discussed up to now, it does not reduce Tollens' or Fehling's reagent. It does not form an osazone, does not exist in anomeric forms, and shows no mutarotation. Therefore, (+)-sucrose has no free aldehyde or ketone group.

Hydrolyzed by dil. aqueous acid or by action of enzyme yeast invertase, equal amounts of d-(+)-glucose and d- (−)-fructose are obtained. Acid hydrolysis actually produces a solution which now rotates light in the negative direction, which has been termed inversion; sucrose is therefore called invert sugar. This is because the large negative rotation by the formed (−)-fructose overwhelms the positive rotation of (+)-glucose.

Because there is no free aldehyde, (+)-sucrose can be considered a d-glucoside and d-fructoside. The linkage is logical: since there is no free aldehyde or keto group, the glucose C1 must link to fructose C2. The stereochemistry at the linkage is difficult to assess when both linkages are hydrolyzed with equal opportunity. X-ray data and finally synthesis of (+)-sucrose (by R. U. Lemieux in 1953) confirmed that the glucose is the α-anomer and fructose the β-anomer.

35.6 Polysaccharides

Polysaccharides are polymers formed by condensation monomeric sugars. The most important ones are starch and cellulose, both compose of d-(+)-glucose units. Cellulose is the least water soluble, being mainly a structural sugar. Starch is found in the reserve food supply of plants and is mostly in seeds. It is more water soluble and more easily hydrolyzed.

35.7 Starch

Starch occurs as granules whose size and shape are characteristic of the plant where they came from. The intact granules are insoluble in cold water, but if the outer membrane is disrupted by grinding, the granules swell in cold water to form a gel. In warm water, the soluble portion of starch diffuses through the granule wall. In hot water the granules swell so much that they burst. 20% of starch is a water-soluble fraction called amylose, the other 80% being an insoluble amylopectin. The amylopection insolubility results apparently from a molecular weight difference. When treated with acid or by enzymes, these starch components hydrolyze progressively to dextrin (a mixture of low MW polysaccharides), then to maltose, then to glucose.

35.8 Structure of amylose. End group analysis

Hydrolysis of amylose ultimately produces (+)-maltose as the only disaccharide, and eventually d-(+)-glucose. The linkages in amylose of the individual glucose units must therefore be all C1 to C4 in the alpha linkage.

Methylation of amylose followed by hydrolysis yields 2,3,6-tri-O-methyl-d-glucose, which is expected from a large number of glucose units connected C1,C4 in a polysaccharide. About 0.2-0.4% of the product is also 2,3,4,6-tetra-O-methyl-d-glucose as well, which is perfectly reasonable considering that one glucose in a polymer must be an end group. Furthermore, at the other end, a glucose with a free aldehyde must be present (the reducing end).

The relative yield of tetramethylated glucose (representing an end group) to trimethylated glucose can provide an idea of chain length in the polymer. For example a 0.25% tetra-O-methyl glucose yield means that 1 end group exists for every 400 chain units. Physical methods actually demonstrate that MW ranging from 150,000 to 600,000 are usual; this suggests that chain degradation is occurring during the methylation step, predictable since a relatively alkaline medium is required. Amylose is therefore thought to be glucose polymers of 1000 or more units with little or no branching in the chain.

Amylose is the fraction of starch producing the blue color in reaction with iodine. X-ray data indicated that chains are coiled in the form of a helix, wherein the iodine becomes trapped and produces the observed blue color.

35.9 Structure of amylopectin

Like amylose, amylopectin is hydrolyzed to a single disaccharide, (+)-maltose. Methylation and hydrolysis produce mostly 2,3,6-tri-O-methyl-d-glucose, suggesting a similar structure to amylose. MW estimations from physical methods put amylopectin as having millions of glucose units per molecule, but 5% of the hydrolyzed methylated product is 2,3,4,6-tetra-O-methyl-d-glucose, suggesting only 20 units per chain.

The answer to the confusion is that 2,3-di-O-methyl-d-glucose is produced after methylation hydrolysis in amounts nearly equal to the tetramethyl derivative. This suggests a C1,C6 linkage about every 20-25 glucose units, showing it to be a highly branched structure.

Glycogen in animals is much like amylopectin, but is more highly branched with shorter chains at 12-18 glucose units each.

35.10 Structure of cellulose

Cellulose is insoluble in water and tasteless; it is also non-reducing. All of these properties may derive in part from its very high molecular weight. Acid hydrolysis produces only d-(+)-glucose as the monosaccharide. And hydrolysis of methylated cellulose give the 2,3,6-tri-O-methyl-d-glucose, showing it to be C1,C4 linked.

Physical methods show MW ranging from 250,000 to 1,000,000 or more, with at least 1500 glucose units per molecule. End group analysis by methylation and periodic acid oxidation indicated chain lengths of at least 1000 units or more. X-ray data and electron microscopy show long chains laying side by side, held together by hydrogen bonds in bundles, each bundle twisted together to form rope-like structures.

35.11-.13 Reactions of cellulose. Cellulose nitrate. Cellulose acetate

Being a very tough polymer, cellulose has been derivatized to advantage as its derivatives are made cheaply. Since it is an alcohol, cellulose can form esters. Treated with HNO3 and H2SO4, cellulose forms cellulose nitrate, the properties of which depend upon the extent of nitration. Gun cotton is used in making smokeless powder and is very nearly completely nitrated cellulose; it is often called cellulose trinitrate (since it would have three nitrate groups per glucose unit in the fully nitrated molecule). Pyroxylin is less highly nitrated, being between 2-3 nitrate groups per glucose; it is used in manufacture of plastics like celluloid and colloidin, in photographic film and in lacquers. It is unfortunately flammable and forms very toxic nitrogen oxides when burned.

Cellulose can be converted to its triacetate in the presence of acetic anhydride, acetic acid, and a little sulfuric acid. Partial hydrolysis removes some of the acetate groups, degrades the chain to smaller fragments (200-300 units each). The result is a commercial cellulose acetate (roughly diacetate). It is less flammable than cellulose nitrate, replacing the latter for use in safety-type photographic film. Solutions of cellulose acetate in acetone forced through the fine holes of a spinnerette cause solvent evaporation and leave solid filaments. The thread from these filaments make up a material called acetate rayon.

35.14 Rayon. Cellophane

Alcohols treated with CS2 and aqueous NaOH form a compound called a xanthate. Acid treatment reforms the alcohol and carbon disulfide.

H<+

RONa + S=C=S —→ RO−C(=S)−SNa —→ ROH + CS2

Cellulose undergoes a similar reaction to form cellulose xanthate, which then dissolves in teh alkali to form a viscous colloidal dispersion called viscose. Viscose forced through a spinnerette into an acid bath caused regeneration of cellulose in the form of fine filaments making threads called rayon. Although other processes for making rayon exist, this is the principal one in the U.S.

When viscose is forced through a narrow slit, the cellulose is regenerated as thin sheets. When softened with glycerol, they are used as protective films (Cellophane). Rayon and Cellophane are often considered to be regenerated cellulose but differ in that they are shorter polymers as a result of fragmentation resulting from alkali exposure.

35.15 Cellulose ethers

Cellulose can be alkylated by treating with alkali and then exposing to alkyl chlorides (which are cheaper than alkyl sulfates). Because of the intense alkalinity, considerable chain fragmentation is unavoidable. The methyl, ethyl, and benzyl ethers of cellulose are important in textiles, films, and various plastic objects.

Chap. 36 Amino acids and proteins

36.1 Introduction

The name protein is taken from the Greek proteios, which means first. A name well chosen, since proteins are the principle biomolecules of life.

36.2 Structure of amino acids

26 different amino acids have been found in proteins. For animals, certain amino acids are essential (i.e., they are obtained in the diet). The table shows the 26 amino acids and their structures. All but proline and hydroxyproline, which form a pyrrolidine ring, are alpha-amino carboxylic acids.

insert table 36.1 in parts

36.3 Amino acids as dipolar ions

The physical properties of amino acids differ from amines and carboxylic acids when their functional groups occur singly.

(a) Amino acids are nonvolatile crystalline solids which melt with decomposition at fairly high temperatures.

(b) They are insoluble in nonpolar solvents like petroleum ether, benzene, or ether, but are appreciably water soluble.

(c) Their aqueous solutions behave like solutions of substances of high dipole moment.

(d) Acidity and basicity constants are ridiculously low for −COOH and −NH2 groups. Glycine has a Ka of 1.6 × 10−10 and Kb = 2.5 × 10−12.

All of these properties are consistent with amino acids existing as dipolar ions in solution. The Ka in this case, is the Ka for a −NH3+ group and the K b for the −COO− group.

36.4 Isoelectric point of amino acids

When placed in an electric field, amino acids being charged molecules can migrate to one electrode or the other. The direction depends upon the net charge of the molecule. In a more alkaline medium, the amino acid becomes negatively charged. In a mored acidic medium, the charge is positive. At a particular pH, the net charge on the molecule is zero, its isoelectric point.

36.5 Configuration of natural amino acids

Every amino acid but glycine has at least one chiral center. Being chiral, they are of course optically active. Stereochemical studies show that the configuration of naturally occurring a-amino acids is similar to the configuration of l-(−)-glyceraldehyde, and they are l-amino acids.

36.6 Preparation of amino acids

Many methods for preparing amino acids exists, but one principal reaction is the amination of a-halo acids.

Direct ammonolysis of a-chloro or a-bromo acid with the use of excess concentrated ammonia is done (the excess ammonia presence prevents the newly formed amino acid amine group from undergoing further reaction to form a complex secondary or tertiary amine).

Br2, P NH3 (excess)

CH3CH2COOH ——→ CH3CHBrCOOH ———→ alanine (70% yield)

The a-halo acids are prepared either by the Hell-Volhard-Zelinsky halogenation of unsubstituted acids or by modification of the malonic ester synthesis.

C6H5CH2Cl KOH, heat HCl Br2, ether, reflux

Na+[CH(COEt)2]− ——→ PhCH2CH(COOEt)2 ——→ —→ PhCH2CH(COOH)2 ——→

heat NH3 (excess)

PhCH2CBr(COOH)2 ——→ PhCH2CHBrCOOH ——→ phenylalanine (35% yield overall)

Better yields are obtained with the Gabriel phthalimide synthesis, in which the a-halo esters are used instead of α-halo acids as the potassium phthalimide salt might react with the acid to re-form the phthalimide.

insert reaction sequence on p 744 problem 11

A modification of this synthesis is th phtahlimidomalonic esters method, a combined malonic ester-Gabriel synthesis.

insert reaction bottom p 1139 top p 1140

Preparation in this way produces the optically inactive amino acid of course.

36.7 Reactions of amino acids

Any reactions typical of amino acids are typical of amines and carboxylic acids. Any other functional groups present undergo their typical reactions as well.

36.8 Peptides. Geometry of peptide linkage

A peptide linkage is a condensation reaction producing the amide bond between successive amino acids.

X-ray studies show that the entire amide group is flat with a carbonyl carbon, nitrogen, and four atoms attached to them all lie in a plane. The short C−N bond (1.32 A compared with the usual 1.47 A C−N bond) shows considerable double bond character in the bond (~50%). For this reason, the nitrogen bond angles are not tetrahedral but approximate trigonal carbon.

insert fig 36.1 on p 1142

Peptides are usually the fragments of proteins. But some have biological power in their own right. Glutathione (g-glutamylcysteinylglycine), oxytocin (a cyclic nonapeptide (Gly-Leu-Pro-Cys-Asn-Gln-Ile-Tyr-Cys; the Cys are − S−S− linked), and a-corticotropin are but a few (sheep protein: SYSMEHFRWGKPVGKKRRPVKVYPAGEDDEASEAFPFEF).

36.9 Determination of structure of peptides. Terminal residue analysis. Partial hydrolysis

Structures of protein are determined by (a) knowing what amino acids there are and in what amount and (b) knowing the sequence they follow each other in a chain.

Hydrolysis of peptides is done in acidic solution since alkaline solutions cause racemization. Gas chromatography can be used to separate and analyze the methyl esters of these amino acids. The relative abundance of each amino acid is assessed in these mixtures. The molecular formula can be determined only from knowing the molecular weight.

Knowing the sequence is in itself quite difficult. Terminal residue analysis and hydrolysis is one way. One method was reported by F. Sanger in 1945, in which 2,4-dinitrofluorobenzene (DNFB) undergoes nucleophilic substitution to give the N-dinitrophenyl (DNP) derivative. The substituted peptide is hydrolyzed to component amino acids, then the N-terminal residue identified as having a 2,4-dinitrophenyl group.

P. Edman in 1950 found that phenyl isothiocyanate (C6H5NCS) reacted with free amino groups in alkaline medium to form a substituted thiourea, which could then be hydrolyzed with aqueous HCl to form a phenylthiohydantoin. The beauty is that the rest of the peptide is left intact.

For C-terminal residue analysis, the most successful method is enzymatic rather than chemical. Pancreatic carboxypeptidase is typically used for this. As fragments are produced, the enzyme can reveal which amino acid is next.

The sequence of glutathione was determined by partial acid hydrolysis. It became clear that Cys was in the middle when g-GluCys and CysGly were isolated as products. Using similar techniques, F. Sanger and coworkers determined the structure of insulin.

36.10 Synthesis of peptides

Conformation of peptide sequence can come from its synthesis as well to show identical physical and chemical properties. Fischer demonstrated that an amide linkage was involved in proteins by preparing peptides.

The first problem involved in the synthesis is one involved with protecting the amino group. In protecting functional groups, one prevents the reaction of an added molecule with itself, so that it can react with a growing polymer. One example is the benzoylation of glycine to protect its amino group, form its acid chloride, allow the acid chloride to react with alanine, and thereby obtain benzoylglycylalanine. But then removing the benzoyl group is not that easy: its hydrolysis would hydrolyze the amide bond being made. Of the numerous methods developed, one involves acylation of benzyl chlorocarbonate, also called carbobenzoxy chloride. (M. Bergamann and L. Zervas introduced this method involving an ester and acid chloride of carbonic acid in 1932.) The carbobenzoxy reagent is made by mixing phosgene (CO + Cl2 with activated charcoal) with benzyl alcohol; the alcohol should be added to phosgene so that the dibenzoxy carbonate is not formed. The acid chloride portion reacts with primary amines to form an amide. Removal of the carbobenzoxy group is under conditions which do not affect peptide linkages: either by catalytic hydrogenation of with HBr in cold acetic acid.

The method is fully illustrated with synthesis of glycylalanine (Gly-Ala):

1. Carbobenzoxy chloride + glycine −−→ carbobenzoxyglycine

2. Carbobenzoxyglycine + SOCl2 −−→ acid chloride of carbobenzoxyglycine

3. acid chloride of carbobenzoxyglycine + alanine −−→ carbobenzoxyglycylalanine

4. carbobenzoxyglycylalanine + H2/Pd −−→ Gly-Ala + C6H5CH3 + CO2

Vincent du Vigneaud synthesized oxytocin using this method.

R. B. Merrifield has developed in automated peptide synthesizer (1969) in which he synthesized the enzyme ribonuclease (124 residues) in 6 weeks. The chemistry involves a solid-phase synthesis allowing attachment of the growing peptide to solid substrate and repeated synthesis.

36.11 Proteins. Classification and function. Denaturation

Proteins are divided into two broad classes: fibrous and globular, the former being insoluble, the latter soluble. The solutions are actually colloidal because of the large size of protein molecules.

Fibrous proteins are elongated in shape and thread-like, lying side by side to form fibers generally held together by numerous hydrogen bonds. Solvents overcoming this intermolecular forces to dissolve the protein must be very strong. They are typically structural proteins, their insolubility and fiber-forming tendency suiting their function. Keratin in skin, hair, nails, wool, horn, and feathers; collagen in tendons; myosin in muscle; fibroin in silk.

Globular proteins are folded compact units which approach spheroidal shapes. Hydrophobic surfaces turn inward, toward each other and away from water. Hydrogen bonding is generally intramolecular, and areas of contact between molecules small. Globular proteins are typically enzymes or hormones.

36.12-.13 Structure of proteins. Peptide chain

Protein structure can be analyzed at many levels. The primary structure is the way in which the atoms of protein molecules are joined to one another by covalent bonds to form chains. The secondary structure is the way the chains are arranged in space to form coils, sheets, compact spheroids, other arrangements. Even higher order structures obtain.

The first level of structure is the peptide chain, wherein residues are linked by amide bonds. The existence of amide chain linkages comes from hydrolysis of proteins by acids, bases, and enzymes to produce peptides and amino acids. Ir spectra demonstrate a characteristic amide group. X-ray data show secondary structures which fit an amide linkage.

36.14 Side chains. Isoelectric point. Electrophoresis

On every 3rd atom of the chain is a side chain R group. In some groups, the side chain is electrically charged, permitting the protein molecule to move in an electric field. That a protein may have numerous charges permits its motion in an electric field. This is electrophoresis.

36.15 Conjugated proteins. Prosthetic groups. Coenzymes

Some proteins contain a nonpeptide portion called a prosthetic group, which is intimately concerned with the specific biological action of the protein. Heme in hemoglobin is a good example, with an iron centered about a pyrrole system known as a porphin. The forces holding heme to the apoprotein include coordination of Fe by His nitrogen in the protein, hydrogen bonding, and van der Waals forces between hydrophobic parts of the two molecules.

Enzymes also require cofactors for catalysis: metal ions are one example. Organic factors are called coenzymes; if covalently attached to the protein, they are also prosthetic groups. NAD is an example of a coenzyme.

36.16 Secondary structure of proteins

X-ray analysis has given much of what is understood about a protein's secondary structure. This has come from observation of a structure with a regular periodicity. Silk fibroin shows repeat distances of 7.0 A. The α-keratin in unstretched wool shows a regular periodicity of 1.5 A and 5.1 A.

Part of this work of solving secondary structure questions involves using the known structures of amino acids and peptides and arranging models which have the lowest free energy conformational states. If the extended peptide chain is considered as one possible conformation, the distance between every other α-carbon will be 7.2 A. These chains may lie side by side with one another to form a flat sheet. However, in arranging this sheet, the R groups, except perhaps for polyglycine, would crowd each other.

Room can be made by contracting the chain to reduce the every-other-residue distance to less than 7.2 A. These chains lie side by side, held together by hydrogen bonds, the contraction resulting in a pleated sheet. This structure is considered a beta arrangement and has been proposed for silk fibroin, which has a repeat distance of 7.0 A. Although 15 different kinds of amino acids are present in silk fibroin, 46% of them are glycine, another 38% alanine and serine. This makes sense since large R groups should make it difficult to form this structure.

Larger side chains are best accommodated by a helical structure. Hydrogen bonding occurs within the chain rather than between chains. Linus Pauling (with R. B. Corey) has proposed that each helix has 3.6 amino acid residues per turn. The pitch of each helix is 1.5 A, accounting for the 1.5 A repeat distances observed in α-keratin. (The 5.1 A repeat distance is a tertiary structure difference, a helical helix, superhelix, or coiled coil, with one turn for every 35 α helical turns.)

Sometimes α-keratin is stretched to a β-keratin, resulting in changes in its x-ray diffraction periodicity data. Uncoiled helixes form sheet structures of a β type, with intrachain H bonds now forming interchain H bonds. The larger side chains present do not permit much extension (repeat distance 6.4 A instead of 7.0 A).

Another structural type is represented by collagen. At the primary level, a high proportion of proline and hydroxyproline are present, usually with frequent repetitions of the sequence Gly-Pro-HOPro. Proline and h ydroproline′s amido nitrogen carries no hydrogen for H-bonding, nor does the ring structure flatness nor amide group flatness permit extension in β sheeting nor helical formation in the α helix. The structure combines beta-type bonding in sheets with alpha-type helical coiling: each chain twists to a left-handed helix, and then binds intermolecularly (a Gly at every third position permits placement) to two others to form a right-handed superhelix.

Chap. 37 Biochemical processes. Molecular biology

37.1 Biochemistry, molecular biology, and organic chemistry

37.2 Mechanism of enzyme action. Chymotrypsin

The sequence of chymotrypsin 245 amino acid residues is known, and x-ray diffraction data has revealed molecular conformation.

The catalytic activity of the enzyme is similar to all chemical reactions studied to this point. Chymotrypsin acts in two stages: in stage 1, an alcohol function within the enzyme attacks the carbonyl group of the substrate amide in a nucleophilic acyl substitution to form an ester and an amine, resulting in fragmentation of the protein. In stage 2, the ester becomes hydrolyzed to generate the carboxylic acid in the substrate, freeing the other fragment, and regenerating the enzyme for the next catalytic cycle.

The alcohol function of the enzyme has been determined in studies saturating the enzyme with substrate to build up a steady-state concentration of intermediate ester, then quenching the reaction with acid to isolate the intermediate. This intermediate has been found to be serine-195.

Other amino acids are vital, too. The rate of hydrolysis appears to depend upon the pH of the solution. The fastest rate is at an optimal pH of 7.4, and changing the pH to either side affects the activity. Hydrolysis requires the presence of a free base with a pKa of 7.0 and a protonated base with pKa of 9.5. The more acidic residue would be the pKa of a histidine imidazole, which fits with studies showing imidazole being a catalyst itself. In the x-ray structure, a histidine-57 is situated very closely to serine-195.

The stronger base with pKa of 9.5 fits that for the α-amino group of most amino acids. Acetylation of amino groups necessary for catalysis eliminate or significantly reduce activity. Except for acetylation of one residue, acetylation does not result in complete loss of activity. The exception is isoleucine-16. When the pH is increased which reduces the protonation on Ile-16, the optical rotation parameters of chymotrypsin changes, suggesting conformational changes. Hence, Ile-16 is believed to hold the protein into proper shape which supports catalysis.

The role of His-57 appears to be in a base-assisted hydrolysis or esterolysis. While the Ser-195 alcohol group is nucleophilic in itself, it becomes more nucleophilic when the proton is pulled from it to make a partial alkoxy group.

37.3 The source of biological energy. The role of ATP

A philosophical discussion of how energy is ultimately obtained from the sun, produced and consumed in a cycle of CO2 and H2O to energy-rich substances and O2. In all living forms, the energy is harnessed with simple reductions in the level of energy in one molecule to the other. The ultimate currency of that energy is ATP.

The energy in ATP is contained in its readiness to phosphorylate. For example, ATP reacts with alcohols (on proteins or nonprotein organic substrates) to form ADP and a phosphate ester. It is highly reactive for the reason that it is an anhydride and places phosphates more easily than does acetic acid compared to acetic anhydride.

37.4 Biological oxidation of carbohydrates

Starting with glycogen and proceeding to CO2 and H2O in the oxidation process is a long multi-step process. The trip is broken down to three stages: (a) breaking glycogen into its component glucose molecules; (b) breaking the glucose into three-carbon molecules, called glycolysis; (c) converting these three-carbon compounds to CO2 and H2O in a process called respiration. Oxygen consumption is required in the third stage.

The first stage is cleavage of glycogen [indicated as hydrolysis in the book, but actually phosphorolysis]. Glycolysis in the second stage requires 11 reactions and 11 enzymes, forming 2 lactate, 2 water molecules, and 2 ATP molecules from a single glucose. The third stage is a complex, in which glycolysis is diverted at the pyruvate stage, oxidized to acetic acid in a highly energized form, acetyl-coenzyme A, which is fed into the Krebs cycle.

37.5 Mechanism of biological oxidation

37.6 Biosynthesis of fatty acids

37.7 Nucleoproteins and nucleic acids

37.8 Chemistry and heredity. The genetic code
